O, sons, since I have completed for you this book, which is the culmination of the science of medicine, through its completion, and through its exposition, and through its declarations, it seems to me that I should satisfy you with this treatise, which is a part of the hand's operation, that is, surgery.
Because the operation by hand is neglected in our region and in our time, entirely deprived, so that the science of it is quickly lost, and its vestiges are obliterated; and nothing remains of it but some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which are mostly altered and lead to errors and hesitation, so that their intentions are obscured and their force and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I should revive it with the ordering of this treatise, in that way, according to the method of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and that it should come with the forms of the cautery instruments and of the other instruments of the work, as it should be by addition of the declaration, and by preparation of it, which is a necessary thing.
But the reason why a good artisan with his hand is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is lengthy, and that its practitioner must first be exercised in the science of anatomy, which Galen has described, so that he knows the locations of the members and their forms, and their conjunction and separation; and he should have knowledge of the bones, the nerves and the ligaments, and their number, and their origin; and of the pulsating veins and the quiet ones, and the places of their emergence.
And for that reason Hippocrates says that with one name much is said, but in the work little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said that in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not learned in what we have said of anatomy, is not free from falling into error, by which men will be killed, just as I have seen many such cases, because some are trained in this science and then practice it without knowledge and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he incised some arteries of the neck, so that a great flow of blood occurred until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who had it since his youth, and the stone was large, and he went to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and it happened to a black eunuch who had a fracture in his thigh; and the physician, through his ignorance, extracted the fracture, on the wound, with pads and splints with strong constriction, and did not allow the wound to breathe (to heal properly).
And then he unbound it according to his wishes, and then he bound it again, and then he left it for some days and ordered that we unbind the bandage; and it remained so until the thigh and foot were festering (abscessed), and it came to ruin (loss of the limb/death).
And I was called to him, and I prepared to unbind the bandage, and quietness was achieved, and thus a lessening of his pains.
However, the corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated for some days, until the malignancy of it was magnified by its lord (master, owner - perhaps referring to the spread of the infection or the worsening of the condition).
The reason is that a cancerous sore is pure melancholic humor; therefore, it is not fitting that it be treated with iron entirely, but rather the limb in which it suffers should be entirely rested (kept immobile).
And for that reason, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be divided into two divisions: that is, into an operation which is associated with health, and into a work in which there is peril in the greatest situations (or "in the most serious cases").
And I have already expounded this in every part of this book where an operation occurs in which there is error and fear; therefore it is necessary for you to guard against this and avoid it, lest fools find a way to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with God's protection, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method, by the grace of God, leading to health and laudable success, and leave the terrible diseases of difficult healing, and abstain from those things that make you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name may not overcome you: for this is more conducive to your glory, and higher in the world and for the sake of your holiness.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you be called a bad physician".
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first, of the first part. The first chapter deals with the head down to the feet,  the forms of instruments and the tools for cautery, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of incision and perforation, and of cupping, and of wounds, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of reduction and dislocation, and of the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I say the quality of the benefits of that cautery and its drawbacks, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and its drawbacks is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and a little of their discourses suffices for me, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, which are with matter or without matter, except for two complexions, which are hot complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not suitable in a disease which is by quality and by dryness, because the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry disease should be healed with that which is hot and dry.
And I refute him who says the contrary, that benefit is done by cautery in every hot and dry disease that occurs in the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of man and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say, concerning his discourse, that I have discovered this by experience several times.
However, it is not fitting that this should come upon one who is already experienced and practiced in the chapter on cautery through previous exercise, and who has known the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of diseases in themselves, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the span of time of those.
But in the other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and moist diseases, for all physicians have agreed on those, and are not in disagreement about the benefit with cautery in those.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire and its goodness, above cautery with a burning medicine, is because fire is simple, its action does not reach except the member which is cauterized and does not harm the other member adjacent to it, except with slight harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches to those members which are distant from it, and quickly causes in the member a disease of difficult healing and quickly kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except that it superfluously flows.  And it is known to us by experiment and by long service of study in the art concerning the truth of the causes; and for this I am excused from the length of discourse. And if it were not that it is not permitted in my book to present to you this secret hidden with fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies and of its expulsion by which it expels diseases, with a philosophical demonstrative discourse, which would be heavy upon your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree on the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, and they place the best time in springtime.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is achieved with cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or necessary pains, strong and urgent, which do not tolerate delay because it is feared that from those (delays) greater harm will come than the small harm entering through time.
And this does not fall into your minds, or, sons, this is only esteemed by the vulgar people and foolish physicians, that is, that for that illness which is healed by cautery there is no remission at all times, and they posit this consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they think for that reason, because cautery is only in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause by which pains come.
However, cautery surpasses medicine because of the speed of its effectiveness and the virtue of its operation and the greatness of its effect.
And it is possible that his illness returns at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his illness, and his strength, and that which is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him, and that which has come to him in his acquisition in foods and similar causes; unless the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a member with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar to that, it is possible that in him that pain does not return, but this happens in small things.
And a more common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and it is a righteous saying, not in what they understand by it, for they believe that it is not a cure that benefits with medicine or with any other thing besides cautery.
And the reason is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer the methods of cure in certain illnesses, and those medicines do not help, and then at the end of the process, one administers cautery, and it benefits for that. And therefore, from here it is seen that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and many foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the evenness of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not scar, although that is not absolutely true, because I have already proven this and found that it does not do so except in some bodies and not others, and cautery with it is good and better than with iron, as they said; however, when you do cautery with fire with gold, it is not clear to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity you want by the roughness of the gold, and because it is prepared to receive cold; and if you apply fire to it, the gold melts from the fire, and some small plates fall into the fire, so that the craftsman is distracted by that.
And for that reason, cautery with iron is done more easily and more readily with accuracy of operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those from the head to the feet, so that I may easily find what the questioner wants from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization is applied to dampness and coldness affecting the brain, the accidents of which are two causes: that is, the cause of sudden and abundant rheumatic flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and excessive sleep, and toothache and throat pain, and universally every illness that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, sudden unconsciousness, apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is this: first, order the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine, purifying the head, for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, their age and their habits.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit between your hands on a low bench and place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm near the brow above the root of the nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that spot, mark that place with a probe, and then ignite the olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place that [cautery] on the spot marked with the probe, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand is gradually constricted, and you rotate it.
And then lift your hand slightly and observe the place, and if you see some quantity of the head of the bone uncovered, like the quantity of a grain of barley, then raise your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron, or with another one if it is cooled, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot and leave it for three days.
And then place on it cotton soaked in wine and leave it there until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the area with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the wound remains open, the arm heals better, because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the elbow should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's substance is charred.
And then scrape away what is burnt from the bone, and then it should be treated.
And others say that with the cautery, you should reach the back, until it penetrates strongly into the bone, until the bone is consumed.  And this is the way of a small alphecat (likely referring to a type of bone disease or injury): And they believe that vapors from the head exhale from that place.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then it should be healed until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men, as a way of fear; and leave that, it seems to me, it is better with health, when it is; because the head is weakened by a solution of its continuity, as we have testified in the other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a fragile nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and use that, and with that work in health.
Chapter Two.
Concerning the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs throughout the head in a patient, and he uses leeches, cupping, purgatives, plasters, potions, poultices, and similar remedies, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we recount and none of these things benefits him, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution by nature; and if it is not fragile and he finds great cold, then cauterize him again with another cautery a little below that one. And then cauterize that [area] above the horn of his head with a cautery until the scab is formed. Incise the scab and uncover the bone to the extent we have recounted, and cauterize that with a cautery in the rear part of the head in the place which is called the vas—and this is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And ease your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient thereby finds much greater pain, different from the pain of all other cauterizations of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place, because it is necessary that the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter three.
Concerning the cauterization of non-ancient migraine.
When a sudden pain occurs in the head, or a prolonged pain extends to the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the descriptions of illnesses; and if nothing helps, then cauterization is in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and you peel it and cut off the ends from both sides. Then incise the location of the pain in the temple with a lancet until a place is made beneath the skin, until it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a cloth for a strong constriction, and leave it for three hours; and then untie it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then place on it cotton soaked in vinegar until the area becomes inflamed.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner: ignite the cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called *claus clavilhs* (nail-shaped cautery), because its head is like the shape of a nail, in which there is a certain curvature, and in the middle of it is a small eminence.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand—so that the flesh underneath is not burned—and move the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount of flesh that is burned by the heat of the cautery is about half, then lift your hand so that the artery beneath is not burned, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the area, and leave it for three days; and then apply cotton moistened with honey, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of a knife, according to the cautery method, and be careful to avoid cutting the artery in this particular, not-ancient, migraine.
Chapter four.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic migraine.
When you treat migraine with what we have said in the previous treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment does not help, and sometimes due to the severity of the illness, what we said about the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is insufficient, then you should heat the knife cautery until it is white hot, since you mark the location of the pain with a line whose length is half a finger, or something like that; and place your hand for a moment and press it until you incise the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful about the continuation of the jaw, which is moved during mastication, and that the muscle is not injured and the nerve that serves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the care and caution regarding the flow of blood from the incised artery, for in its incision there is danger, and especially for one who is ignorant of what he does and is not studious and experienced; and abandons the operation at the beginning.
And I will come with a recollection of the treatment of accidental bleeding from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this disease is not relieved by this cautery and the patient's body can tolerate it, cauterize it in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of an artery with a cautery having two blades, and this cauterization is better and more suitable.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5: On the cauterization of earaches.
When there is pain in the ear due to cold and it is treated with laxative remedies and with the other treatment which I have described in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is the one which is called "puncta", of which this is the form:
And then, quickly apply the cauterization, and let it be led around the whole ear as a wheel is turned, or in the circumference of each part if the pain is in a specific part. Lengthen the cautery from the root of the ear little by little after you have marked the place with a probe.
And if the cautery makes ten points in the whole ear, and whichever one it is, then treat the places until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the cauterization of a cough.
A cough which is cured with cauterization is only of those species which come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of illnesses.
Avoid cauterization of the species that arises from dryness or from spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of cough with leeches and purgatives and gargles, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauterizations: the first at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected part, because softening does not occur except in the part that appears healthy.
And the method of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the ear, a little above the corner of the head, and another in the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the index finger, and hold your cautery there until half the thickness of a quill is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of knife, the form of which is shown above. (This assumes a diagram or illustration was present in the original text.) However, it is more subtle to do it a little like this; and it is necessary to cut away some superfluous thickness.
And then treat the area with what was previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
On the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you have treated it with what we have said, and your medication is not effective and the patient has no fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries: on all the corners of his head; and cauterize the middle of the head, as we have said; and cauterize the nape of the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And now it is cauterized above the stomach; and it is better behind; and then let it be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
On cauterization for forgetfulness done by flame.
So that at the beginning, large leeches and purifying pills are given to the patient.
And then shave the patient's entire head and apply to his neck a plaster of cetre described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cauterization. And do this according to the same order which I have already said.
And therefore if he is healed by those means, it is good; and if not, then make for him three cauteries in the back part of his head, which descend from the uppermost part of the head down to the lowermost part of the neck. And place between each cautery the width of a finger, and then treat the cautery with what has been described.
But if you add more and the patient tolerates it, then make a cautery in the middle place; and if you wish greater addition (a stronger cautery), then cauterize that above the two horns (presumably referring to anatomical locations on the head).
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped, according to the form given before.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
That you proceed in the purification of the head with jars and with that which we have said; and then shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery on all the horns of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of him, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to find it and the patient tolerates it and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery until it is burned from the coccyx upwards, and raise your hand. And then treat it until it is healed, and this should be an olive-oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
And therefore it is necessary to purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatment which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and tolerates the expulsion of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, before that for many days, with improvement of the diet.
Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery in the nape of his neck, and cauterize above each temple. And if the patient is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, as has been previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and a thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cauteries which we have mentioned in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is humid, then give him something to drink that cleanses his brain, according to what has preceded in the division (of treatments).
Then shave the patient's head, and then make a properly-made poultice of it over the circle (presumably a previously drawn circle on the head), and then place that in the middle of the head.
And let the patient be seated and recumbent (or, sitting leaning back), and be held on all sides.
Then apply a liniment; and it is most worthy to use old sheep's grease (or suet). And heat that (liniment) over the fire with a moderate, measured heating, which the said sufferer will feel when it is applied to him. Then pour that (heated liniment) into the middle of his head within the circle, and leave it there until it is cooled.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with his other good treatment until he is healed; or, if you wish, cauterize it making small punctures according to his need, but hold your hand with a cautery thus prepared: this is a kind of cautery which moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply that cotton soaked in grease or goose fat.
Chapter 12.
On the cauterization of water descending into the eye.
When the beginning of watery discharge in the eye is declared to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something to purify his head, and keep it from all humidity.
And make him sweat in a bath for a few days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the beginning of the watery discharge is in two eyes, or on one side if the beginning of the watery discharge is in one eye.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the temples; and let the cauteries be far apart in the width of the temples.
And beware of a flow of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see a flow of blood from it, stop it with any medicine which is possible for you.
And I come with wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of those and the caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries placed apart.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears (likely referring to long-standing sores or wounds).
When the tears from the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest area of the head, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold, phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we recounted in the beginning chapter about water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it requires additional treatment, then cauterize it at the outer corner of the eye, above the end of the eyebrow, with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (or, more accurately, a foul smell from the nose).
When you treat this with what we have said in the division and your treatment is not effective, then you prepare and give the patient the decoctions for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a medium-sized olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize it with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauterizations above the two eyebrows, slightly below the hairline.
And beware of burning the artery, that you do not burn it.
And of the clove-shaped cautery, this is the form/shape.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the cauterization for the softening/relaxation of the eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize, do so above the two eyebrows a little, with two cauteries in each area, and let them be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand and the cautery, but only to the extent that a third of the eyelid is hardened/cauterized.
Chapter XVI.
Concerning the cauterization of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inward and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward so that they prick the eye, cauterization in those cases is according to two methods, for cauterization is either with fire or with a burning medicine.
If with cauterization by fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave his hairs as those who cleanse them, until they are elongated and even; and if they prick below their roots, then you bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so that they are not hurt until the hairs have grown.
Then, when they have grown and are even, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid of his eyes with a probe, with a mark which is according to the figure of a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then place a cotton pledget soaked in egg white or in gum arabic over the eye.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the figure which you have marked, little by little, in several times, until the surface of the skin, which is like a myrtle leaf, is completely burnt.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hairs thereof raised from the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with ointment over it until the scar is healed. And then treat that with ointment until it is healed.
But if something returns through the hairs near their origin and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again over that, just as you did previously.
But if the hairs are beneath the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is even, and the hairs do not prick the eye.
But cauterize with a burning medicine; this is to order the patient to pull out the hairs until they are elongated and even, and then a leaf-shaped plaster of cloth should be made like a myrtle leaf.
And then take known soap and quicklime, one ounce, or however much, and then mix those ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; and then spread this on the cloth which is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place this on one or both eyes; and place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it a little and do not move it until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like fire.
And so when you perceive that he feels the bite, then leave the medicine and move it with your finger; then when the bite ceases, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then consider if you see that the eyelid is now raised as it is washed, avoid constriction with fire or incision, for then it is well; and if not, return to that medicine to the place where the medicine has not yet been applied and do not darken it until your operation is even and the eye is contracted.
And then place cotton with a bandage on it until the scab from the cauterization is eradicated, and then treat it with healing ointment or with another until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to watch with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid in a few days is softened and some of the hairs stick to the eye, then repeat your medicine on that place of the eyelid which is softened, and precisely as you did at the beginning, and then cover it until it is healed.
And this is the form/method for myrtle leaf. (The sentence refers to a method using myrtle leaves, likely as a medicine.)
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size and smallness, and therefore according to that is your contraction made.  (Meaning:  The treatment must be adapted to the size of the patient's eye.)
And the path of rectitude shall not be hidden from him who studies this art. (Meaning: The correct method will be clear to anyone who diligently studies this medical practice.)
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula that is in the lacrimal duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment does not succeed, then you must cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, and let a minister hold his head between your hands in such a way that his head is not moved or agitated.
Then place a cotton swab dipped in egg white or gum arabic on his eye.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the shape:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cautery is performed.
And if you wish, let it be penetrating to the other end. And if you wish, let it be pointed like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the rottenness from it.
Then place the cautery, which should be well ignited, over it, and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not burn or the patient is not disturbed, and then place the cautery on the hailstone of the eye and destroy it.
And so if at the beginning your cautery penetrates into the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place over it a compress with a bandage and around it with drying ointment until it is healed.
But if eleven days have passed and it is not healed, then place a sharp corrosive medicine on it until the bone is exposed and is scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which some ancients said:  understand the location of the fistula and open it, and then place in that fissure a thin tube, which is shaped like this: And pour into it the quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand with the tube with good retention, and let the patient not be moved so that the molten lead does not reach his eye; and it is necessary that you place on the patient's eye a compress soaked in egg white or in water, because the lead will burn the site of the fistula and heal it by a marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cautery and of the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a puncture of the nose and to reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it will come to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the cauterization of the fissure of the wells (likely referring to a type of wound or ulcer).
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat those fissures with what we have said in the divisions and your treatment does not help, then ignite a small, knife-like cautery according to this form that is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then carefully place that ignited cautery into those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
Then treat that with cerots until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops and then breaks, and then becomes chronic, and pus flows and a fistula is formed, and you treat it and the treatment is not effective, then you should ignite a cautery according to the size of the fistula.  Then place that ignited cautery into the opening of the fistula, holding it with your hand until the heated iron reaches the bottom of it; do this once or twice on the back of it; and then treat that with what we have said concerning treatment until it is healed.
And so, if the wound is closed and heals, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to open the area and remove the corrupted bone, as will be explained in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the cauterization of teeth and the softening of the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines and it doesn't help, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat a cautery, the shape of which will be described later, in such a way that you place the small spoon over the tooth and quickly introduce the heated cautery into it, and hold it briefly until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization several times as needed.
And then let the patient fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth is fixed, the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt moisture is dried up.
Chapter 21.
On the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or a worm in it, and you cannot cure it with medicine, then cauterize it in one of two ways: either cauterize with a hot iron, or cauterize with fire.
Cauterization with a hot iron is done by taking a hot iron and heating it in a coal fire. Then take cotton and wrap it around the tip of the iron, and then dip it in boiling water, and quickly place it on the aching tooth until it cools.
And then repeat this several times until the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool or cotton in the aforementioned boiling water, and place that on the aching tooth, and place the heated iron on top of it until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is done by using a small tube of bronze or iron, and there should be some thickness in the tube and the intensity of the fire should not reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery to the desired shape, and place it on the tooth and hold your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times because the pain will be relieved either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, due to the impression of that cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good boiling water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the shape of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
Concerning the cauterization of extrophulos (likely referring to external swellings or eruptions).
When extrophulos are caused by phlegm and cold humors and are not responsive to digestion by medicines, and you wish to digest them lightly, then you ignite the cautery whose shape is as follows:  having two open ends so that the smoke escapes through one end of the cautery, and place this ignited cautery on that abscess once, and again if necessary, until it reaches the bottom of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and apply a cloth soaked in butter over it until what the fire has burned is softened.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the cauterization of hoarseness and the constriction of the throat.
When humidities gather in the lung's passage, and especially when this is accompanied by coldness of constitution, then the patient should be evacuated initially with laxative medicines, and afterwards cauterize that area below the root of the throat in the constricted place.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn more than a little of its cartilage.
And cauterize that area with another cautery below the juncture of the neck, at the last vertebra. And his cauterization should be done with a nail, following the previous method.
And afterwards treat that with what was prescribed before until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption [tuberculosis] and the disease is chronic, then cauterize him with two cauteries on the clavicle, in the places previously mentioned on the left and right; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a nail according to the previous method.
And if you wish, let your cauterization be punctate with the cautery that is called a punctate cautery, the form of which precedes in the punctation of earache.
And let the points be close to those; and then treat it with the same things that have been mentioned before until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs because cauterization is done with this: with one application you can make three cauteries according to this method:
Chapter 25.
Concerning the cauterization of the testicle, which is the head of the helper (presumably referring to the penis).
When the head of the helper (penis) is dislocated due to lubricating humors and is not fixed in the moment of its return from its dislocation until it is in its accustomed place to return, and then through a small movement it is dislocated again as it should be, as we have testified, then it is necessary that first you reduce the dislocation, and then the patient should be turned on his back or on his healthy side; and then raise the scrotum which is on the inner part of the testicle to the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated to the inner parts.
And then heat the cautery having two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the scrotum until it penetrates from one side to the other; and the figure of the cauterization will then be of four cauterized points.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count, and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place on the cautery cooked porridges with salt, and then keep the patient at rest, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a nail-shaped cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened and the humidity is expelled and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors enter the stomach, exceeding its capacity and multiplying within it, and if you continue the treatments and do not resolve it, then the patient should lie supine, extend their hands and legs, and then cauterize it with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet, about the width of a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery, and with two other cauteries below on either side of that cautery, so that a triangular figure of cauteries is formed; and space them out so they do not touch to avoid festering.
And the depth of the cautery should be two-thirds the thickness of a finger.
And let the shape of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a circular cautery according to this form:  (This line indicates that diagrams or illustrations were present in the original text, showing different cautery shapes.)
And let the stomach be cauterized, paying attention to the area to be cauterized:  that is, mark points on the stomach with a probe to the desired extent, and then cauterize those points with the cautery; and then treat it with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
On the cauterization of a cold liver.
When pain arises in the liver due to coldness, humidity, or excessive flatulence, so that it is thrown from its normal constitution by superfluous egress, and the patient is not helped by what we have said, then the patient should be laid supine on his back, and three cauteries should be marked with a probe according to this form and this same size upon the liver, beneath the hypochondria, where the patient's lying down allows. (Again, this line refers to a diagram showing the placement of the cauteries).
And let the space between each cautery be according to the width of a finger, and let each cautery be according to the length of a straight finger.
And do not press too much with the cautery.
And if the amount of what is burned is half the size of a hazelnut, no more should be applied; and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him be lying down, having already spread his thighs, and raising his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these areas with a knife-shaped cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And beware that you do not proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the size of a whole hazelnut be cauterized so that the whole body is cauterized and it reaches the intestines, because the hazelnut here is small; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the perforation of a liver abscess with a cautery.
When an ulcer or abscess develops in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the liver flesh or in its tunic, then if it is in the liver flesh, the patient will experience heaviness and dull pain.
And if it is in the liver tunic, the pain is very sharp and sometimes it tires the doctors to cure it.
Therefore, the patient should be positioned on their side; then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then apply the cautery, which resembles a rake, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire boil is consumed, until it reaches the pus, until blood escapes.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this type of cauterization be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is long and in whose hands many diseases have been treated;  but let someone with some experience sometimes dare this operation, however, let him leave it to us, it is better.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that cauterization with the roots of aristolochia for cold pleurisy, according to this method, is effective, and it is this: that you take a long, dry root of aristolochia, as long as can be found, and of the thickness of a finger.
Then immerse it in oil and heat it in the fire, and then cauterize with a cautery in the place which is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the chin, slightly inclining towards the part below the beard, and with two cauteries in the area inclining between the fifth and sixth rib, inclining slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another on the stomach; and with three cauteries on the back; and with one cautery in the place which is between the two shoulders, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary that you grasp the cautery with your hand, but rather let it be in the obvious and evident manner of the work. (or: ...but rather let it be handled in the clear and straightforward manner of the work).
And some ancients said that some men use a cautery of iron similar to a ray and ignite it, and place it in the area between the ribs, until it reaches the abscess and it is purged of the disease, as we have said in the case of an abscess of the liver.
But in this perforation with a cautery there is danger, because one can quickly cause death in the place, a fistula which is incurable.
Chapter 30.
Concerning/On the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen with what we have said of the treatment in the division and your treatment is ineffective, then cauterization is appropriate in three ways, all of which are correct.  One is to make three or four cauteries lengthwise, according to the length of the spleen, following the pattern of the liver cauteries, the form of which has been previously described.
And let the distance between each cautery be the width of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as we described for the liver cauterization.
And do not press your hand against the cautery; and the patient should be lying on their side.
And another method of cauterization is to ignite the cautery with two points,  which procedure we described in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and elevate the rib which is in the area of the spleen where the patient's left side lies.
And your elevation, by which you raise the rib, should be along the length of the body, so that cauterizations are made along the width of the body.
Then insert the two ignited points deeply into that space so that with them you penetrate the rib on the other side.
And then withdraw the cautery; and thus four cauterizations have been made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three points.
Then you should care for the cauterization until it has stopped discharging pus for many days, for this is more important than the other preceding causes in the healing.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the cauterization of a dropsical person.
Cauterization is not suitable for all cases of dropsy, but only in a specific type.
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment that we have mentioned in the division, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize them with four cauteries in a circle around the navel and with one cautery over the stomach, and with another cautery over the liver, and with another cautery over the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and with another cautery over the stomach. And the depth of the cauterization should be approximately equal to the thickness of a finger.
And then leave the cauterized wounds open so that they drain freely for a long time.
And do not remove the patient from treatment near the cautery with what is necessary for two intentions to be added to it, and it will be hastened to him with healing aid.  (This sentence is difficult to translate precisely because the phrasing is quite archaic and possibly uses medical terminology that is no longer current.  The gist is that the treatment should continue despite the cauterization, aiming for a speedy recovery.)
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its form is described above; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its form is described previously.
Chapter 32.
On the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are inflamed with dropsy and filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the dorsal surface of the foot, in the space between the little toe and the ring finger. Raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; then raise your hand again, and do not completely withdraw it, because the yellowish water will flow out.
The cautery is according to this shape: Then make two cauteries on both thighs, and on each thigh a cautery should be made with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh; one below the knee and the other in the middle of the thigh; and two cauteries on each thigh.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that some water drains from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is cold and damp to the point that it weakens the vital functions – that is, the digestive and retentive functions which reside in the stomach and intestines – and this is treated with methods of healing that do not help, and sometimes the patient suffers, exhibiting a need for cautery, then cauterize it with a large cautery on the stomach as previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and with four small cauteries around the navel, using a small, nail-like cautery; and with cautery on the hypochondrium, either one large cautery or two.
And if sometimes the humors are excessive and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize it on the pubic bone and on the intestines, and quickly apply two cauteries near the stomach and near the groins, for this is a perfect cure, a help from which you will not err.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the cauterization of old hemorrhoids.
When there are old hemorrhoids by nature, or those of thick, cold humors or corrupt humors, and they are treated with what we have said in the division and your treatment is not effective, then cauterize with three small, triangular cauteries on the lower part of the sacrum, and with a cautery above the navel, placed as described twice.
And if you suspect that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and his face appears inflamed, then cauterize his stomach with a large cautery as previously described; and with one cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on his spleen using a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter 35.
Concerning the cauterization of swellings near their excision.
When you excise a swelling, then immediately apply a cautery that resembles a ray (or, a radiating cautery). Then insert that ignited cautery into the excised swelling, and keep your hand there until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood flows.
And do this once or twice; for if the swellings are numerous, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and afterwards treat them with ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on something, it is slower in helping.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. On the cauterization of fistulas that are formed in the lower parts of the body.
When nature does not obey the treatment which we have recommended in its place, and the patient is frightened by it, let it be healed with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in the lower parts of the body, and it is old, running with pus and corrupt humors, then what is most appropriate is to probe it with a thin probe, and then ascertain the extent of its depth with the aforementioned probe.
Then ignite the cautery which is similar to a ray (or, perhaps, a 'rod'). And then insert that ignited cautery into this fistula, according to the extent to which the probe penetrated.
And repeatedly apply the cautery to it until those corrupt humors are burned away once or twice or three times, according to your assessment of necessity.
And beware of burning the nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes towards the body of the bladder or towards the body of the intestine, then beware of all those places; and do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy place and you think it is not penetrating (deeper vital structures).
And then treat the area until it is healed.
And if the wound is sealed, and the matter has been excised from it, and remains so for a long time, then know that it is entirely cured.
And if the matter has not been removed from it, then know that it is penetrating, or that the bone itself is corrupt, or that it comes from those causes which will be recalled in their place (in the later parts of the treatise).
Chapter 37.
On the cauterization of sores.
When in sores pain arises from cold or excessive windiness, and because of this the patient's body is weakened, then it is necessary that you cauterize that [area] over the sores with a cautery, over each sore with a nail-shaped cautery, of which the preparation was previously mentioned.
And quickly cauterize again a little lower, so that three linear cauterizations will result, and then they will be more efficacious in providing help.
Chapter 38.
On the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, such that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that [area] in the lowest part above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubic region begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side, and with another on the left.
And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the patient's finger; and cauterize that [area] with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be a nail-shaped cautery, as is customary/appropriate.
Chapter 39.
Concerning the cauterization of the breast.
When cold and humidity come to the breast, and because of this pregnancy is prevented, and her menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and pain occurs during their arrival, then it is necessary that the area be cauterized with three cauteries in a circle around the navel, as we have said in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery above the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
Concerning the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and a sign of this is that the thigh is longer than the other when they are compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it protrudes.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient on the part of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the affected area as it is swollen, so that the affected area falls within the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the kind described previously for the cauterization of the stomach.
And if this cautery is not at hand, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be done according to the extent of the thickness of the entire limb; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, the patient is treated with what we have said and not with bloodletting, and this is antiquated, then you should purge the patient of thick humors with foul-smelling pills or with artificial or similar pills; and then cauterize it.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
Indeed, according to two known methods, one of which is that you cauterize the area of the patient's hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, whether the cautery be olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be in the middle of the place, on the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of the hip dislocation, so that it is a continuous circle and burns the thickness of the whole buttock.
And this is the method of cauterization in sciatica when the pain is adherent to the hip and is not extended to the thigh.
But if it is extended to the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the place to which the patient points as being the location of pain; and with one cautery on the groin, four fingers somewhat below the left side.
And if the cautery is knife-like, let the depth of the cautery be according to the thickness of the buttock.
And if the patient indicates that the pain extends to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery in three or four points, or more if needed.
And if the patient indicates the pain to be below the buttock towards the thigh, then cauterize there with a knife-like cautery.
And be careful in all cauterization that you do not use the cautery so much as to burn nerves or cause great injury, and that because of this there is no harm or contraction in the patient.
And I have seen one and another of those whom I cauterized on the ankle and went too deep in the cauterization, and the leg was swollen until the tumor reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then a very strong bowel movement happened, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places at once, cauterize each according to this manner.
And the wise men say that in the cauterization of the ankle, a cautery like this is the method: make a circle of iron whose circumference is half a palm; and let its opening be according to the thickness of a date pit, or a little less.
And within that circle let there be another, a third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a hazelnut; and let those circles be open on two sides; and let the length of those irons be a hazelnut or two hazelnuts, and make in those irons a properly prepared margin in the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the ankle, the patient lying on their side. Therefore cauterize that with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave that for three days; then plaster it with a bandage and leave the wound open for many days; and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
Says Albucasis: O, son, we have used this type of cautery little, because of the fear of looking at it, and because we have found few who dare look upon it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and finds with it the place of it (the ailment).
But cauterize with adhering medicines, so that you make two circles having a perforated handle in which the hook of the iron door should fit; let the length of the walls in the height of them be two nuts or whatever those are.  (The last phrase is difficult to interpret precisely due to the archaic language and potentially imprecise measurement reference.)
And if the space which is between each is the quantity of the thickness of a finger; and let them be opened below and above, and let them contain themselves between them according to this form:
Then place the patient lying on their side over the affected part of the hip, and press your hand on the hip area; and then take warm water and pour it between the two circles, and the quality of that should be with the fire little by little, and hold that for the quantity of an hour, and let the patient suffer the biting and burning of it—for this causes a burning like fire—until the burning ceases.
Then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the warm water from the entire hip with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and put a bandage on it until the blackness of the burn is removed, and leave it for some days, and pus will drain from it; this is the final healing.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the hip or leg, make for him a bath that contains water according to how it is done for the hip similarly.
And this is the manner of hot water, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, an equal part of each, and crush them and place them in a new pot, the bottom part of which is perforated with a small hole, according to the quantity that will enter the ray (presumably a type of instrument or container).
And place under the bottom of the pot another inverted pot, and pour over that alkali and quicklime fresh water which covers them by a finger's breadth; and after you compress them with your hand by good compression, and leave the pot to drip the sharp water into the inverted pot below.
And then gather all that water, and pour it over more fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be of a very strong sharpness, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterizing other limbs, because it acts like fire.
And some medicines with which it is also cauterized are,  for example,  verdigris and honey mixed with quicklime and soap in such a way that they are mixed together.
And Galien recounted from an ancient man the operation and cure with which pain in the hip and sciatic vein is cured, and greatly praised the effectiveness of it, and said that it had no need of another cure, and that it was healed at once as he recounted, for he quickly entered a bath and was already free from it.
And it is that there should be taken some green hellebore, and if green is not found, a fresh fig should be taken; and it should be well crushed with a little grease and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and bound on.
And it should be left on for three hours, or for as long as the patient feels a lessening of the burning.
Then put him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, expose him to the sun, for the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that no one should use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
But Dioscorides said that when the varicose vein is cauterized with goat's dung, it will benefit him greatly; and let the cautery be applied in this manner: let wool be taken and soaked in old oil, and placed between the toes and between the ankle bones.
Then take dried goat dung and burn it until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it until it is extinguished. Then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of burning reaches the ankle and relieves the pain.
And this method of cautery is called cautery of  goats (or perhaps, more literally, "cow cautery," though the previous lines clearly specify goat dung).
Chapter 42.
On the cauterization of back pain.
Sometimes pain is in the back due to various causes, such as a fall or a blow, or excessive evacuation, and similar causes, due to the infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cautery is only suitable for that single type which is caused by the effusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after evacuation of the patient with purging pills and similar remedies,  make incisions on the back where the pain is, three lines according to its width, then mark with a cautery iron five cauterizations or more on each line, according to the patient's tolerance and their strength.
And let the cauterization be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries with a nail-shaped cautery according to this form:
Chapter 43.
On cauterization at the beginning of gibbosity (or hunchback).
Often this illness befalls small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it occurs on their spine with elevation and movement.
And you find in the last vertebra of the back and neck that it begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And so when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize that with a cautery that is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in all parts of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the circumference of the vertebra with the pointed cautery two or three lines. And let the points be close to each other.
And then treat the area until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered in swelling that is caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
Concerning the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is customary for physicians to call this gout properly.
And so, if gout caused by cold is cured with a method of cure and the pains are not relieved, then cautery relieves them.
And it is that you cauterize that area for evacuation in the circumference of the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains ascend to the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize that area on each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize the area, and do not press too hard with your hand and the cautery beforehand; let it only be around the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains rise to both knees, then apply the cautery which we have described in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands, and it is called  (a type of) numbness, then puncture in the circle of the veins as they twist, puncture those two lines.
And if the pains remain in the fingers, then puncture in the circle of the veins on each finger and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains rise near the elbows or muscles in a few days, then cauterize them in every part of the joints.
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and the reception of medicines, for if good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, they will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of fractures.
When a fracture occurs in the knee and the intestines protrude in front of the patella into the genital area, and this is beginning to happen, then you should order the patient to stop eating that day and use lenitive remedies so that the rupture descends from their abdomen.
Then support the patient between your hands on their back, and instruct them to hold their breath until the patella or intestines are expelled or appear.
Then, with your words and signs, gently return the rupture over the bone of the pubis, noting that the extremity should resemble a half-circle in the upper parts of the body.
Then heat the cautery, whose form is this:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not protrude.
You have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion underneath; and let another servant be on their legs, and another on their feet, who should hold their hands.
Then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and hold it there until you feel the bone of the hip within the middle of the swelling; and repeat with it another time if you did not achieve it in the first attempt to reach the bone.
And observe carefully the position of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn the intestine, lest death or other great harm befall the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, do not continue your operation.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small, according to their size and measure.
Then care for the cauterized area for three days with a poultice until the burn scab is softened.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and wound are sealed/healed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which will cleanse his belly, and that the intestine does not escape due to the effort of healing.
And then, when he wants to get up after about forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be reduced in work and in the repletion of food and drink, and in vehement voice, for when he uses this regimen of complete healing he will be healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the belly; and at the beginning, if you want them not to spread, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that which is exuded from the cautery be like a third part of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is appropriate.
And some first said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this form, since it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed which goes through the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a pointed cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion.
When pain develops in some member because of a fall or a blow, and that pain persists for a long time and does not yield to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cauterization of each member be according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and its pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cauterization, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization; for it is customary for such pains to be punctuated from one member to the next nearest one, so it is necessary to treat those with cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes treated with cautery with great benefit, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so, if you wish to cauterize the leper, consider whether the leprosy is spreading, and if it is not cured by what we have said in the division, and if it persists, and you fear that the corruption will affect the whole body and its entire complexion, then cauterize it on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and the second a little below, towards the forehead at the hairline; and the others on either side of the two horns; and one behind, on the nape of the neck or back of the head.
And finally, with your hand, press the cautery slightly inwards so that it makes a small impression on the bone, and the scabs are removed from it, so that the expulsion of thick vapors may be relieved from it; and cauterize over the spleen as needed.
And if the leprosy is already spread over the patient and is manifestly apparent, then you must cauterize it with those cauteries which we have mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large one on the vertebrae near the buttocks, and one on the buttock, and with two on the hips, and one on each hip, and with two on the knees and one on each knee, and with two on the elbows, and with two on the muscles, and with two on the ankles of his feet; and cauterize it on all the joints of the fingers of the hands and feet, and on each heel of his feet, and on the wrists of his hands.
And beware of the nerves which are on the heel, that you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and with another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.
And know that the more closely placed the cauteries, the more beneficial and helpful; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as he does from wounds, because his body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as is prescribed by the types of instruments.
And then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes some numbness is cauterized which extends to the hands and feet, and to the vertebrae of the back and near the origin of the nerves which move that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not attempt this except one who is skilled in the anatomy of the limbs and the origin of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of albaras, which is morphea.
When albaras is chronic and you do not apply any medicinal ointments to it, then cauterize it with a hot cautery, a little according to the size of the affected area, until the whiteness and its color are relieved, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of barley and lentils with rose oil and arnaglossa leaves, and with pigeon's blood or hen's blood, of each an equal part and all mixed together, and then apply a linen cloth over the area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you wish to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around its circumference, as it is swollen.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery in the middle; but to me this is not advisable, because I fear that it will be ulcerated, and I have already seen this happen several times.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 51.
On the cauterization of swellings.
When a swelling is slow to mature, and this is due to the superfluity causing it, or to the age of the patient, for he is old and decrepit, with little blood, or because of the time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the swelling; then cauterize around its circumference with many small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the swelling until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in a lower place, so that the body of the arm may be relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
On cauterization with a corrosive.
A corrosive is a corruption that moves in the limb, which corrodes it like fire consuming dry wood.
