Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place that has suffered cauterization with fire, then you immediately cauterize with very small nails, and large ones according to what is appropriate in the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it completely until you eradicate all the corrosion and none of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and place crushed sulfur mixed with oil on the cauterized areas until the scabs are completely eradicated and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with flesh-generating ointments.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh has grown where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on what remains in the corrupted places.
And corrosion is sometimes treated with a sharp medicine, for that serves in place of cauterization. But however, cauterization with fire is of more readily available help.
And I have already described the treatment of it with sharp medicine in the division, and therefore take that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
Of the cauterization of wounds and other similar things.
Many times a disease called a wound occurs in the parts below the foot, and it is a large, penetrating wound which injures the foot; and cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, that is, cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is that you heat the concave cautery resembling a vulture's beak, which should be made of iron according to the size of the wound in every part, and should be of fine edges.
Then place that heated instrument on the wound, and move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed pus. Then plaster it with crushed wild mallow herbs mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
Then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it does not heal, and often appears on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you must apply cautery with water to those areas. Take a small tube of bronze, iron, or vulture bone, and place it over the wound; then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube, and hold your hand, and rotate it with the pressure of your entire hand, pressing the edge of the tube into the root of the wound. The hot water will find its way to the root of the wound; let the patient endure the bite of the hot water for about an hour, and then remove it, for the wound with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this to those and then to the others until you have treated all of them that are in the body.
Then treat the sores, once the roots of the wounds are eradicated, with ointments that generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
Concerning the cauterization of tremor.
When someone experiences tremor due to cold in the nerves, or from a cold fever, or from a similar cause, then you should cauterize them with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, with an olive-shaped cautery.
And their tremor will subside, and hasten the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
Concerning the cauterization of accidental swellings in the body.
When in the body there are swellings and pustules formed from thick, corrupted matter; then at the beginning, as it appears, it should be cauterized over the head of the fistula with a fine cautery with myrtle wood, whose extremity is extended with fire; or with a long root of eryngo; or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes the cautery is also suitable at the beginning of its expulsion, according to this manner; and they are not believed, and the superfluity is broken up by doing this, and heals from it.
However, it is necessary that this be done near evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56. On the cauterization of a blood flux accidental to an incision from an arrow.
Often there is a flux from an arrow wound which is incised through the wound, which comes out, or through the incision of abscesses or cautery of the limb, and then similarly, and it is difficult to stop that.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you press with your hand on the opening of the arrow wound, and place your finger on it, tightly pressing it many times until the flow of blood stops under your finger, and it does not come out.
Then put small and large olive cauteries in the fire, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then take one of them, small or large depending on the location and the wound where the artery is broken,  quickly applying it to that vein after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery there until that blood is stopped and restrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those which are prepared in the fire, and do not cease to do this one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that no other harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to stop it in those cases, especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction if it is not extracted, for when it is extracted the extremities are better burned and the bleeding is stopped, or that it be tied with thread or with a strong ligature, or that medicines whose virtue is to stop blood be applied to it; and they should be held in place with a compress with a decent constriction.
But that which administers a decent restriction with ligature and constriction with cloths or by application of cauterizing substances and the like, does not confer benefit except slowly.
But if this happens to someone and neither physician nor medicine is present, then gently place the index finger over the opening of that vein as we have said, and apply strong pressure until the blood is stopped from the main part of the wound, and the finger is not removed from the artery; and on the artery sprinkle very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and then consider what need there is of cautery or medicine.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their similars.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire and with burning medicine is appropriate, and its occasions and causes, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the head to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one who seeks this will be relieved, then concerning that which he will have requested.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter there is fear, that which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because it is necessary that there be great caution in it, for in the operations which are in this chapter, many times there is evacuation which is with blood, with which life remains, because of the opening of a vein or incision on an abscess, or opening of a fistula, or purification of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision of a stone, and their like. And with all those things are accompanied fear and trembling, and in the worst of them, death.
And I warn you that this should not befall you, in which there is hesitation, for sometimes it happens to you, the ways of men with many kinds of illnesses. And of them there is some who is angry with his illness and death is light for him, and this is because of the vehemence of what he finds in his illness, and because of the length of his illness, and with the illness is a fear which signifies death.
And of those there is one who gives you his wealth and says, you hoping with that for health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to him who comes to you of whom this manner is; and your caution should be greater than covetousness and your greed; and do not presume anything about this except from certain knowledge which verifies this for you, and to which follows praiseworthy success.
And use in the cure of all your patients prognosis and indication of him to whom health is restored, for in that is to you help towards acquisition of fame and memory, of praise and glory.
O, sons, may God inspire in you his understanding and not hinder your direction and protection, for this is in his hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as I preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that this which you ask for and of which you have need may be alleviated for you.
Chapter one.
Of the treatment of the water which is contained in the head of infants.
Know that this illness often happens to infants during childbirth and when the midwife squeezes the infant's head excessively.
And sometimes it happens due to a hidden cause which is unknown.
But I have only seen this illness in infants; and all those whom I saw died from it, and thus it is, in my view, a deadly affliction for them.
And I have seen that an infant whose head was filled with water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could no longer support himself because of the size of his head, and moreover, the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity is either accumulated between the skull and the bone, or it is accumulated under the bone on the scalp.
Because, in that case, if the humidity is between the skull and the bone and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with a fissure in width, and let the length of the fissure be about two nuts so that the humidity flows out, and this is the form of the spatula:
And therefore, if the humidity is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two fissures in this manner:
And if the humidity is under the bone, the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head opened on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress that with your hand on the inner parts, and this is not hidden from you. It is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
And then extract all the humidity from the incision; and then tighten the fissures with cloths and feathers; and then bathe it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then untie the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little humidity until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appears, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore attend with the incision where the abscess and the repletion of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and that a flow of blood ensues and the patient dies from that flow of blood with evacuation of humidity.
Chapter 3. (or Second Chapter, depending on whether the numbering restarts with each major section or continues sequentially.)
When someone suffers from cataracts or rheum in the eyes or feet, and this is chronic, and medicinal treatments do not help, then the last resort is incision of those two arteries (likely referring to the arteries mentioned in the previous chapter, near the ears).
Therefore, when you wish to incise them, have the patient raise their head, and gently rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear.  Then, tighten the patient's neck with a cloth; and carefully observe where the vein pulsates; and the location of each, above the points near the ears, is compressed, and is thus hidden except in certain individuals.
Then mark the artery with a probe, and incise each one with a sharp lancet on each side, making an incision that reaches the bone, and this should be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and cut it at the incisions made above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' breadth, for when a vein is incised, the blood will exit with a pulsatile flow, leaping out frequently.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you must measure three fingers' width from the ear, and mark it with a probe, and cut down to the bone.
And the amount of blood to be let is about six ounces, and quickly let more or less, depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
And then examine the wound, and if any cause of the injury remains on the bone, then incise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
Then bind the wound with feathers and linen bandages, and treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrimonious humors and a quality in the tissues of the temples, or a chronic and similar ailment, and it is treated with medical remedies and this does not help, then we must attempt in these illnesses the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or their cauterization as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hair which is on the temples; and locate the artery appearing in the temple, for it is shown to you by its pulse, and is hidden except in a few men because of the intensity of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the neck of the patient should be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or warm the area with hot water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you scrape the skin with this easily until you reach the artery; and then put in it a hook and extract it to the superior parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are below it, from every part.
But if the artery is subtle, then wrap it with the end of the hook, and then cut a part of it according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, because if they are not incised the blood will not flow at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with cords at length, and do not let it putrefy before the consolidation of the wound and a flow of blood occurs.
And then incise the superfluity which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone with a knife-shaped cautery until its ends are incised, and it will be in place of the same work.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or is of a hot complexion; because cautery is among those things that help drying humors, and therefore it is better in this situation.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you fill the place with old linen, and that powders are placed on it decently made, and then it should be treated with drying medicines causing the flesh to grow and with bandages until it is healed.
And if while you are operating another time, a flow of blood from the artery occurs, then you should take care to restrain that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the place with linen, and press with your hand until the blood is restrained. And if none of those things are at your disposal, then place your finger on the place until the blood is congealed, and bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and it is constricted as needed.
And what is lighter in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and apply each, very hot, until they reach the bone and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between the extremities is elongated, because they are not entirely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be equal. However, if it has less sharpness than two knives, it is better, because if it is sharp like two knives it will approach each cold [presumably referring to a cold instrument or condition] and will not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since in those [instruments] there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is retained in them and the flesh is cut lightly.
And this method is better in every respect and gentler.
And let the space which is between the two blades be the amount of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter four.
Chapter Four: On the treatment of sharp and acidic tears of the eyes.
When there are acidic tears, and it is the flow from those veins which are on the head's surface, and you find no cause for treatment in medicine, and sometimes the patient's face reddens and they feel in their forehead a crawling, as if it were the crawling of ants, and their eyes are swollen, moist, so that their hairs are stuck together, and the eyelids are inflamed by the acridity of the tears, the ancients were skillful in the treatment of these cases, and they called that treatment *spassa*.
And it is that you command the patient to shave the hairs which are on his forehead, and then incise three fissures in the forehead, equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure reaches two fingers: one fissure should be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length of a little movement of the muscles which are in the lacrimal duct, and the third on the other side.
And guard yourself from the incision of the arteries which are on both sides, and lengthen your hand from the continuation of both jaws. And if the space of each incision is the amount of three adjusted fingers.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which to dry the blood.
And then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that [tool/instrument] the entire area which is between the two incisions, with the [surgical] knife which is above the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a curette, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that [instrument] against the flesh attached to the bone, and the blunt edge against the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to that part of the bone, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed, press the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a pledget of old linen, and put on them powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day, untie the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the treatment of tears caused by cataracts and descending to the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone experiences acute and persistent cataracts, and the patient's eyes appear small and sunken, and their vision is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their skin falls away, and there is sharp, persistent pain in the forehead and consequent sneezing, then know from these symptoms that the matter and cataracts come from many veins originating in the inner parts.
And the best treatment in such cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then incise a fissure in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be between the bones; and extend your hand from the temples which are damaged through chewing), until the bone is uncovered.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two lips of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then bind it above with powders soaked in wine and oil so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind it and see that the abscess has diminished, then you should scrape the bone until new flesh begins to grow there, and then treat it with a drying regimen that will cause the flesh to grow, such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and an emplaster is made from them and applied to these wounds to promote the growth of new flesh.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the ears.
Every cause that falls into the ears is one of four types, for it is either a mineral stone or something similar to stone, such as iron and glass, or it is from a plant such as a thistle or bean, or it is a fluid substance such as water and sour wine and things like them, or some animal.
And therefore, when a stone falls into the ear or another similar cause among those things which do not adhere to or grow in the ear, and you place the ear in the sun, and if you see the stone, then instill into it some violet oil or sesame oil, and then proceed with its extraction, and induce sneezing with a suitable remedy, and obstruct the nostrils before the onset of sneezing. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upwards, for many times it is expelled by these means.
And if it is not expelled, then proceed with its extraction using the instrument called a slender, epicyclic probe, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the probe, it is good; and if not, then proceed with its extraction using a thin hook with little curvature.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by this, then make a small tube of brass and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear, and seal around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open except the tube. And then extract it with your breath with strong suction, for many times this has been accomplished as we have recounted.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small amount of lit tinder or a similar preparation used for catching birds, and place it on the end of a thin rod, and then carefully wrap a suitable cotton around that. And then carefully insert that into the opening of the ear with ease, and then dry the ear of its moisture.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by all the methods which we have recounted, then you should consider cutting it out before it develops into a hot abscess or spasm.
And the manner of cutting it out is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract the blood according to their strength, and then hold the ear between your hands and turn the ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear, above the cartilage, in a well-prepared area. And let the incision be crescent-shaped until the stone is reached; and, when the stone is found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then immediately close the incision with speed, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause lodged in the ear is a growth of fat that is swollen, then administer its cure and extraction; but if it does not respond to such treatment, then take finely powdered litharge according to this method: and apply it to the excision of that growth which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and apply it in small amounts, and then remove it with tweezers or a fine instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thereby eased.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing initially, and fill his ear with some old cotton, and lie down on the ear in which the water is, and if it is expelled by this, it is good; and if not, then take several heated pebbles or beans according to the length of the finger, and warm them slightly in the fire, and the patient inserts one of these into the opening of his ear and jumps on one foot on that side and strikes with another stone upon the stone which is inside the ear. Therefore do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And the water will be expelled in this way: you take some paper or tow, and put one end of it in the ear and hold the other end to a fire until the greater part of it is burned.
Then put in more tow and do this several times until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula as was said above of the extracting stone.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small, like a louse or similar, treat it as we have said in the division; but if it is large, which appears to the judgment of the eye, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks, and the cause of this is easier than all the other causes which fall into the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment does not work, then you must expose the ear to the sun.
And then, if any cause of the worms appears, then extract it with forceps or with fine hooks; but if no cause appears, then take a cannula narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And introduce the narrow end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it strongly, and do this many times until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you should fill and close the opening of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the treatment of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and flat above.
And then let it be such that it pushes what is in the concavity of the cannula outwards where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a ray [possibly referring to a thin, rod-like instrument] and wrap its end decently with cotton, and then pour oil into the concavity of the ear, or the juice of some herb, or whatever you want of these medicines into the cannula — and that is in the ear — and then insert the ray with cotton on top and press with your hand that with moderate compression until the oil is expelled into the concavity of the hearing and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is poured into the ear should be warmed a little by fire. And be careful that what you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of ear obstruction.
Sometimes children are born without perforated ears, and this also happens in some adults, an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or a growth of flesh within; and this obstruction sometimes occurs in the inner part of the ear canal, which does not allow hearing, and sometimes in the outer parts.
And that which occurs in the inner part, which does not allow hearing, is in most cases difficult to heal.
And in that case where hearing is possible, you should expose the patient's ear to the sun and examine it. And then, if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a fine spatula, of which this is the form:
If its end is narrow, one side being sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be two finger-widths wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And so if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or its canal, then take thin hooks and cut away that fleshy growth forcefully until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the vestibule of the ear, then take a fine, thin probe and heat it slightly with fire, and then place it into the ear canal.
And then if you have felt an obstruction with which the patient experiences difficulty, due to impaired hearing, force the obstruction out gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a linen cloth, according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take a wick and soak it in warm water, and sprinkle crushed  [herb/powder - the word is unclear] on it and use it; and if you see the ear is already abscessed, then you must remove the wick and replace it with another wick that you will wrap in a cerate made with rose oil, until the abscess is completely drained; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which stops the bleeding, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which develops on the eyelids.
Styes which develop on the eyelids are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with an awl, and incise them with a spatula from their roots, and afterwards place a poultice on the incised areas.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a thin, lentil-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains from their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often there appears on the eyelids of the eyes some growth which resembles a swelling in its firmness and hardness, and because of its swelling it is called [a swelling/tumor].
And it is an accumulation of thick humidity in the eyelid, below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you should consider then if the swelling originates in a way that it appears from the eyelid and is spread to every part; and its cause is light.
Then make an incision on it in width, and then peel it off from every part easily until it is removed, and then lift it with tweezers and cut it off.
And if the lesion is large, then you should close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, it will not harm you because the ointment heals it and causes it to granulate (heal by forming new tissue).
And if the growth is leaning towards the inner parts of the eyelid from the inner surface, then lift the eyelid and lift the growth with tweezers, without needing to make an incision, and press it from all sides, and if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, this does no harm in any case.
Then, after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with a healing agent until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a stye on the eyelid above the eye.
A stye is a swelling which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and more frequently in children, it irritates their eyes and causes them to become inflamed; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears gather in them.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and the lower eyelids, below their eyebrows, are moist, showing inflammation, and the imminent appearance of a stye is in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the stye within.
And one method of operation is that the patient rests their head in your lap, and you place a cloth between them and yourself, and make a circle of it according to the size needed to encompass the whole stye, and place that over the stye and press with your fingers all around so that the stye is contained in the center of the circle, and then, in the center of that swelling, make a cut with a lancet, creating a fissure, but let the fissure be no larger than the incision made in bloodletting.
But in the beginning it is necessary that you incise the skin completely until you reach the stye, because in most cases the stye is excised through an incision, according to the form of a white swelling.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers at the edges and roll it between your right and left hand until it is removed.
And be careful not to go too deep with the incision so that with the lancet you do not reach the eye and injure it.
But if the stye does not appear at the beginning of the incision, then go a little deeper into the incision gently until you reach the stye; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in wine and vinegar and apply them to the area, and press them with powders.
And some people crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision so that it dries up the remaining moisture.
And then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the site, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the various ways of touching the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes, growths appear under the natural hairs and are hardened, then these harm the eye and cause various illnesses such as continual tears and softening of the eyelids and membrane, until this causes destruction of the eye, which is called a touch-infection of the eye.  It is cured in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with a sharp medicine as previously stated in the chapter on cautery; or the touch-infection is treated by incision and suture; or by canes, as I shall explain.
And therefore it is necessary that you place the patient's head in your lap; then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, good; and if not, introduce a needle into that part where there is a swelling beneath the eyelid, and push the needle with the swelling upwards, and this should be — near the margin of the eyelid where the hairs grow — near the hairs, and pull the swelling towards the upper parts with the eyelid, and turn it with the ray (presumably a surgical instrument, perhaps similar to a speculum).
And then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid beneath the hardened hairs with a sharp instrument from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct; and then remove the swelling and place small pieces of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then sew up the eyelid with thread, and let it be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of elevation of the eyelid, for this varies in people, for some it is necessary that a large amount of the eyelid be incised, according to the extent to which the eyelid is softened.
And then make an incision with the sharp instrument along the two lines which you have sewn, beginning from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, like the thickness of a king's (presumably a reference to a specific size or type of needle or instrument).
And then place the hook in one of the two angles of the carbuncle, and then excise the whole of it, and then join the two lips of the wound with glue or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and stick what superfluous matter remains of the carbuncle to the two eyebrows with some sticky substance, if you wish to do so, and if not, do not worry.
And then leave the suture of the threads for three or four days, and then treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its union; but suture is better.  Therefore the ancients advised this.
However, let there be diligence in the patient's care, and it is a good operation, and let there be no fear in it.
And there is another way to treat it, which is to mark on the eyelid a figure like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three separate and joined hooks according to this form:
But if this is not possible, to hold the hooks, and this is not suitable, then take a needle on which there is thread and place it in the middle of the figure, and introduce the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads between your fingers with equality, and then with those, with equal elevation, raise the entire area on which it is marked as we have recounted.
And then join the two lips of the wound with a suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted; then it is necessary that you use on it what we have said about incision; however, suture in each case is more skillful/artificial.
To splint with canes is according to this form: and it is that you turn back the eyelid and split the growth that is within, as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two thin canes or splints whose length is according to the length of the eyelid and whose width is less than the width of the eyelid. And you have already incised at the ends of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully add [something] over the excess of the eyelid's skin, and bind the canes to each part with a strong ligature, and leave it for some days, for the growth, through the binding, will die and dry up and rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Then, when it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not sting.
And this is the form of the two canes which you will make according to this form: and let it have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Chapter 12. Of the elevation of stinging hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and stinging hairs in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle, and place on it a fine silken thread, and then adjust its ends, tying each in a very subtle manner. And let the length of the threads be the quantity of a palm.
And then you prepare two other fine threads underneath, and tie their ends if you wish.
And then have the patient place their head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that your work is illuminated, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the offending hair, which needs to be removed, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair upwards and insert it into the  *alixati* (likely a solution or ointment – the exact meaning requires further context), and let there be one, two, or three hairs, and no more.
And then pull your hand with that until the hair is removed along with the *alixolati* from the eyelid, along with the natural hair.
But if the *alixalati* is pulled and the hair is not removed, you will pull the *alixalati* downwards with the thread that you have composed with it, until the *alixalati* is pulled out from the wound above.
And examine it with the vision of your eye, for then you will remove the *alixalati* and the thread, and clean each and close the eye, and let it be closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is elevated with that, or you let it be until it is lengthened by some space, and then you elevate it.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which appears on the upper eyelid.
When a stye appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor consolidation of a wound or incision or cautery, and it is called *leporina* (harelip-like), and this *ahecrati* (likely a variant of 'hacreati' meaning stye or related eye condition) is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the consolidation of a wound or incision or cautery, or by similar poorly healed conditions.
The manner of the operation is that you incise that consolidation and separate its lips, and place between them a small piece of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not fitting that you use treatments which dry or constrict; for if you do this, the stye will return worse than it was before.
Use on it things that soften, such as fenugreek, and bathe it with the water in which mallow and its seeds are boiled, or with diachylon ointment, which should be liquefied with some oil, and immerse the linen cloth in it, and treat it with that.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you ensure, by every means, that it is not consolidated in a way different from its original form.
And this operation which we have described is nothing but a correction of the stye so that it does not return to its former state.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of a stye which is formed on the lower eyelid.
This stye which forms on the lower eyelid, which is truly called a stye; and it has a natural cause, or an accidental one.
But an accidental one is caused by a wound, or by a cut, or by cautery, or by similar things.
And the method of its operation is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left lachrymal gland to the right lachrymal gland, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle and sew it with the needle, and incise it with a wide spatula. And then if the shape of the eyelid returns as it should be, it is good; and if not, take a splint and place it in the slit, and with it reshape the eyelid, and make the two incisions in the edge of the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two incisions be two angles of the incision which you have made until they meet, and let an angle be made of them so that when they are joined together, their shape is made.
And this shape is a Greek letter: lambda (Λ).
Then oppose the flesh to that (amount) which is the width of the sharp lower eyelid, so that it may be the eyelid.  (This is a difficult sentence due to the archaic phrasing and somewhat unclear reference; it likely refers to precisely aligning the incised flesh to restore the eyelid's natural shape and width).
And then join the separated parts and sew them with wool thread; and then treat them with what we have said of emollient medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if it is raised by a fissure, suture, or cautery, then it is necessary that you make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid according to what has been previously described. And then separate with a poultice between two lips as we have said.
In summary of the discourse on the treatment of an abscess, whether above or below, let the operation proceed according to the preparation of the abscess, and that is in the form of many varieties.
And the skilled artisan always prepares instruments in every possible way so that he can restore the figure to its natural form, or close to it, and the patient according to what suits him in the operation and instruments in the best dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Chapter 15. Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation happens to many men such that the upper eyelid is adhered to the white of the eye, and its natural movement and function are impeded, then you must introduce the tip of a probe under the eyelid and lift it up, or spread it with an unguent.
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharply pointed, but rather slightly blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and ensure that the spatula does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision gently separate that adhesion until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and the adhesion is completely healed.
Then you apply salt water to the eye to wash it; or distil into it some acacia dissolved in water.
Then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small, smooth instrument, and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye.  Then, on the third day, use consolidative remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types, for it is either nervous, and is similar to hard, thin horn; or it is non-nervous, similar to a congealed white humidity, which, when the iron touches it, or when it is forced to be removed with tweezers, is cut off and the tweezers are not inserted into it.
And both types begin only from the larger tear duct, until it grows little by little, covering the vision and preventing light and the movement of the eye.
But the manner of operating in its incision is that the patient places his head in your lap, and then open his eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with tweezers of little breadth and extend it towards the upper parts. Then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or ox hair, or cow hair, or similar, or strong thread, or double the end of the needle little by little and insert it into the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it to the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the side of the nail which obscures the pupil. And thus you follow it with the hair into the back of it.
And then you incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that by the incision of it there is no continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail, with the tweezers alone extended or with thread, as we have said, and then it should be scraped with a lion spatula, of which this is the form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then, into the wound, put a little crushed salt or a poultice made of linseed, and leave that in until the next day; and then treat it with what is necessary until it is healed.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is it embedded with the tweezers, then many times this type of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed as I have recounted in the division (chapter/section).
And if you want to incise it, then you open the wound of the patient and take a fine lion spatula of sharpened iron according to this form: with subtlety/precision. And if you see that it is rarefied and is congested and festering in that root, then distill from your still into the wound a poultice made of linseed or with rue or with crushed salt, and plug the wound until the next day.
And then repeat the operation on it until all that is there is removed, unless on the contrary a hot abscess forms in the wound, for then you leave it be, and treat first the abscess until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it with reason until it is healed.
The eminence of the lachrymal flesh, if noticeable in the wound as a great impediment, should be suspended with hooks; and incise it on one side, and do not extend the incision so that you do not cut the flow of tears.
And then distill into the lachrymal duct a poultice of rue and linseed until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the incision of a carbuncle and of that which is born of flesh fastened in the wound.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is grown, and it is over-raised until it covers the pupil, or approaches or presses on the eyelids, and strongly quickly compresses the eyelids to the outer parts and is similar to a rose of a balustrade.
But the operation in it is that the patient puts his head on your lap; and then open the eyes of that one and collect all that flesh with instruments convenient for this, and hold it with forceps or with scissors; and then incise the flesh at the beginning until all is finished with the lancet, and guard yourself from the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And let your incision with which you incise those be with a spatula, which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which the hair is collected, according to how the form of it will come in this chapter.
Then when your incision is completed and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt, or distill into it red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place on the outside of the eye a bandage with egg white until it is secured from a hot abscess.
And if some cause of inflammation remains and hot blood is observed, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and treat that abscess with what will cure that abscess, and then repeat on it with an operation until it is healed, and similarly do in the fleshy excrescence which happens in the eye of this kind.
However, you should avoid any treatment of the fragile eye by natural or accidental means, operating with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that the limb is able to endure that operation.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is red veins which are swollen on the eye and prohibit the proper operation, and they weaken the eye over many days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and has no other disease than pus; then you may collect that pus.
And it is that you command the patient to place his head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with one or two onces, according to your judgment; and let that ounce be of subtle doubling according to this form:
or two ounces gradually in one piece according to this form:
And then let those veins be collected with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins be removed from the eye and be healed.
And be careful that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is in the midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation that you do not incise except those veins.
And when you have finished, then you distill into the eye verdigris or vinegar so that with its acidity it corrodes what is removed from the eyelid.
But if that is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster everything with something that relieves his pain, and leave it for some days until his pain is calmed and the danger of a hot abscess is averted.
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Of the reduction of dislocations in the nose.
When you treat a dislocation with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as previously described, and it is not healed, then there is no generation in it, but rather you should make an incision on the abscess before its maturation, and extract all the humidity that is within it and the pus, until the bone is exposed.
And then, when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a rasp, and it should be made of Indian iron, and its head should be round, like a chisel, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle pointing (as is shown in the following form).
And place that [rasp] on the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the place with drying and styptic substances.
Then, if the place is consolidated and good flesh has grown there and the body of the pus is separated and remains for forty days, and it is not renewed and is not festering and no accident happens to it, then you will know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is no other remedy but to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and according to this manner:  it is that you uncover the underlying bone with an iron instrument or with a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a trephine according to this form:
Let the extremity of the iron trephine be triangular, and its shaft be thin and tapered to the extremity, like this.
And then place that [trephine] on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and extend your hand a little from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the trephine until it penetrates the whole bone, and the patient will feel it because they will feel the wind escaping when you have your hand there.
Then restore the place with styptic and drying medicines, as I have taught you.
Then, when the flesh grows and the place is hardened, the matter which flowed to the outside parts returns to the nose, therefore this is easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
Of the reduction of the protrusion of the eye.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety and is not injured by a wound or diminution, then you should let the patient lie down, and then you should follow that, and then you should place cupping glasses on the neck without scarification and suction for good suction; and then place on the eye an ointment which is made from acacia, aloe, incense, and sarcocolla, and then firmly bind the plaster on the eye with many powders.
And the manner of the powders is that you take many linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded into four layers; and make many of those.
And then place those one on top of the other according to the amount that the eye needs. And then bind tightly over those with a strong cloth with a firm constriction; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if meanwhile the ligament is softened, then it must be very tightly bound.
And then remove the bandage during the day and night, as we have said.
And if at times the eye is already reduced, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the binding and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the incision of the eminence of the uvea tunic. (or, Of the incision of the prominence of the uveal coat)
When there is a rupture in the uvea tunic, and it protrudes and extends beyond the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is marred by it, and you wish to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted: that you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from the lower part to the upper part, and then place another needle with double thread from the lachrymal side (near the tear duct), and make that penetrate and leave the first needle according to its placement; and then incise the place of the double thread and lift with that the part of the uvea in the circumference of the needle, and tighten that firmly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and leave it until the thread with the uvea falls away.
And then treat the eye with something that will strengthen it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes gather from all sides with a circle of cloth around the eye a large prominent swelling, and then it should be perforated with a fine instrument that reaches the pupil of the eye, and lets out the albuminous moisture, and the eye descends and enters immediately.
And then compress it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of [a condition, likely an eye condition, given the preceding context.  "Alconati" is likely a corrupted or archaic word needing further context for precise translation.]
This illness, which is called alconati, is nothing but a fluid that is located in the eye, similar to descending water, but it is not that [i.e., it's not simply water].
But the method of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight chair, and then take his head in your hands on both sides and manipulate it until you see the fluid come to the lower parts, and then let it be dried and not descend, and let the light be obstructed, and the patient see things as he used to see them.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the fluid is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a fine spatula and use it on the pannicle, wiping a little below the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the limbus (towards the empty space), until the fluid is expelled.
Then, when it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have previously boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then let it be treated with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of the watery discharge from the eye.
We have already discussed the types of watery eye discharge in the division and which ones it is appropriate to pierce with a lancet, then that which according to health. (This sentence is grammatically awkward and could be improved with more context.  A more natural translation might be: "We have already described the types of watery eye discharge and which should be lanced, and those that will heal on their own.")
Then it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands on a square [seat/cushion] and tie his healthy eye, a very tight bandage.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of his eye, if the eye which has the water is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is left; or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
And then place the end of the almond near the cornea, along the width of the iris, in the white of the eye on the side of the smaller lachrymal duct, and then gently press the almond and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate the white of the eye and feel that the almond has already reached the empty cause. (This likely means "feel that the almond has reached the source of the problem"  or something similar. The phrase "causa vueda" is unclear and could refer to a cavity or a void related to the ailment.)
And it should be that the distance of the almond to the pupil is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, which is the cornea; for you will see with that pupil through the vision of the eye by the clarity of the cornea.
And then place the almond into the place where the water is, and then you pull it down once, then another, and then if the water descends immediately, then the patient will see that upon which his sight falls immediately, and the almond in his eye. (This refers to a procedure involving removing fluid from the eye.  "Aygua" means water, likely referring to accumulated fluid.)
And then rest a little; then if the water has descended, then you should remove it another time before the almond is expelled.
And then when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and gently rotate it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the water and wash the eye inside with it; and then place outside the eye cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white and bind the eye with this.
But we and those of our time place over that a compress of egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you upon entering the eye because of its hardness—for there are some men whose eyes are very hard—then it is necessary that you take the spatula which is called an alberid, of which this is the form:
Then perforate that conjunctiva with it, and do not thrust your hand into the hole, but only make a subtle pathway for the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And for the completion of your work, let a comfortably made bed be prepared for the patient, in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room, and let all movement be forbidden.
And position him in such a way that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And let the healing be according to its disposition within the third day.
And then release him from that dark room and gradually expose him to light, and show him some things, and then return to the treatment within the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that you do this at the hour of the treatments or immediately after the infusion. Furthermore, it is necessary that you avoid this because the water might easily rise through great and vehement observation/attention.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you open the wound before the seventh day, and rectify it with something that reduces the abscess.
Then you cover his eyes and place over his face a cloth by which his sight is governed, keeping him for some days in the darkest room.
And then let him be moved from that room gradually and gradually converse about his affairs.
And know that in a cataract, the pupil is not excused from some visions, and then you should be bold about the operation.
And it has come to us from some of those who are at Artach, and someone said that they make fire in Ayrac, a perforated vessel, by which water is sucked.
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read this in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the kinds of magdaans so that he who is ignorant of them may know them:
And they should be made of bronze or iron properly, and their ends should be with this triangulated subtlety, the sharp end like that which is the form.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of rotting flesh in the nose.
When various fleshy growths appear in the nose, some of which resemble a very small scorpion, or a polyp fish; and this illness is called polyp because it resembles that represented fish. And from it is cancerous flesh, spreading, of a dark color; and from it is flesh like a lion, not of dark color.
Therefore, in the case of these fleshy growths that are not malignant or cancerous, you should make the patient sit between your hands and place them in the sun, and open their nostrils, and gently prick those growths with your lancet. Then extract it to the outside, and then incise what you have grasped of those growths with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; if any part remains from which excision is possible, then scrape it with one of the subtle instruments easily until nothing remains. But if it bleeds profusely or a hot, malignant abscess forms, then apply what is necessary; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you should incise and cauterize it until the bleeding is stopped and all the cavities are cleared.
Then, after the incision, apply sharp vinegar and water, or just vinegar, to the nose. But if the nose is open and no moisture flows into the palate, then you know that it is healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is rotten flesh in the upper bones of the nose to which excision has not reached.
Therefore, it is necessary to introduce a linen thread into the nostrils, which should be of some thickness, and tie knots on it; and place between each knot the amount of a finger or less, and then insert the thread into the nostrils with a rod or other suitable instrument.
And after this has been done, let it draw its fragrance until it reaches the nasal passages and comes out over the throat, for often children at school do something similar, and it is an easy thing for one who wishes to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end outside the nose and the other remaining inside the nose, and then apply sawdust with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is now together with the knots of the thread.
And then remove the thread, and apply to the nose, near the extraction of the blood, a poultice, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that is rotten flesh.
And finally, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for several days until it is healed.
But if you need a more drying cure, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument, a head-purging device, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or brass, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small bowl, and the spout similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the head-purging instrument should be flat and open; it should have a handle at the back, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or water, or whatever you want of the juices and extractable substances.
Chapter 25.
Of nasal polyps in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a polyp grows in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in a few days to the point that it is easily visible.
And for this it is necessary that you incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate it completely. And then apply to the area either a cautery or a burning medicine which will stay in its place.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened, firm, of dark color and with little sensation; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous ulcer; for many times he who incises this ulcer suffers great harm from it, the one who had it.
And if the ulcer is of a clear appearance and not of dark color, and sometimes incision is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and cure the area with something that dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when their continuity is broken by a wound or similar injury.
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, you should have little confidence in that operation except in certain men.
Therefore, when such a thing happens to someone, consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, and then you join the two lips of the wound with suture, and then cure it until it is healed.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then it is necessary that you scarify each part of their apparent skin until blood comes out. Then join the two lips with suture and tighten them, and on them sprinkle dragon's blood and finely ground egg shells, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave it bound for two or three days, then unbind it and change the plaster and leave it until the threads are themselves cut, and then cure with ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of the suture is that you close the solution of continuity either with needles, as we have recounted in the suture of the belly, or close the solution of continuity with thread, as has been made known to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things which come into the two lips in the inner parts.
Many times there appear in the inner parts of the lips of many men certain hard abscesses resembling goose fat, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn back the lips and separate each of those hardnesses on all sides, and afterwards fill the place of the excision with crushed sage until the blood is restrained, and afterwards it should be washed with sharp vinegar and the place treated with something that is styptic, until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the incision of the flesh adhered to the gums.
Often a growth appears on the gum, flesh that adheres, which the ancients called an albulus; then it is necessary that you lift that with a hook or hold it with forceps and incise it down to its root, and let the serum or blood flow.
And afterwards place on the area crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for afterwards the cautery will prevent it from returning.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the treatment of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, rough, sticky, and sometimes blackened scabs are attached, and they become citrine or green, so that corruption extends to the gum, and the teeth become loose.
And then it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and place his head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those scabs and things resembling sand appear, until nothing of them remains, and do this to remove the blackness, the greenness, and the others until they are cleaned.
Then, if what was in those [teeth] at the beginning of the treatment is removed, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the treatment on those [teeth] on the next day and the second and the third until you arrive at the desired end.
And know that the teeth require many scrapers of diverse shapes and figures, according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the scraper with which the tooth is scraped on the inner part is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth according to other forms, these forms of scrapers should be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
That you cure the tooth of its pain with all skill and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that supplies its place when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance, wherefore its extraction is excusable.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which is the painful tooth, for many times the pain deceives the patient and he thinks it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and then the pain does not cease until the sick and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operations of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which tooth is painful, and you know it well, then you must scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the tooth's gum on all sides, and then loosen it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, at first little by little until you loosen it completely.
And then apply strong, large forceps for good traction or firm grip, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold it well and it does not move, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not dislodged, take one of those instruments and place it under the tooth on all sides easily, and force its removal as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or eroded, then you must fill that crack with cloth and tighten it with a thin, pointed instrument so that it is not broken at the time of your traction on it with forceps.
And you must open carefully with scarification around all parts of the gum and take great care that you do not break it and leave a part of it behind, which would return to the patient causing an injury worse than the initial pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity regarding extraction, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses in men which are more difficult to cure, and leave their roots all behind, or some of them, or extract them along with some bones from the jaw, as we have witnessed many times.
Then, after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with warm wine or sour wine with salt.
And if bleeding occurs from this, as often happens, then crush some amount of alum and sprinkle it on the wound, and if this alum does not work, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and delicate tongs with which the tooth is initially grasped should be long in the gripping part, short in the handle, and thick, so that they do not bend when you have to work with them.
And let them be, as it were, of thick handle, so that when you tighten them they are not bent, [and] with short gripping ends.
And let them be made of Indian iron, carefully made with straight ends, and at the ends of these let there be teeth, of which the ones interlock with the others and tighten by strong compression; and sometimes the ends of these should be in the shape of the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* (that is, a stork), and they are of strong compression.
Chapter 31.
On the extraction of teeth and the removal of broken jawbones.
When you must extract a tooth that is broken and some portion remains, then you should place a cotton compress soaked in a softening agent on the area for one or two days until the area is softened; and then insert forceps, the ends of which are shaped like the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork – the shape of forceps:
Their ends should be made internally like a file or rasp.
And if it does not yield to you thus with these forceps or pincers, then you should dig around the root and uncover all the flesh and insert the instrument called an *atali*, that is, a small lever, the shape of which is this:
And let it be of short length, somewhat thick, and not too thin, lest it break.
Therefore, if by this the root is extracted, good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the shapes: the shape of the first instrument is a triangular end, which should have some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument is that it should be triangular and of a more slender end.
And you are already aided with this instrument having two hooks, of which this is approximately the following form: and with other instruments which precede in tooth extraction; for help is given with this instrument which resembles a large hook, and this is its form:
of the triangular, curved or short end, which should have some thickness, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
And know also that the instruments for molar teeth are similarly made to the other instruments.
But the wise and subtle artisan in the art finds for himself, according to what the operation and the diverse illnesses signify to him; for there are illnesses for which the ancients neither named nor devised a proper instrument, due to the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken or injured, then it should be treated in its place with what is convenient for it from among the instruments and forceps which we have described in the extraction of tooth roots, and help yourself with a splint, of which this is the form:
And let there be some thickness in it little by little, and let it be tightened or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone is completely set; and then restore the place with medicines convenient for this.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural alignment, their shape is flawed, and this is especially true for women, and most especially for venerable old women; therefore, you must consider: if a tooth is close to another tooth, and neither suture nor filing is possible, then extract it; but if it is not connected to another tooth, then you should incise it with an instrument of which this is the shape; and it resembles a boar's tusk and should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be done gradually over several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other teeth are damaged.
And if the protruding tooth is suitable for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of this shape:
And let the entire file be of Indian iron and very carefully made; and let it have very fine points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days carefully, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. Then smooth it at the end and polish it with some rasps.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is chipped and hurts the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and it is filled so that the tongue is not injured and speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
On the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and loosening from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated this with styptic medicines, and your treatment has not been successful, then to prevent them from falling out, they should be fastened with gold wire and not silver, because silver turns green and becomes powdery after a few days, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness as can fit between the teeth; and the method of binding is that you take the wire and pass double the length between two healthy teeth, then, making two turns of the wire between the broken teeth, whether one or more, until the wire reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving back to the point where you began, and tighten with your hand gently and skillfully until it is firm.
And let your tightening, with which you fasten the wire, be such that it does not slip from the roots of the teeth.
Then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and smooth those ends at each location, and tuck them away and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightly bound.
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and the method of straightening two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, thus as you see them bound:
And when it is done that one or two teeth return to their proper places, and they have grown back as we have described, and that they remain fixed.
And only a good and skilled craftsman does this.
And sometimes some cow bone is adapted, and it is made from them in the form of a tooth, and it is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is tightened as we have said, and it remains and serves in its place for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Of the incision of the ligament which occurs in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which is under the tongue is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs from a wound which has already healed.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, their head being in your lap; and lift their tongue, and then incise that nervous ligament according to its width until the tongue is freed from its constraint.
But if in that area there is any hardness or knotting, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you should make an incision and divide it along its width until you have cut the ligament and the knotting is dissolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the depth of the flesh and that you cut only superficially, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
