And then wash the site of the incision with warm water or with sour wine or with cold water; and then place under the tongue a linen cloth; that is a kind of sponge which the patient should keep under their tongue every night so that it does not heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess resembling a small tumor occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it is quickly enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting facing the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark or black in color, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancerous.
And if it is inclined towards white, and there is moisture in it, then place your hook there, and incise it with a fine spatula, and incise it completely; and if blood flows during your operation, then apply crushed vitriol on top until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completed it, until it is washed with wine and salt; and treat it with the other appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. On the extraction of an abscess from both ears, and from that which is located in the throat, and of other abscesses.
When a goiter develops in the throat, these are glands that are gathered together like cotton, which protrude outwards and are called "two ears," and when you treat them with what we have said in the previous section and it is not healed, then consider if the abscess is hard, of a dark color, and insensitive to touch; then do not touch it with an iron instrument.
And if it is red and the root is large, then do not touch it with iron because of the flow of blood; rather, leave it until it matures, and then you will extract it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then it is something that should be incised.
And the operation is that you should consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already matured completely through rest, or if it has diminished in some way; and then make the patient sit in front of the sun, with their head in your lap, and open their mouth.  The assistant, holding their hands, will press down on the patient's tongue using a very fine instrument, which is of this shape:
And it should be made of silver or bronze, and should be as thin as a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue with this and the abscess is shown to you, and you look at it carefully, then take an oncia [ounce, possibly referring to a type of surgical instrument or a measurement of ointment] and place it in the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can. Furthermore, if you pull out some material from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of this shape:
Furthermore, if you pull out some material from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of this shape: and it resembles forceps, but its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be made of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula, of which this is the shape: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner, and immediately after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water.
But if bleeding occurs, gargle with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar astringent substances until the bleeding is stopped, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those near the two ears, and incise those in the same way as I have said in the incision of the two sides equally.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat. This abscess was dark in color, relatively insensitive, and had already closed off her throat, so that the woman was breathing through a narrow passage.  I had already forbidden her to eat or drink water, and she would have died if she had lasted another day or two.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had created two channels through which it was discharging.
Therefore, I treated it with a fistula, and I inserted a probe into one of the openings and pulled it through. A large portion was extracted.  Then I incised the abscess from where it extended to the other opening and opened up its mouth, compressing her tongue. Then I placed the probe in the abscess.
And then I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water and then eat food.  I continued to incise the abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess formed in its place, which I incised until it was completely resolved.
Then I burned and cauterized her throat; this stopped the infection. Then I waited and did not know what God would do next about it.
Chapter 37.
Concerning the incision of a wen when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is abscessed and elongated, then it is called a perpendicular [abscess]; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a boil.
But when it is treated with what we have said in the section, and your treatment does not work, and you see that the already existing abscess is small and subtle, then you must incise the one that is round and aggregated; and if it is long, and is bloody, of a dark color, and the patient feels no sensation, then you must delay the incision, for in that case there is danger for the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner I have described, in its whiteness and length, make the patient sit and press their tongue with an instrument which follows its shape, and then place the hook in the uvula and pull it down, and incise it with one of the two instruments which are described in the incision of the tonsils.
And you must not incise it except what is adjusted to the natural limit without addition, for if you incise more, you will impair their speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of the tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use caution in cauterizing it without causing fear, and the method of cauterization is only with boiling water, and it is that you place the patient's head in your lap, and then press their tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned, and then take the boiling water which we have described in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with that live coal, and apply it neither thickly nor thinly, and with that fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the shape:
Let its extremity be a concavity in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on their side so that the saliva or mucus from their mouth runs out due to the internal medicine, so that nothing of it descends into their throat and harms them.
And then take your hand with the medicine, and press it on the uvula for half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried by the action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine, and insert the spoon with the cotton into a tube so that the cotton adheres to that tube, and do this as many times as necessary until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it until it dries and falls off after three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and after the cauterization, clean the area around the uvula with cotton soaked in vinegar, and cleanse with it what remains around it from the medicine, and wash it with cold water, and treat the outside with an embrocation, and the inside with gargles until it is healed.
And this method of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method more subtle than cautery or incision, for it is cured with fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, origanum, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile, and similar herbs, and combine all these ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them in sharp vinegar, and boil; and the pot should be well sealed with suitable clay, and in the middle of the pot there should be a hole, over which should be placed a concave instrument of brass, or made of silver or brass in this manner.
And insert the end into the patient's mouth so that the vapor rises to the uvula through the cannula, until the uvula is sufficiently fumigated.
And then repeat this many times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the appearance of an abscess, for it would aggravate the abscess too much, and you should not do this, but rather cauterize the declaration of the abscess.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a reed and attach a hen's egg shell to the end of it, so that the patient's mouth is protected, because the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is salutary.
Chapter 38.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and that which adheres in the throat and other causes.
Many times something adheres in the throat, whether a fish bone or some other thing.
Then it is necessary that you extract that which of those things is apparent, upon which the eye falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument, in front of the light, so that to you is made clear that which is in the throat.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, it is necessary that you make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very quickly the cause attached to the throat will be expelled by vomit; or that the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or take a piece of dry sea sponge, and tie that firmly with thread, and then let the patient swallow it, for when it arrives at the place where the thorns are and is extracted, then they are expelled. And moreover, take an eel, well tied with thread, and let it be inserted and quickly extracted; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly draw it out.
But if they are not expelled by those means which we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be thicker than a finger, and at its extremity let there be a concavity which you introduce into his throat with ease.
And he should have his head raised up, and be careful not to touch his throat bone, lest it harm him, and with that you push the bone or thorn, or that the physician does it with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself inserts it, because of his knowledge of the location of the attached cause, and pushes downwards, and pulls his hand up with the instrument, all this according to the quantity that is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and it does not work, then consider placing the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed their tongue with the instrument we have recommended to you.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with gentle, careful movements.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this as many times (until the leech is dead and detaches from the throat) and abstain the patient from water all that day.
Then take a vial full of cold water, and open his mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not swallow any of it and stir the water from time to time with your hand, for the leech immediately falls when it feels the water.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cyprus or with asafoetida through the instrument we recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall.
And another method of operation in the fumigation of it, is that you take a pot in which there are burning coals and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole, and the end of the instrument is fitted into that hole; and then direct the fumigation upwards; and let the patient put his mouth to the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall.
And if it does not fall, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and restrain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and do not drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is drawn from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps like this, except that there is some curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and the end of it is similar to a stork's beak, which is hollow, in which there is a roughness of filing, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and on their incision.
Abscesses are of many species, of many manners, according to the remembrance of them, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in perforation and incision of them by two methods, of which one is by the species of abscesses in themselves; and by what we understand of the humors.
And the second method is according to the locations in which they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and each of them has its own judgment for operation.
And moreover, of abscesses, there are some which should not be perforated except near maturation of the pus in them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which should be opened when they are raw and not matured according to their completion, such as abscesses that are near the joints, because when an abscess is near a joint, its being is prolonged until it consumes what is in its circumference, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore a cause of contraction of that member is made; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured, it will harm the principal member; or it is near the anus, therefore perforate that which is not mature; and it is not fitting to perforate it raw, with incomplete digestion, except that which is near the anus, so that its matter may not consume and be elongated to the inner parts of the anus, and a fistula be made, or that it be made in such a way that it may not heal. Moreover, you must know the time of perforation of the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when there is a lessening of the pain and the cessation of the fever, and also the diminution of the redness and of the pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know how to make the perforation fall in the place lowest on the abscess and from where the healing comes from the eminence.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and in the place of the tendons, of the cords of the nerves, of the arteries; and, lastly, in all equal places in which there is no reflection; for in those that are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you leave the perforation of it until its digestion is confirmed according to what has been said; for if you perforate it before that, the course of its pus and much filth is prolonged, and quickly its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation, or many incisions, according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which it is necessary that the point be curved and incised, when it is made like bread and is made in such a way that it is already ripe, like that which happens in cases of buboes and swellings.
And some of those are incised with a three-angled incision; and others are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And there are those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are administered, and similar ones.
But that which is not like the aforementioned superficial abscesses, you must perforate it with only a superficial or flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you must perforate it and do not worry about extracting all the pus at once; having extracted a part of it, then compress the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger part, and do this many times gradually until it is all expelled.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in decrepitude, because the animal spirit is often dissolved with the egress of the pus, and the patient dies very quickly; and you open them.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, you must clean the wounds and consider then if the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then you apply to it lint or old cotton; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you must place a wick in each hole until they meet.
And if in the abscess a part of the skin is severed or you have hollowed it out in the circumference, then you must fill it with old cotton or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have extracted splinters or roots, without moisture; and bind it until the third day; and then proceed with this and treat it with the ointments that are necessary until it is healed.
But if a flow of blood occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and sour wine in which you dip a linen cloth, and place this over the place of the blood flow many times.
But if the flow of blood persists, then you must apply the regimen and treatment and powders which I have recounted in many places in this our book and division.
But if it is in winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you should mix powders in warm wine and oil, and apply those to the areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders soaked in water and oil or with wine and oil, and all this should be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and clean it and apply to its treatment what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction I have already spoken of each abscess and in what manner is the way of its treatment in particular chapters.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which occur in the back of the head.
In the back of the head, small abscesses are found, and these are a type of node which contains membranes that are attached to their surfaces; and their appearance is varied.
And they are suppurative, and those contain a humidity similar to slime. And some of them contain a humidity similar to pus, and resembling them; and some of them are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, since the extraction of those does not cause contrary harm.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is appropriate for this, which is called a probe, until you know what they contain.
For if what it contains is humidity, then incise it with a longitudinal incision, a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin the incision from line B to line G.  Then, when the humidity is evacuated, you scrape that which contained the humidity, and do not leave any of it at all, for often it returns if any of it remains.
And then immerse cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with this and leave it there until the next day, for with this you corrode all that is left of that membrane.
And then repeat applying cotton with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if needed, until you are confident that no part of that membrane remains; then cure the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you incise it according to this form: with an incision having the form of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from all sides, and exert your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if the artery opposes you, then do what I recounted to you first.
And similarly proceed in a petrified abscess, in incision and cure as we have told you; and the incision on a petrified abscess is easier, because it has little blood and little humidity.
And sometimes some of these abscesses are in the head of some men, in which there is no humidity at all; the reason is that I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a deaf, rough, white stone, which could not be crushed, and if anyone were struck with it, it would hurt them.
But in other accidental abscesses in the head besides this, this is not the chapter, such as the abscesses that occur on the heads of children and behind the roots of the ears; however, the incision of all these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those from the lowest part of them so that the body may be relieved of the pus in the lowest parts.
And then treat those with what is suitable for that treatment.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42. Of the incision of scrofulas which happen in the neck.
Many times these abscesses occur in the neck and in the armpits and in the groin; and sometimes they happen in other parts of the body.
And those which happen in the neck, and many are engendered amongst them, and every scrofula of these is within its own proper tunic, and thus, as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, for of these some are stony, and of these some contain humidity, and of these some are malignant which do not obey cure.
Therefore, in that which you see of good disposition in the touching, of which the color of that which is manifest is near to the color of the flesh, and is softened on every part, and is not annexed to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, it behooves you to incise that with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body, from line G to line B, and scrape that from every part, and extend the lips of the flesh with hooks, or with many hooks if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract that little by little.
And it is most cautious that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the spatula not be very sharp, lest it injure you with the incision, or that it disturb the patient, and that you incise that which it is not necessary to incise; for if you should incise a vein or artery, and prohibit you from your operation, then place on the wound powdered vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound is softened and begins to be purulent. When the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then examine with your index finger if there is any cause of other small scrofulas, then incise those and cleanse them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, it is necessary then that you not incise that scrofula at its root. Beforehand, it is necessary that you tie it with strong doubled thread and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then cure it.
And if you incise the whole scrofula, then it is necessary that you adjust the two lips of the wound and sew it immediately as soon as there is no remaining superfluity.
But if you see that there remains superfluity because of the size of the scrofula, it is necessary that you intend to incise the root of it, and extract it and administer suture and what is said [above].
And for those scrofulas that contain humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and place the perforation so that it is the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound to be already cleansed, then you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the abscess of the accident within the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the abscess, but no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the inscription/writing of the discourse of those (people/ancients).
But in men having a swelling, the extraction of the abscess should be avoided, lest harm be done thereby, because all the veins and the major ones of the patient are there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth, throat, or ears, when the illness is not in the windpipe, it is necessary to use the incision of the abscess to avoid the destruction which is caused by perforation (or suppuration).
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the abscess below the three circles of the abscess circles, or four, with a small incision in width between two circles in quantity, so that the incision is in the skin, not in the cartilage.  This location is suitable for incision because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant.
And if the physician is fearful, then he should extend the throat's membrane with a hook, and then slit the membrane until he reaches the channel (windpipe), but avoid the blood vessels if he sees any of them.
Then incise the skin as we have recommended; and the incision of the windpipe is indicated by the inflammation which comes from it with what is broken from it, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Then, when the time has arrived in which perforation is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound's membrane and sew it alone without the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the sum of the discourse which we have recounted, the exposition is nothing other than that you see that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the throat of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and his breath is cut off, and some go to incise the epiglottis so that the patient breathes over the area of the wound through some respiration, and is saved from death.
And for that reason I order the wound to be left open until the malignancy of the disease is consumed; and its malignancy lasts for three days; and then I order the wound to be sewn, and cure it until it is healed.
And this which I have seen myself, is that a servant took a knife and put it in her throat and incised with it a part of the windpipe, and I was called to her cure, and I found the murmur as it is murmured by the dying, and I uncovered the wound and found the blood which had come out of the small wound.
Then I was confessed that she had not incised a vein or artery, and the wind was issuing from the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And nothing happened to the servant except hoarseness in the voice, and after some days she returned to a better state of health.
And now I say that in the incision of the tonsil there is no fear.
Chapter 44.
Concerning incision of an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess, named throat hernia, is a large abscess according to the complexion of the body, and it is common in women.
And it is of two types, for it is either natural or accidental.
And the natural type is not possible to cure.
And the accidental type is of two kinds, of which one resembles fatty nodes, and the other kind resembles an abscess which is caused by the knotting of an artery, and in this one there is danger. And one should not use iron on it presumptuously except in that which is small. And you should probe and investigate it with the finger; and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you should incise it like nodes and remove it with what contains it from the skin and  [remove] the little blister; and if not, then its whole being should be examined, and then treat the place with what is needed for healing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
On the incision of the types of nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division.
And it is necessary that I recount thus the difference between a node and an abscess, lest there be doubt.
Therefore I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and vehement pain until the boiling of the superfluity is settled; and putrefaction is complete, then the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that lion-colored fluid which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is formed like a blister, either larger or smaller, and they are of two types, for they are either fatty and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division.
Therefore it is necessary, when you arrive at the treatment of a node, that you extract it and investigate it at the beginning with an instrument which is called an intromes, according to how its form will appear in the chapter which is about this.
And the manner of investigating abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will turn your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the core.
And then place your hand according to the size of the abscess, and then extract the intromes and consider what has come out in its track.
And if the flowing humidity, whatever its color, is expelled, then you incise it with a superficial section as I have said in the other abscesses.
And if there is no humidity in the track of the intromes, then know that it is fat (or greasy).
And dry it with an incision having the form of a cross according to this form: Just as I have taught you, and suspend that with hooks, and peel the skin from all sides easily, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over that and extract that pus with the node.
But if it is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract that pus, and this often happens, and then you extract it in pieces until nothing remains of it.
Because if anything remains, little or much, the node will return accordingly.
And if you see that some small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and bind the wound, and place over it a hot poultice, and treat it with another treatment until it is healed.
And if it is a large node, then you sew the two lips of it and treat it with that which causes it to granulate (heal by forming new tissue).
But if a pulsating vein is contrary to it, or a non-pulsating one, then you cauterize and fill the place with crushed vitriol and leave it tightly bound for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood subsides; and then return to incising that which is rotten in the node.

Chapter 47.
On the treatment of men's breasts which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are inflamed due to excessive sleep; and then the breasts are inflamed, and resemble the breasts of women, and remain as pustular, whitish swellings.
And for one who has this, it is necessary that you incise those with a lunate incision, according to this form, from line B to line G, and then scrape away all the thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medicine, and then close the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the breast droops downwards and is softened by its size, as it happens in women, then let it be split in two its upper sides, with two incisions similar to a lunar figure, of which each is contained within the other, towards the end of each, so that the larger line contains the smaller according to this form, from line B and line G. And then scrape the skin which is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said of the suture and powders which are necessary in the healing until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what needs to be incised because of the patient's unease, and because of the flow of blood, then you must fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of the perforation of abscesses that form under the armpit.
These abscesses that form under the armpit are a kind of scrofula, hard, and containing a sac-like skin.
And some of these contain humidity; and those that contain humidity should be incised with a lunar incision according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all the pus within the abscess has been evacuated, then you fill it with old cotton and leave it there until the next day.
Then the cotton should be removed and it should be treated with a suitable ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the kind of nodes and scrofula, then you should incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula, similarly.
But if the wound is so extensive that it causes putrefaction, then apply cautery as we have previously described.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from an arrow or vein.
When an arrow is broken, and the remaining shaft is embedded, then often an abscess occurs.  The signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are due to an artery or vein are these: because when an abscess is due to an arrow, it is long and extends deeply into the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a hissing sound within it.
And that which is due to a vein is round on the surface of the body.
And incision of this abscess is dangerous, especially in that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large, therefore its treatment with iron should be avoided, and that which is in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
But concerning that which is caused by inflammation of the opening of the arrow wound, make a lengthwise incision in the shaft; and then widen the incision with hooks and around the arrow, and free it from the tissues until it is uncovered.
Then place a needle on it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the arrow with a double thread in two places—this method I have shown you for the extraction of two arrows that are in the temples.
Then pierce the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood that is in it comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
Then administer treatment in which powder is applied so that the ligaments may heal; and then treat it with ointments suitable for this until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess caused by a severed vein, then you should hold with your hand as much of the abscess as you can with the shaft, and then insert the needle under the place that you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread in it so that you can extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with a good ligature as I have told you in the ligature of a grape, in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that you will extract the thread, then insert the thread with that needle under the entire abscess below the incision of the first needle, and tighten the threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until, when what is inside it has come out, then you incise the superfluity of the shaft, and leave what is tied to it.
Then place on it a powder which you have submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1: Of the abscess which occurs due to the twisting of nerves.
Just as abscesses occur in arteries and veins, similarly abscesses occur in the nerves when they are struck or injured by excessive labor, and the like.
And it is most often found in the areas of the joints or the heel, or in any place where the joints are weakened.
And this abscess is serious, and is similar in color to the rest of the body, and is in the worst conditions when it is constricted with force, causing the patient to feel a kind of numbness.
And this abscess is not located in the front of the body, therefore it is below the ribs, and it is weakened in every part and does not extend to the front or back.
Therefore, that which is in the joints should not be invaded with iron, for contraction will quickly ensue.
But that which is in the head or forehead, finish it with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps and excise it from the roots.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and flay it, and then remove it; and close the wound with suture, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51.  (or Chapter LI, using Roman numerals)
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the abdomen (or belly).
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies that there are swellings which are called fungals by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and the lips of these are  turned inward (or inverted), and some of these are small, and some are very large.
And I have seen one, and in his belly were two swellings which were very similar to fungi, and there was no difference between them,  [being] white, with slender roots.
And the lips of these were inverted and split open, and humidity constantly flowed from them.
Therefore I incised them and found in one eighteen ounces of pus, and in the other six ounces.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the swelling is white, humid, with a slender root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and let cauteries be in your presence in the fire, for many times from the incision of it much blood is expelled; and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, beware of incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind the swelling with it, in the manner which we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease to tighten the lead greatly over the swelling, until it is severed and falls off by itself without work.
But if the swelling has a thick root, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the belly.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
And then care for it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to incise a swelling which is of a dark color, of little feeling, and of a hard consistency, for that is a malignant cancer.
And I bring to mind a crab in relation to this.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the treatment of a swelling of the umbilicus.
A swelling of the umbilicus is caused by many things; either by fissures of the skin which is over the belly, and a protrusion of the intestine or bowel as happens in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as has been said before, or by wind constricted there.
But if it is due to a fissure of the skin and a protrusion of the intestine, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the bowel, then its location is as we have recounted, with even greater variability, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and quickly returns; and it is easily replaced there, and is greatly increased by the intake of baths and strenuous work.
But if it is due to moisture, then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it grow.
And if it is due to blood, then the swelling appears with these signs, tending towards blackness.
But if it is due to rotten flesh, then the swelling is serious, large and hard, and is located in a specific area.
But if it is due to flatulence, then touching it is soft.
And the operation is that you consider then if the swelling of the umbilicus is from arterial blood, or venous blood, or from flatulence; then you must be prohibited from treating it, because in that there is fear and danger as I have taught you in the chapter in which I discussed swellings from arterial and venous blood.
And if the swelling of the umbilicus is due to the intestine or the hernia, then you must command the patient to hold their breath and lie down; and then mark with a probe around the entire umbilicus, and then command him to lie between your hands on his back, and then surround with a broad spatula the circumference of the umbilicus over the area you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the swelling upwards with a large inch; and then tie it around with strong thread or with a bandage and tie it in a proper manner.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling over the ligature and insert your finger into that opening and search for the intestine; and if you have found it and secured it with the ligature, then you carefully extract and push the intestine into the inside of the abdomen.
And if you have found the hernia, then grasp it with forceps; and incise its excess.
But if it is against your practice to operate on an artery or vein, then circumcise that spot, and then return to your procedure; and take two needles and thread them with strong thread and insert the two needles into the circle which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, making them penetrate; and then tighten the swelling in four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the protruding flesh dries and falls off by itself; or incise it when it is thinned and dried, and then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the umbilicus is from flesh growing there or from a humidity, then it is necessary that you empty the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or humidity which you find there, and then treat it with what is appropriate and which will consolidate the area.
Chapter 53.
Concerning the treatment of cancer.
We have already discussed, in the division, the types of cancer and in what manner it is possible to treat it with medicines, and that it is to be avoided to treat it with iron so that it is not ulcerated; and we have said that cancer is generated in the manner and caution of its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as cancer which is in the breasts or in the groin and similar members, to extract all of it, and especially when it is small and beginning.
But when it is old and large, it is not appropriate to apply oneself to it, for I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone else who has succeeded in doing so.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you proceed and let the patient bleed, purging some black bile, and then bleed him, if a manifest repletion is found.
Then prepare the patient with a preparation in which he is situated for the operation, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then scrape thoroughly from all sides with a good, sharp curette until no trace of its roots remains. And let the blood flow, and do not staunch it lightly, and press the area, and wipe away the thick blood completely with your hand or with whatever instrument is available to you among your instruments.
But if you find excessive bleeding from a vein incision or artery, then cauterize it until the flow of blood ceases, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a standard chapter numbering system)
On the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the ascites type, and iron is not applied to the tympanites type or the anasarca type, for that is mortal.
And when you have treated this tympanitic type of dropsy with medicines and your treatment is not effective, then consider, for if the patient has already acquired debility, or has another illness along with this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain and similar things, then beware of treating them with iron, for there is danger in that.
But if you see the patient with exuberant strength, and with no other illness but dropsy alone, and neither a child nor an old person, then the manner of operating on them is that you lift the patient between your hands, and the attendant gently presses their belly with their hand, driving the water towards the lower part of the pubis, and then take a pointed trocar in this manner:
Let it be two-sided, sharp and long, with a sharp point like a trocar; but let there be some shortness in it, little by little, so that in the operation with it you do not pierce the intestine, and thus injure it.
And then consider if the origin of the dropsy is from the intestines, for then it is necessary that you make the incision at a distance of three fingers from the navel towards the lower parts, directly over the pubis.
But if the origin of the dropsy is due to liver disease, then your incision should be three fingers to the left of the navel.
And if its origin is from the spleen, then your incision should be on the right.
And it is not fitting that your incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie, lest the superfluities flow to that weak place.
And then perforate the entire peritoneum with the instrument, and then introduce it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the peritoneum and the omentum, and you should separate that; if [the separation is] the amount of a fingernail or thereabouts.
And then perforate the omentum until the spatula reaches the empty place, which is the location of the water. And then extract that and introduce an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or tin, to which, in its lowest part, there is a small hole, and on its sides there are three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its incised extremity is shaped like a feather, like this:
And at its uppermost extremity is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then that immediately rests on the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in a moderate amount, because if you evacuate more than is necessary at once, the patient will quickly die from the dissolution of their animal spirit, or they will suffer syncope, which is near death.
But evacuate according to the quantity of its virtue and according to what the patient's condition, the virtue of their pulse, and their overall well-being signify to you.
And then extract the instrument, and retain the water, the reason being that it is immediately retained by the chain which holds the hole that is above the cup, which I have described to you how to perforate in that manner.
And then repeat the procedure with the instrument the next day if you see the patient tolerating it, and extract a small quantity of water, and do this for several days cautiously, and guard yourself against error so that only a little water remains.
But if you are concerned about the patient, and leave a large quantity of water, then treat them with burial in warm sand, and induce sweating in a bath, and expose them to the sun for a long time; and treat them with drying medicines until they are healed.
And cauterize the stomach, the spleen, and the liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 54.  (Assuming a chapter numbering system starting at I or 1)
Concerning the treatment of children who are born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born from its mother's womb and the opening of its urethra is not perforated (or is narrow, or is not in its proper place).
Therefore, it is necessary to treat it with perforation as soon as it is born, using a very fine probe, in this manner:
Then place a thin lead pin in the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when it wants to urinate, it should be encouraged and will urinate; and then the pin should be replaced.
And if you do not place the lead [pin], it is possible that the urine which flows over the area will not allow it to close.
But that in which the opening is narrow should be treated with a lead pin, as we have said, for several days until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its proper place, that is to say, those in whom the opening is below the end of the penis, so that they cannot urinate unless they elevate the penis with their hand,  it is not a generative issue, because the sperm cannot reach the womb in a straight line, and this is a very protracted illness.
And the manner of the operation is that the patient should be positioned on his back, and then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with a strong pull, and incise the head of the penis at the location of the foreskin with a sharp scalpel, like an incision with a feather, or that you shave a portion so that the middle of it becomes similar to the head of the penis, and that the opening falls in the middle as it should.
And be careful in your operation of blood flow, for this often happens; and staunch the blood with something, and tend to the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Concerning phymosis, which occurs in the prepuce at the head of the penis, and of corruption, blackness, and adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis.
Many times this phymosis occurs on the penis and is a fleshy excrescence.
And of these, one is malignant and another is non-malignant.
For the non-malignant type, you should suspend it with a fine hook and excise it until you have removed it completely; then apply to the wound a pledget soaked in Egyptian ointment; and after that, treat it with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
And if the phymosis is malignant, of a livid color, then you should apply cautery near its incision and its root.
But if the phymosis is on the prepuce of the glans penis of an uncircumcised man, and one part of the phymosis is inside the prepuce and the other part is outside, then you should remove the phymosis that is inside first, until it is consolidated; and then you treat the outside part, because if you treat both together, the prepuce may be damaged.
And if you see in the genitals and prepuce blackness and corruption, then you should remove everything that is already blackened and beginning to corrupt or is already corrupt; and then apply to that area a poultice of honey, which is mixed with finely ground bark of pomegranate and crushed and sifted chickpea flour.
And then treat it with another suitable treatment until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
Because cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, that is, for bleeding and for a wound when it is corrupted/infected.
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you should insert a lead cannula into the meatus of the prepuce so that the patient can urinate through it.
In the adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis (and this adhesion occurs only in one whose prepuce is healthy, and circumcision has not been performed; and when adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess) it is necessary that you scrape with a blunt instrument until the adhesion is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate these exquisitely, then you should scrape with a sharp instrument some part of the head of the penis and the prepuce, and this is because the prepuce, when it is thin, is very easily perforated by its slight thinness.
Then separate the prepuce from the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth, which has been soaked in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
And then let it be treated with styptic wine until it is consolidated (healed).
Chapter 57.
Chapter 57: Of the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to those circumcised.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and since it is properly done on children, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest path leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience, which is why I have found most artisans and barbers to use circumcision and cleansing with a lancet and with forceps; and they use ligature with thread and incision with the nail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with forceps, with ligature with thread than cleansing with the nail, because many times the foreskin retracts further up, because the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, and it is easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with cleansing which is done with an awl, and is digital, which shoemakers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not assured, because it easily enters the hole.
But in the operation with a nail, it easily becomes rusty and corrupts your operation. And the foreskin of the child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need of cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with forceps and ligatures with thread, the goodness, experience reveals to me, because the forceps are of proportional incision, for this reason that the sharpness which is above is thus like the sharpness which is below; and thus as you press your hand, two sharpnesses are proportioned, and you incise according to a comparison at one time; and compression of the thread is made with a figure containing the foreskin of the penis from every part, with which no error at all happens.
And the manner of the first operation is that you make the child, especially if it is one of those that understands a little, stay still, so that you do nothing but tie the thread to its penis alone, and leave it there until the next day.
And then make it happy in every way possible that it receives understanding. And then make the child lie still between your hands, prepared and not at all fidgety; and hide the forceps in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them or any other of the instruments.
And then place your hand on his penis and blow on the foreskin, and pull it until the head of the penis is exposed, and then cleanse it of whatever dirt is clinging to it.
The shape of the forceps for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let the one extremity be non-cutting and the other cutting, so that the head of the nail does not come out, and let the length of the two sharpened points be like the length of two handles equally. (This describes the design of surgical forceps;  "nail" likely refers to a pin or rivet connecting the two parts of the instrument.)
But the error in circumcision is that you too quickly remove the whole, or part of, the inner foreskin below the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you stretch that (skin) with your fingernail before the suppuration of the place and that you incise it equally.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with tweezers and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the inflamed penis, then you leave it until the hot abscess has subsided, and then carefully scrape it, and incise it as you see fit.
And be careful of the head of the penis, for that will harm him.
And then treat it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised beyond measure, and obeys the upper parts, this will not harm him.
And furthermore, treat it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
Concerning the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction from a stone, or by congealed blood, or by pus, or by a fleshy growth, and similar to those things we have said concerning the methods of treatment in the division.
And if it is not dissolved by what we have said, and the cause is vehement upon the patient, then it is necessary to administer extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver and be subtly long and concave, like a bird's quill, with a slender length similar to a palm and a half, to which is a thin tube at its head.
And the manner of drawing the urine by this means is that you take a doubled bladder and tie at its end wool or cotton with a good binding; and introduce the ends of the bladder into the lower part of the syringe, and cut with scissors if any excess of wool remains, but introduce it into the tube forcefully. Then grease the syringe with butter and egg white.
And make the patient sit on a chair (or stool,  "sella" can mean either), and rub their bladder and penis with moist oils, olive oil and rose water.
Then gently introduce the syringe until it reaches the root of the penis; and then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; and then push the syringe inside that; and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and this indicates to the patient that it has reached the void, and it is not done according to this order, but the meatus, which goes in a tortuous way.
Then extend the bladder with the wool little by little, because the urine will soak the wool.
Then extract it and perform the urine cure, and use a syringe, do not cease to do this and use a syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a clamp and with similar instruments which are suitable for this.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or congealed blood in it, or putrefaction is constricted, and you wish to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument which is called a clamp, and this is its form:
except that at its extremity it is blunt, that is, closed. And in it there are three holes: two on one side, thus twice.
In the beginning, there is a concave place, in which that which is to be injected is placed, and it should be according to the quantity that fills it, without addition, until when something is drawn from the humors with it, it is drawn, and when it is expelled, with that it is drawn, and when it is expelled long, as the clamp does with which naphtha is poured in sea battles.
Therefore, when you want to draw humors from the bladder, insert the extremity of the clamp into the humor, and draw with it that which is pushed to the upper parts, because the humor is drawn into the concavity of the clamp.
And then insert the extremity of the clamp into the urethra as we have described for the syringe; and then expel the humor with that which you expel, because that humor immediately reaches the bladder, and the patient feels it.
The form of the subtle catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is its form:
And let it be made of silver or tin, of which the uppermost end should resemble a small feather, and below it a slender tube.
And then take a goat bladder, and place in it the liquid that you wish to inject into the bladder, and then tie it between two with a strong double ligature, and warm that liquid over the fire.
And then insert the tip of the catheter into the urethra.
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid with gentle pressure until the patient says that the liquid has reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it; and perforate it throughout its roundness; and then insert strong thread into those holes, so that it is wrapped around; and then attach the circle in such a way that it is joined together as a suture, after you put in it what you want of liquids, oils and waters.
And then you tie that to the instrument and do with it as you have done with the bladder, and express with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have already mentioned in the division the types of stone and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and a stone generated in the bladder, and the signs of it.
And we have spoken of the stone in which [one performs] an operation by hand and incision.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating that with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that the stone generated in the bladder mostly comes to children; and of its signs it is that the urine which is expelled from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs his penis, and often cries, and is restless; and with that comes forth the anus in many of those [children].
And the healing of children from this [condition] is easier while they have reached fourteen years; and it is difficult cure in the elderly. But in young people it is milder because it is among them [i.e.,  the condition is less severe in young people compared to the elderly].
And of that [stone] which is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is contrary to that [i.e., the cure is more difficult].
Therefore, to arrive at the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning that we purify the patient with a clyster which removes all the phlegm which is in his bowels; for that phlegm sometimes prevents the extraction of the stone because of its obstruction.
And then the patient should be taken with his feet up and massaged and shaken gently so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or that it sometimes jumps from another location.
And then make that [patient's] body between your hands prepared, and his hands should be below his thighs, so that the whole bladder is made to decline downwards.
And then search for it and all of that outside.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space, then you should immediately take care of it with an incision.
And if it does not completely descend from your touch, then it is necessary that you anoint your index finger of your left hand with oil, if the patient is a child, or the middle finger if he is a fully grown young man, and introduce it into his rectum. And search for the stone until it is under your finger. And return it little by little into the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards to the place where you made the incision.
And command the assistant to press on the bladder with her hand; and command another assistant to extend the genitals upwards with her right hand, and with her other hand support the perineum which is below the genitals, near the place where the incision is made.
And then introduce a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: and incise that which is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be above the stone, and your finger be in the anus, and let a transverse incision be made, so that the external incision is wide and the internal narrow, according to the size that the extraction of the stone allows, and no larger, for you will easily compress with your finger, which is in the anus, the stone against the incision, and it will be expelled without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and rough, wherefore their extraction is made difficult by this.
And of those that are like a gland, and are round, the extraction is easily done.
Therefore, if it is one which is angular and rough, adjust it in the fissure.
And if it is not thus expelled, then it is necessary that you push on it, or that you press on it with a suitably shaped instrument, the end of which is like a file with which you press on the stone, and it does not escape from it; or that you introduce under it a slender instrument with a curved end.
But if you cannot [remove it] in this way, then you enlarge the hole a little, and if you see a flow of blood, staunch it with crushed vitriol.
But if the stone is more than one, then at the beginning you apply your hand to the mouth of the bladder, and then incise it; and then gently push it; and similarly do if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is ignorance to incise it with a large incision, for two things happen to the patient: either he dies or he suffers continuous urinary incontinence, because the place is not completely consolidated.
But administer its expulsion until it is ejected, or work on breaking it with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Then, when you have completed your operation, fill the wound with aloe and dragon's blood and bind it. And place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be calmed.
Then lay the patient supine on his back, and do not untie the bandage until the third day.
And when you have untied that, bathe the place with water and plenty of oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and diabazilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound you find a hot, burning, corrosive abscess, similar to blood frozen in the bladder, and because of it urine is suppressed (and this is known by the passage of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains it provokes corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and with any other suitable remedy, doing what is necessary for its healing until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the entire treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is lodged there, and the flow of urine is obstructed, then treat it with what I recommended before resorting to incision, because many times because of this treatment incision is required; and I have already proved this, which is that you take a polished iron borer, of which this is the form:
of triangular end, sharp, set in wood.
Then take gall and bind it to the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the borer reaches the stone. And turn the borer with your hand in that stone little by little, and you will enlarge the perforation until you make it penetrate from the other side; for the urine will immediately be released.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outside of the penis, because that is broken, and it is expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this cure is not suitable for you for some reason that prohibits it, then bind one gall below the stone, and another gall above the stone. Then incise over the stone in that penis between the two ligatures. And then extract it. Then untie the ligatures and clean the congealed blood which is formed in the wound.
And the ligature of gall below the stone is not necessary except so that it does not return to the bladder, and the other ligature above, because when the gall is untied near the extraction of the stone, the hernia returns to its place and closes the wound.
And for this it is necessary for you, when you tie the gall above, that you elevate the hernia to the upper parts, or that it return through your accomplishment, and thus close the wound as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
Stones are rarely generated in women.
But if a stone is found in one of them, then its cure is difficult, and prohibited in many ways.
One reason is that women are easily made virgins (implying the difficulty of accessing the stone due to the hymen).
And the second is that you will not find a woman, be she chaste or married, who willingly exposes herself to a physician.
The third is that you will not find a woman who knows this art well, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for a stone in women is far from the stone itself, requiring a deep incision, and this is dangerous.
But if necessity compels you to this, then you must find a female physician who is skilled, and they are rarely found.
But if you are deprived of that, then seek a skilled and subtle male physician, or find a matron well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who has some knowledge of this art.
Therefore, have that person present, and command them to do everything you order, from the initial examination for the stone; the reason for this is that if the woman is a virgin, she should place her finger in her rectum and search for the stone.
Therefore, if she finds it,  press it down with her finger, and then instruct her to incise it.
But if she is not a virgin, then instruct the matron to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder and compressing it with good pressure.
Then, if she finds it, she should gently push it from the opening of the bladder downwards, using the sum of her strength until it reaches the root of the thing.
And then incise it near the junction of the vagina and the root of the thing, wherever it is situated, and center the stone in that area, and her finger should not be removed from the stone while compressing it downwards.
And the incision should be small at first; and then insert a probe into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then adjust the incision according to the size that you know the stone will be extracted from it.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches.
Therefore you should know how many types there are of them, so that you can signify that which you wish.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the place and leave it there for an hour until the bleeding stops, and then return to your operation until the stone is removed.
And make sure you prepare the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you are aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been incised, then you should apply powder to the place and bind it tightly with powders and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will quickly die. And then treat the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the bleeding has subsided after a few days, and the place has healed, you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of humidity in the white sheath which is below the scrotum containing the testicle, and is called the sheath.
And sometimes it is in the proper membrane which nature prepared for it in the part of the testicle, so that it seems that there is another testicle; and this is the scrotum between the white sheath that we have mentioned, and this is nothing but a swelling.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it happens due to a blow inflicted on the genitals.
And this humidity has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or red, or black and foul-smelling, or watery white, and this is what is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white sheath which we have mentioned, then the swelling is round, tending slightly towards length, like the shape of an egg, and it surrounds the testicle, because the humidity contains it on all sides.
And if the humidity is in the proper membrane, then the swelling is round in the part of the testicle and therefore one believes it is another testicle.
And if the humidity is between the cord of the genitals and the white sheath, then it falls below the feeling/sensation.
And if you want to know the color of the humidity, then you test the color of the humidity in the swelling with a lancet whose shape precedes it; and according to what has emerged in the trace of the lancet, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to its treatment with iron [cautery], then it is necessary that we order the patient to be phlebotomized if that is possible for you, and you see his body full [of blood].
Then let him be supine on his back on something a little raised, and place many cloths under him.
Then sit on his left side, and command the assistant to follow on his right side, who will extend his rod [likely a probe or similar instrument] to one of the two sides, to the cord of the genitals and to the part of the navel of the belly.
Then take a broad spatula and split the cord of the genitals in the middle lengthwise almost to the pubic bone.
And let the incision be made straight and equally distant from the line that divides the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the included white fat.
And scrape that [fat], and observe, and beware lest you incise that; and your scraping with which you scrape it, let it be suspended somewhat, and more subtly scrutinize with the scraping according to the quantity possible for you.
Then perforate the water-filled fat with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the fat to the upper parts; and do not touch the cord of the genitals within, and incise the fat in whatever manner is possible for you. And incise it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, for if you do not exquisitely scrutinize its incision, it is not ensured that the water will not return.
But if the matter proceeds from outside its cord during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the fat, return it inside, and then join the two lips of the cord of the genitals with suture, and then treat it like other wounds until it is healed.
And if you find the testicle corrupted by another illness, then you should tie the vessels which are on the spermatic cord, and extract the testicle, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the fluid is gathered in two separate parts, then know that there are two hernias; therefore, incise that part in the same way that you did the other; and if it seems to you that it is one operation, then you may perform it.
And then you should wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place on top more wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread this over the genitals and the area of the abdomen; and place on top folded cloths and powders, and bind those on top with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then two cloths should be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and the ends should be made with which it is bound with cotton or damp wool, and they should be added with a tight structure in the waistband of the breeches, and one of the ends should go from the buttocks to the groin, and the second should go to the part of the scrotum, and two ends over the two thighs and two other ends over the two knees, and the entire binding should be adjusted in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and that you incise with it the skin of the genitals, and it is ignited as we have recounted until the white, fluid-containing cyst is revealed, and then you take a cautery of this shape: and it resembles the Greek letter.
And then perforate the cyst with it, and ignite it until all the moisture is expelled.
And then stretch the cyst with tweezers, and scrape with tweezers, and scrape with a thin, ignited cautery; and incise it as much as you can until you eradicate all of it; and be careful that the fire does not touch the egg (testicle).
And know that this operation is safer and involves less blood loss, and is better than an operation with an incision.
But if an abscess forms contrary to expectation during the incision or cauterization, or there is a flow of blood, then you must cure whatever is contrary to expectation with the treatment that is appropriate for it, as has been said.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I have recommended; and this is when you are certain, through what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should make the patient sit on some raised seat; then perforate the abscess at its lowest part with a broad spatula, and place the perforation along the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough that all the water flows out.
Then place a cloth over the area and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water can drain out. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is an obstruction to the outflow of water, then you should perforate it: and this is done only because the white pannicle in the opening of the wound creates an obstruction and prevents the exit of the water; then you should put into the wound a tool with which water is extracted from dropsies, the form of which has been described before; or apply a small incision to the obstruction in the wound.
