And know that the water will return, and will accumulate again within six months or thereabouts; and when it has accumulated, then it should be perforated in the same manner, for the patient will thus pass their days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
Know that in the incision of this hernia there is fear of loss leading to death in the worst cases; therefore, to me it seems better to leave it alone and let it be saved by that means.
And I am remembering the operation on that [hernia] and the types of abscesses.
Therefore, I say that abscesses originate in the bodies of which the genitals are composed, and this is caused by many things: either by an acute, sharp superfluity which is poured into the genitals, or by percussion (a blow).
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly is a stony abscess formed, of which the color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by a knotting of the arteries, and by the inflammation of these as it is perceived in its appearance, or by inflammation of the veins.
But that which is from inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we compress it with our fingers; and it should not be touched.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any means through compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that an incision is made on this type [of abscess] and on the other tissues; and it is that you incise the tunic of the genitals, and then extend the testicle upwards, and extract it from the white sheath, and free the suspensory ligament, and ligate it, and incise the suspensory ligament after you have freed it from all parts of the testicle.
Therefore, if the testicle is consolidated with those inflamed tissues, then you must extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion to any part of the sheath, or to that which is between the vessels, then you must free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh at the site of the adhesion which is behind, then you must incise all that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Then, when your operation is completed, fill the wound with wool infused in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with the other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of a hernia which is of the scrotum.
A scrotal abscess is a swelling enveloped by some covering similar to a boil, with softening of the testicles, and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, one must fear that which precedes it [i.e., the cause of the illness].
However, we must describe the operation in that case, as the first physicians did.
And I say that you should make the patient sit on a raised seat; and then push up the suspensory ligament of the testicles towards the lower parts; and then hold the skin of the genitals with your fingers, along with the vessels that are close to the groin; and let another assistant hold those [parts] as you do, and extend them with firm extension.
And then incise with a broad, sharp knife using a transverse incision directly over the vessels until the vessels are exposed.
And then scrape away all the parts as we have told you in the extraction of arrowheads which are in the temples.
And then insert into those [vessels] a needle with double thread, and tie it at the first of the places where you made the incision, and tie it again at the other end. And then incise those [vessels] in the middle with a straight incision along the length of the body, and extract what is gathered there of fetid and corrupted humors.
And then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds in which you want healing to be engendered so that the vessels that nourish one of the two genitalia do not fall, for that is not harmful.
But if you cut all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed or that the testicles be tied off along with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they wither, or that they are tied off so that there is no nourishment for them.
Chapter 65.
On the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen from the inguinal rings of the abdominal muscle, causing the intestines to descend through that rupture.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these two types occur for many reasons: percussion, jumping, shouting, lifting something heavy, or similar.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, is that it happens gradually over a long time, and not suddenly, and it is an inguinal abscess towards the groin because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, is that at the beginning it causes sudden, intense pain; and it is an abscess appearing differently under the pubic bone, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and its body in the parts outside the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" is likely a corruption or dialectal variation of a word related to the omentum; a precise modern equivalent is difficult to determine without further context.)
And sometimes it is caused by flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and it is retained there, and this causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of these types of illness with iron [presumably referring to cautery or other surgical instruments], caution is needed, because cauterization should be avoided in that area.
And the method of operating on this is that you instruct the patient to reduce the intestine with his hand to the inside of its cavity if it obeys its return, and then it should be pressed on his neck between your hands, and raise his legs.
And then stretch the skin which is the skin of the testicles upwards, and incise the skin of the testicles completely lengthwise; and then place two hooks into the lips of the incision as you need to open the incision with them.
And if the incision, according to the amount that is possible for you, allows the egg [presumably referring to a hernia or testicle] to be expelled.
And then scrape away that skin which is under the skin of the testicles until the hard white skin is uncovered on each side.
Then you insert your finger into what is the testicle, into what is between the white skin, which is under the skin of the testicle, and the second skin, and break up with it the adhesion that is in the back part of the testicle.
And then gently pull the skin of the testicles outwards with your hand, and with this, stretch the white skin upwards with your left hand, and raise the testicle with the skin to the edge of the incision; and tell the assistant to pull the testicle upwards and you dissolve the adhesion that is behind with a complete solution [likely referring to a complete separation].
And check with your fingers that there is no intestinal matter enveloped in the hard white skin. But if you find any such matter, then push it back into the abdomen below.
And then make an incision in the area under the skin of the testicles, through which incision blood and pus will flow, just as we have recounted in what we have said.
And then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have described (and sometimes the white skin is cauterized, which we mentioned near its incision to prevent blood flow, because this often happens) and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed with warm water, and apply that until it falls off as we have said.
And then treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Of a Windy Hernia (or,  Of a Ventosity Hernia).
In this hernia, I have not seen anyone who has dared to treat it with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done in the same way as we have recounted in the hernia that is with wine; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on that side with ease; and then dry the middle area around the abscess with that which opens it, until the vessels fall away.
And cure the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the rupture that is made in the groin.
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and it swells the area.
And do not allow the genitals any cause of the intestines in the genitals; and if they do, it is little; and it returns at all times.
But if the time with this is prolonged, a fissure is made in the sheath until the intestine descends into the sheath; and this happens because of the extension of the sheath which is in the groin as we have said, which causes the sheath to be stretched, and then it is softened and the place is suppurated and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery as has been described previously.
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner:  the patient lies on his back between your hands, and then dry the place of the rupture from the swollen abscesses with a cut across the width according to the amount of three fingers.
And then tighten the white sheath that is under the testicle until it is uncovered.
And then take the iron (cautery) and place it on the prominent part of the sheath, and press it against the front of the abdomen.
And then sew the two prominent places on the end of the sheath's iron, and join one of those to the other with a suture.
And then remove the end of the iron, and do not incise the sheath completely, nor extend the wound, nor do anything else like that, as I will make known to you in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat it with the treatment for wounds, and if you have incised the veins, clean it and repair the wound until it is consolidated; because the sheath is contracted and the prominence of the rupture is not adjusted.
And cauterization is better in this place, because it is closer to health (i.e., more likely to promote healing).
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the swelling of the testicles.
Many times the swelling of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and its appearance is damaged.
Then it is necessary for the one who is charged with his cure to make the patient lie down according to his condition, and incise all the swelling which is softened above the natural swelling; and then that he join the two lips with a suture.
And if you wish, sew up initially the excess of the softened swelling with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and then treat it with the cure for other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
Concerning castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, but because of this I am required to remember it in this book; and its remembrance is necessary only in two ways, one of which is so that it is within the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it, that he may know the treatment when it occurs; and the other way is that many times we have need of the castration of other animals for our benefit from them, such as of horses, and of goats, and of pigs, and of similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by abrasion, or by incision.
But in that which is done by abrasion, the method of operation is that we should immerse the animal in warm water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. And then grasp them with your hand until they are relaxed and do not appear tense to the touch.
But in castration done by incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the scrotum of its testicles with your left hand.
And then tie off the suspensory ligament, and incise each testicle with a single cut until both testicles are freed.
And then incise them after you have skinned each one. And do not leave any substance on those of the cyphus except the subtle cyphus which is on the vessels.  (Note: "cyphus" here likely refers to a specific medical term, possibly related to a type of bandage or dressing, that is not readily translatable without further context).
And this manner of castration is better which is done by crushing, because strong crushing leaves no substance of the testicles by which the animal desires copulation.
And then care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is according to two manners: one is that which appears as if there were space in the scrotum of testicles, in that which is between two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva in which there is hair.
And sometimes from that area urine flows, which is in the scrotum of the testicles.
But in women, it is one type: and it is that the vulva of the woman above the pubis is like two testicles of a man, somewhat diminished at the outer part, like the prepuce of a man and two testicles.
And the cure is of three types: two for men and one type for women. And it is necessary that you incise those tightened flesh until the impression of that [problem/growth] is removed, and then that you cure those with the treatment of other wounds.
And in the second type of men, from which urine flows which is in the scrotum of the testicles, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Of swelling and prominent flesh due to causes in women.
A slight swelling is usually caused by natural reasons until it is stressed and made worse; and sometimes it is magnified in some women to such an extent that it is spread like in men and interferes with intercourse.
Then you must hold the excess swelling with your hand or with bandages and incisions.
And do not overdo the incision, especially near the root, so that bleeding does not occur.
And then treat it with wound treatment until it is healed.
But a birthmark is flesh that grows in the womb of the mother until it fills it, and quickly protrudes outwards resembling a tail (and for this reason some first called this "tailed disease"); therefore, we must incise it as we incise a swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of sciatica.
Sciatica is when the woman's vagina is not perforated, or the opening is small. And this is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a preceding illness, and that is caused either by insufficiently formed flesh or by a subtle or thick scar tissue.
And it is formed either in the front of the mother, or in her sides, or in her upper part, or in her lower part, and it prohibits coitus and conception and childbirth, and it strongly inhibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by examination of the womb; if the obstruction is apparent, it is profound; but if it is not apparent, then you search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin scar and is close to the two labia, then you cut and break it up.
And you place powders on the two labia, and then place your index fingers of both hands together.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then forcefully spread the two labia until that thin scar is broken and the obstruction is opened.
And then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area, and keep it moist all day so that the incised area does not heal.
But if the scar is thick and dense, then you should incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to an abscess, then open it with a lancet and incise it.
And have with you medicines that stop bleeding without biting, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and enses [this word may be a misspelling or a less common term; its meaning in this context is uncertain, requiring further research into medieval medical texts], mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep it there for a few days and apply some dry poultice.
And then it should be treated with another healing method until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the natural birth of other flesh [likely referring to a growth or tumor], and then it is necessary to incise in this manner. But if this is due to a cancerous abscess, to the cancerous abscess which is in the breast, no counter-irritant [or harmful treatment] should be applied in any way with iron.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and a boil or red swelling that occurs in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a dilation of the openings of many veins from which much blood constantly flows; and when they are old hemorrhoids, they become boils [or swellings].
And I have already mentioned in the division the types of these and their signs, and I will say thus which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and boils, when they are in the mother's womb and do not appear externally, then in those cases no treatment should be done with iron [i.e., cautery or surgical instruments].
But those which are at the orifice of the womb, upon which one feels [them], those are the ones that are cured [or treatable].
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cool place and then extend the boil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them to the roots.
And then sprinkle on those [wounds] some powder to stop the flow of blood, from among the incisive powders without biting [properties], such as acacia and similar ones.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place them in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then have her sit in a decoction of sumacs and pomegranate bark and gallnuts and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this and apply it to his back.
And if the blood is drawn off, it is good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice or in comfrey juice or in wild-carrot juice with diluted wine and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointment until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Concerning the perforation of the rectum, an accident in the mother.
Many kinds of abscesses happen in the mother, such as cancer, and stony abscesses, and ulcerations, and buboes, and erysipelas, and hemorrhoidal fistulas, and fissures, and boils, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses and their types and their signs, and their treatments, in the previous section/division.
And it is fitting that I speak in this treatise of the hot abscess which occurs in the mother, for it is among the abscesses that require cauterization, and if you wish to know in what manner its operation is performed with iron.
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute with throbbing and phlegm and fever, and sometimes it reddens and if you feel it there, then do not attempt its perforation, and treat it with things that promote maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms subsides.
And then you should make the woman sit on a stool that has two seats; and she should lean on her elbows and adjust her feet to the lowest part of the abdomen, and her thighs should be spread, and she should place her arms under her knees, and bind each one with a suitable bandage.
And then the midwife should stand on the right side and provide the instrument with which the orifice of the womb is opened, the form of which will be described in the relevant passages.
And it is necessary, when you wish to use this instrument, that you place with the ray the neck of the woman's womb, and that which of the instrument is inserted into the womb should be no more than the neck of the womb and harm the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the neck of the womb, then you should apply powders to the texture of the neck of the womb so that the entry of any instrument into the neck of the womb is prevented.
And it is necessary that you place the turning instrument from the upper side and that the person administering it holds the instrument and turns the turning instrument until the orifice of the womb is opened.
And when the wound appears, and it is touched with the hand and is soft and moist, then it is necessary that you dry that wound with a clean cloth.
And when the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a wick submerged in rose oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the caulking in the section in the neck of the womb.
And place outside the womb and on the vulva clean wool submerged in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then let it be treated for about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed so that it is cleansed with water and honey, and then with water in which the root of lily or of birthwort has been boiled, and then it returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the closed womb, then you must be prohibited from treating it with iron, taking care of it with what we have said in the division.
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner live embryos should be treated when they exit in an unnatural way.
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the form of natural childbirth by its signs, and that is when you see the woman strain in the lower parts and desires to smell the air, and on top of that is relieved by that which is above her of the birth, and hastens the exit of the embryo, then you know that this birth is according to the natural form, and it is the exit of it on its head, and the afterbirth with that or suspended or hanging from the umbilicus.
Therefore, when you see those signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily, because when they descend on their head, they leave the afterbirth, and it is cleansed of those superfluities with good purification.
But that which is otherwise is an unnatural and unfavorable birth, because when the embryo is exited on its feet and its body is exited before its head and its feet, and one of its hands, or at its foot, or its head and one hand are exited, or at its belly, and is quickly turned on its neck, resembling those unfavorable forms.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and skillful in all these matters, and to guard herself against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of those, and what skill is in those so that it may be signified by that and be established on that.
When the fetus is exited head-first through natural exertion, and this is strenuous for the woman and the birth is difficult, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then make her sit, or have the women support her, and evaporate her genitals with cooked fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small, flat spatula between her labia and with it splits the secundina (afterbirth), or scrapes it with her fingernail until it is free of moisture. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be clysterized with a mucilage of chickpea and a mucilage of fenugreek with wine.
And then, after the clyster, order her to be compressed and make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then you must reduce them gently little by little. But if it does not obey the introduction, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs, and then shake the stool on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she does not fall from the shaking.
And if the hands are not returned inside and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the remainder of the fetus, or tie its body to its hand with a cloth and extract it gently, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus is on its feet, then you must push them upwards, and gently turn the fetus, and then grasp it, and then take one of its feet and extend it gently little by little.
But when it has come out between the thighs, then you command her to strain, and make her sneeze forcefully, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled with what we have recommended, then you return the fetus little by little until you place it according to its natural position, for then it will be expelled easily.
And if it is prevented by everything we have recommended, then take mallow mucilage and dissolved gum, and pound it all well in a mortar, and then apply this to the vulva of the woman, at the very bottom of her belly. And then make her bathe in lukewarm water, up to her hypochondria (the area below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts are softened, then make a fumigation of myrtle for her and apply it to her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and then make her sneeze and hold her mouth, and lift her up, and press the lowest part of her belly with gentle compression, for the fetus will soon be expelled.
Thus [extract] the fetus laterally, holding it with one of your hands. Let [the reduction] be skillfully done by hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her thighs with violent shaking, and then administer poultices which I have recommended made of mucilage.
And if childbirth has begun, then make her sit on a stool, and then work on the reduction by hand, and grasp the fetus according to its natural shape, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain until it is expelled.
Thus [extract] the fetus over the neck, spreading your hands, and its face is towards its mother. Let the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then gently rotate it, and let this be done easily.
And when it is grasped, then place on its neck that which we have recommended of oil and mucilages.
And then instruct her to cough gently, and when you have done this, then make her sit on a stool and command her to lean to her right side, and then command her to strain; and make her sneeze with a [suitable] concoction. The prepared fetus exits sideways.
When the mother's horror subsides according to this method, consider, and then you incise the secundines with your fingernail until the moisture is evacuated.
And if the fetus is suspended around its face and neck, then you incise it at the umbilicus, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then push the fetus into the birth canal until you place it as it should be, and tell the woman to strain several times, and it will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four, but that is rare and seldom happens; but there are causes for this in nature, and they do not live.
And they share, even if they are multiple, a single placenta, and they are separated by a membrane, distinguishing them within the umbilical ligament of each one. And sometimes they are expelled as a single fetus by easy natural delivery, and sometimes they are expelled by difficult delivery as we have recounted.
And if any of their births are difficult and reach the point of unnatural presentations that we have described, then treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and skill, and with patience, and is ingenious according to every presentation, as she is prepared to manage with skill leading to health.
Concerning that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or more than ten are formed in the mother.  And it has been verified by me that one woman aborted and expelled six, and another ten, all formed, which was determined by the measurement of the glorious wise men.
And all this must be within the knowledge of the midwife, because it often appears similar to this, and therefore she must oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
On the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medication, and it has not yet been expelled, and you are forced to operate with your hand, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and is not afflicted with any of the conditions that threaten her with danger.
Then the woman should be placed on the bed on her side, her head inclined downwards, and her hips raised.
And the attendant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mother's vulva with warm oils and a mucilage of mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then let the midwife anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and gently introduce her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient places for this: then you consider if the fetus is positioned to descend head-first, and then you grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or at its clavicle, or in the appropriate places on its shoulders, or under its hypochondria.
But if the descent is feet first, then at the heel, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to hold the umbilical cord with the right hand, and place its curvature in the space between the fingers of the left hand, and introduce the hand gently and place the umbilical cord in one of those places which we have mentioned, until the empty space is reached.
And then place and fix another umbilical cord in opposition to it, or a third if necessary, so that the traction is equal, and there is not extension only along its straightness.
Rather, it should be manipulated on all sides, so that its expulsion is eased, just as it is done in a tooth extraction.
And it is necessary in this case that the extension is softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some of her fingers with oil and skillfully insert them and loosen what is retained with them.
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the umbilical cords should be moved to a higher position; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
But if the hand is delivered before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then extract it until it is entirely delivered, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you should proceed with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or experiences constriction during delivery, or if there is accumulated water in its head, then it is necessary to insert a spatula between the fingers and puncture the head to extract the water, or to break it open with an instrument which is named “alundat”, the form of which will come with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is next.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: and then it is necessary to incise it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with forceps as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is delivered but constricted at the clavicle, then it should be incised so that the moisture which is in the feet is drained.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it obey delivery, then you should incise the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the lower part of the belly is abscessed or dropsical, then it is necessary that you perforate it until the moisture which is in it runs out.
But if the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy and its direction to the mother's mouth is easy.
But if it is constricted around the belly and feet, then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and that you incise the belly and feet until that which is in them flows out.
But if the other limbs are obstructed and the head returns, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she inserts her hand into the womb, and searches with it for the head, and draws it with her fingers towards the mother's mouth, and then inserts into it one or two fingers, and draws it out with them.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted by a hot abscess which harms her, then it is necessary that nothing be disturbed about it; rather, it is necessary then to administer the effusion of humid, oily substances. And administer plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then, if possible, let it be rotated: and let that which we have said concerning a living fetus be administered.
And if this is not possible, then let it be incised in pieces, and let it be extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave no part of the afterbirth inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer that which we have said concerning the treatment of the hot abscesses which might happen to the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and soon after that, she was pregnant again, and soon after, the fetus died again. A long time ago, an abscess occurred in his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and it began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied plasters of strong attraction to him, and I extracted a bone from him; and after some days, I extracted another bone; and I was greatly surprised, since there should not be bones in the belly.
And then I considered that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Therefore I cleansed the wound, and extracted many bones from him, the woman being in good condition, and she lived in this way for a long time, with a little pus draining from him.
And I did not consider this to be strange, but because in this there is knowledge and help, so that the skillful physician administers cure with his hand.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of the incision with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a constrictor in which the limbs are bound, which constriction has two incisions at the two ends of two poles. However, this incision should be finer than the constrictor, and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each piece of wood be about two fingers, and the thickness of each be about one finger, and the length of each be one and a half palms; and in the middle space between the two pieces of wood are two fittings of the same wood which are already fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that she may be opened with them when you turn the two aforementioned incisions.
Another form similar to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, in the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity there are two additions like those you see, and the length of these additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with these, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her legs hanging down, that which is between her thighs open. Then open your hand with the instrument as you do with scissors equally, according to the amount you want to widen the opening of the mother, until the midwife does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
The form of the impellent by which the fetus is expelled:
Form of the [malady/problem] by which the large head is contained/held back.  (The "2" likely indicates a superscript or annotation in the original text, perhaps clarifying "amisidat.")
And it is similar to forceps which have teeth at the end, like tongs, and sometimes it is made long like forceps according to this form, like tongs, which have teeth like saw teeth with which it is incised and held back. Form of a small ailment/problem/malady.
The form of a hook having a spine/thorn.
The form of a hook having two spines/thorns.
The form of a spatula having two ends with which the head is severed/cut off.
The form of two broad spatulas.
All these instruments are exercised, and when the types of these are more multiplied and prepared by the artisans, the easier is their operation and the more plentiful they are for the people.
And do not neglect to have these instruments prepared, for the necessity of them is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should command the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze forcefully, and press your hand over her mouth and nostrils, and if it is expelled by this, it is good.
And if not, then you take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid and put in it herbs, opening the mother's mouth; thus with rue, chamomile,  anise,  and camomile, dried arabic gum, cassia lignea, and holy thistle. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that over the fire.
And then place a reed cannula in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's womb.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if after this treatment it remains and is not expelled, then you instruct the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or mallow poultice, and then introduce it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it; and when it is found, then take it and extract it little by little. But if it is attached to the womb of the mother, then she should introduce her hand as we have recounted, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it little by little as instructed, so that the mother does not collapse from the violent traction. Afterwards, it should be turned to the right and left sides with ease.
And if the mother's mouth (cervix) is constricted, we have already recounted the treatment with sneezing and with the oil and herb medication.
But if it is not expelled by all the means we have recounted, then be careful not to tire yourself in repeated attempts at extraction; however, what has come out at the mother's vulva should be tied to the woman's thigh, and then cauterize it with ointment and tetrafarmaco; for this will cause it to rot in a few days and it will dissolve and be expelled.
But when it rots, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then fumigations suitable for this should be administered.
But some of the Ancients have already proven the fumigation of hellebore and dried figs.
Method of instrumentation with which a woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and afterbirth:
Let it be made of glass or bronze.
And let the thin end be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation be placed on the embers of the fire.
And let her hold that until that fumigation is consumed and then another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are very narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that narrow passage with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil on it; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And if anal obstruction occurs due to the healing of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that that consolidation be incised, and then it should be treated with what we have said about treatment and lead (possibly referring to lead-based ointments).
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that develop below (presumably, in the anal or perineal region).
The fistulas that develop in the lowest part are nodular and thick, which develop near the anus externally or in the space between the buttocks.
And it is one fistula or several.
And when that nodule is old and discharges a watery white fluid or fine pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because of what comes out of them through discharge and flatulence; and sometimes the patient experiences discharge, and worms easily emerge from them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the space leading to the bladder, and into the meatus of the penis; and some penetrate into the hip joint and into the buttock.
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating into the rectum from one that doesn't, is that you insert your finger into the rectum, and insert a thin probe of brass or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor does anything emerge from the opening, neither discharge, worms, nor flatulence, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then attend to its cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the meatus of the penis, then its sign is the passage of urine from it, and prohibition of it so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it penetrates into the joint of the hip or into the bone and causes pain in the surrounding area, then its sign is that the probe penetrates thus far if there is no tortuosity in it into the bone, and pain in the circumference of the area; and that from it there is always foulness; and that medication is of no use in it, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no complete cure, and the treatment of these is the work and vanity of foolish physicians on which they are fixated.
But in that which is not penetrating and not chronic, healing with iron is hoped for, according to what I shall recommend to you, and this I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back and raises his legs towards you, his thighs inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels pain around the anus, then you must insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe itself penetrating on your finger, besides what you feel between your finger and that fistula or flesh, then you know certainly that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, for there is no cure in that, as we have said.
However, some say that it is healed slowly in some men.
And of the medication by which help is hoped for him, it is that you ignite a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as is preceded, and introduce that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those fleshy, fistulated parts resembling a bird's feather quill are cauterized.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen until all the cauterized flesh sloughs away, and then treat it with flesh-generating and consolidating medicines; and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other cure.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate to your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or fistula, and you see the fistula in such a way that it is the surface of the skin, then dry the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will dry it with the probe; and it shall be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the end of the probe arrives, and through the probe it is freed and removed.
And then cleanse those fistulated tissues that resemble a bird's quill, and let no part of them remain.
But if you see a flow of blood come between you and your work when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and lightest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire achieves two good things: that is, cauterization of those affected tissues, and the staunching of blood and drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed cloth until the place is clean, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate firm flesh.
And these are medicines in which there is stypticity and desiccation; when then it is restored, it is healed and we do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the rectum and is elongated from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and examine it with that.
And so if you feel the probe and between you and it there is a covering of fat or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then it is not an operation in itself but according to faith and hope. (This implies the outcome is uncertain and relies on a positive prognosis)
Which condition requires one of three approaches: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that you can insert the poultices and the cure is closer; and do not proceed excessively with incision so that you do not cut the muscle containing the rectum, and that this happens to the patient against their will; and then treat it with what we have said because it will heal quickly; or elongate that covering and clean it after drying it near the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or another instrument with a sharp tip, and then clean those fistulous tissues which are in the incision where you can with cauterization; and then strive to make the restoration of the entire wound with incision, and if the orifice above is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the rectal area, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the anus near the surface of the body with the tail and in the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like a needle for preparing stitches in this form:
and insert it into that fistula wrapped with five threads or around them; and then insert a probe with thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it penetrates the margin of the anus inwards, then you pull the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the anus; and pull out the end of the thread and tie the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for a day or two, and when you cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues are cut which are between the two ends of the thread and that they fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, except that it should be in the preon, because you should not do it so as to cut the muscle; and then treat it with that until it is healed.
The form of the pointed probe with which the fistula is probed: its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is unnecessary, as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows during their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds, for either they are between the buttocks, similar to charcoal, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of these, there are small and large ones, and blood flows from them constantly, and there is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the months of little humidity, from which flows a citrine liquid or a little blood with continuous flow, and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to strain and to push until the anus comes out and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you should seize and suspend them with hooks or hold them with your nail, and then incise them near their roots.
But if the hooks cannot hold them because of their humidity and softening, then take them with rough cloth or with cotton or with a wool pledget, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle on them some hot powders near the incision, so that they act on them in place of cautery; or cauterize them as was described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey the expulsion, then you should  stimulate the patient with a  stimulant which has slight biting properties, and it should be washed with that water which is in the anus and which should slightly obey expulsion when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and it is that you should take them with your nail, or that you take them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, you must suture it in this manner: take wrapped thread and insert it into a needle and then pull the hemorrhoids to the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle into the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, and it should be laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong constriction, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one of them unstitched, so that the superfluity of blood may flow from it.
And then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place that on the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointments or with other cures until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then you must scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are moist and the crust above is removed, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are consolidated.
Then remove them with more forceful scraping than the first time until they bleed much and are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a whitlow, that is, a felon, is nothing but a round sore according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a carnation, and is found throughout the body and especially in the parts below the feet and in the fingers, and from these, pain arises from the inflammation.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the felon be incised, and it should be held with forceps or with a hook, and be eradicated from its root; and then treat that.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is prescribed in its chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round eminence on the surface of the body, and many times it appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is that you extend and incise it, or that you tie it tightly with thread or with a hair so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize that, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine (caustic substance).
But a mole is a small, rough or fistulated, large eminence on the surface of the body, growing much in the pubic area, and when the cold finds it, it is felt in it similar to the bite of an ant, and it is made around the body (or, "all over the body").
And its cure is that you take a cannula of goose quill or vulture quill, and it should be strong, and place that over the mole until the circle of the cannula contains the parts of it; and then rotate your hand with the cannula until you incise what is in the circumference of the mole, and you place that into the depth of the flesh, and you eradicate the mole from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a bronze or iron cannula according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn out to a thinness, blunt (that is, non-perforated), wrapped so that the grip of it might be facilitated and its rotation.
And if you wish, cauterize the mole as was previously mentioned in the cauterization of a boil in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the form of the instruments that are used to give a clyster.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or of brass, or of molten or hammered bronze; and sometimes it is made of these instruments, small and large, according to those to whom they are administered, for that which is administered in the cure of small children should be small; and for those whose rectums are fragile or painful, the clysters for them should be small.
The form of a large and a small clyster:
The head of the cannula above, to which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and there should be a distinction where the bag is tied, as you see.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the rectum, should be blunt (that is, not open) and very smooth, tapering to a point; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the [patient's] rectum, and slightly larger.
And the bag, in which the medicines are placed, should be made of an animal's bladder or sheepskin, and its length should be one palm.
And it should be perforated with many holes, as it is rolled up; and the space between the holes should be the width of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong, raw linen thread, about ten threads, or thereabouts; and tie the bag with it; and when the bag is placed in position, tighten it at the head of the clyster tube between two points with strong compression, and then cauterize each hole:  The way to cauterize this is to fill the bag with the medicine, which should be warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then the patient should lie supine on their back, raise their legs, and place a cushion underneath, on which are placed cloths or a blanket; then lubricate the tip of the clyster tube with oil, or egg white, or fenugreek mucilage, or something similar; then insert it into the rectum easily; then the attendant should take the bag and squeeze it with both hands and forcefully until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the clyster tube and the patient lowers their legs.
And if the medicine forces a bowel movement, hold it as long as possible; and if they sleep with it all night, it is more beneficial.
But if you use this clyster for diseases of diarrhea and the bowel movement troubles them, then repeat the clyster until they are healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then the clyster tube should be very thin and soft, like those used for infants.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have already said in the section on wound treatment what was appropriate for the book, and I will now describe the treatment of those wounds with hands and medicines according to what is suitable.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what causes the wounds and according to the location on which the wounds occur.
And the causes by which wounds are often made are such as a stone throw, or an incision from a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a club, or with an arrow, or similar to those from many causes.
But the wounds that are in the locations of the body are such as a wound which falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the thigh, and similar to those of other limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or the like, and it has only bruised the scalp and is a large wound, and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should immediately bleed him and phlebotomize the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and extract blood from him according to his strength unless some reason prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has flowed from the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and from heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his diet be reduced and he should not approach meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs in him, a linen cloth soaked in rose oil alone or in wine in which something astringent has been steeped.
But if there is certainty of a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent, its own blood, and the air has not changed that powder which I will shortly recount.
And if the air has already changed that [wound], then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is from a sword cut or similar, and its lips are not brought together by themselves, then bring them together with sutures, according to what I shall recount concerning their suturing.
But if the wound has already exposed the bone and hangs, and there is not retention of it but only a small suspension, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh properly in place of the bone, and command the patient to be fed on foods which are not coarse: heads and strong cooked meats and similar, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood might occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you search for the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you cut it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity compels you that what we have said does not suffice, then you cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is slight; thus you sprinkle on them this powder, for it consolidates them before the air alters them.
And the manner of the powder is this: take olive oil one part, dragon's blood two parts, unslaked lime three parts; let all be well crushed and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and let it be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with small bandages and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is thus, that it is not festering, for the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes it does this alone with heat, when you do not have olive oil and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary that it be placed on them and they are stitched, and they are fresh wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is in summer time, then this powder is not intended to be applied, but rather treat those with what we have said, in such a way that you apply digestive ointments on them, or that you place on them powders of barley flour with water and honey until they shed pus; and then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if a fracture in a bone happens with the wound, and it is small, then you extract it with tweezers.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of the neck wounds.
But if the wound happens in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a simple head wound.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are incised, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to be done but to avoid treating it with something that causes strong stypticity, such as vitriol and things similar to it, because they are harmful to the nerves and constrict them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is cured with light medicines, such as being washed with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or without, and  incense when washed with fresh water and other minor remedies in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or draw together its edges, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or there is pus at its lowest point, then perforate it at the most depressed point.
But if an artery has been incised in the wound and there is a flow of blood, then separate or cut it, or tie it, or cauterize it if necessity requires it.
But if the vertebrae of the neck have been incised, or all, and the veins of the neck have been saved, then you should align the edges of the wound with sutures over the cervical spine and do not touch that; first equalize it and return it to its natural shape, and then tighten it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for a few days; and then treat it with ointments that possess styptic and healing properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has damaged the bone, then clean it, and if there are bone fragments in it, extract all those that you can; and what you cannot remove, leave it until the wound suppurates, and is separated and the injury is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle upon it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that of which the recollection will come later in its chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if there is a puffing up from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is recent with its own blood, then you apply powder to it in the first hour, and do not bind it tightly lest the blood be retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the patient, but apply attractive ointment, and unbind it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not at hand, then place old linen on the opening of the wound so that it draws out what is there of humors; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that what is contained therein flows out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and it has not caused the patient spasms or tremors of the heart, or constriction of the throat, and you see his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with caulks and with other treatment until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; treat it then according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from an incision of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the foot or leg, then treat it with what has been previously described for sutures, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has caused damage to the bone, and pieces of it are already detached, then you should clean the wound and try to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound is cleaned, so that their extraction is easier.
And for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the method of treatment is the same as what we have said.
Of wounds of the abdomen.
The illness which happens in the abdomen is either great, or small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, and it is so that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not take care of their replacement immediately, because it opens and swells, so that their entry is made difficult.
And therefore the best rupture is moderate, because in that one the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in the two species previously mentioned.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is according to four methods, but two sutures are common which are suitable for the suturing of other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures peculiar to it are used for other wounds, but however they are more appropriate in the suturing of the abdomen.
And I recount to you each one with explanation and clarification.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has protruded through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed by the coldness of the air.
And therefore when this is so, then it is necessary that you warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or that you boil it in water with squill, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with it until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it is done with something that has astringent properties, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And so when you see the inflammation to be resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marshmallow have been boiled, for by this the entry of it is eased with little trouble.
But if the return of it is hindered despite this treatment, then incise the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is proper that there is no incision, and there is no need to dry it because of necessity and debility.
Of the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not-sharp, and the extremity is thin, and not with the thinness of a spatula, rather it is somewhat blunt.
And thus, when the wound is widened and the intestine protrudes, then its reduction should be according to its natural form, in its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and is in the lower part of the belly, then you should make the patient lie on his back, and raise his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you should raise his head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the belly, then always direct your attention and consideration to this, so that the side in which the wound is located is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, place what you have prepared, and then let the assistant, between your compassionate hands, hold the entire rupture with his hand and adjust its lips, and then uncover from that side to that side so that the suture is somewhat further away.
And this is the common method of suturing: first you take the needle, or several needles according to the size of the wound; then leave the width of your ring finger from the edge of the rupture, and thread the needle, ensuring you don't put thread in the two ends of the suture, with the two ends of the thread that is under the suture of the inner parts, until you make it penetrate that part; and you have already added two ends of the suture and two ends of the thread, and this makes four points.
Then tighten with double thread in the needle's circuit, and sometimes from both sides together, and then bring together the two edges of the wound with a decent approximation; and then leave the width of a finger and place another needle there, and then tie this with thread as you did with the first needle; and do not cease doing this with the needles if necessary until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And let the needles be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin, for thin needles cut the flesh too easily; and thick ones are difficult to thread, therefore they should be of medium thickness.
And the needle should not be inserted too deeply into the two ends of the suture, so that the flesh is not excessively cut and the wound does not open before it heals.
And the sutures should not be too long, lest the wound be prevented from healing.
Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not harm the patient during their sleep; and you will place over the suture finely powdered material from all sides, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them there until you know that the wound is healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient for small wounds, because one or two needles, or thereabouts, suffice for their suturing.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you stitch the four ends, of the wound and two ends of the flap, at once, with a needle around which a thread is wrapped, even in thinness and thickness.
Then, when with the needle you have penetrated these four ends, return the needle to the same side from which you began, so that the drawn thread may fall from the uppermost part to the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth stitch with which cloths are tightened.
And place between each stitch the width of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture I sewed up the wound which had happened to a man in his belly, which had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was more than a palm, and two palms of intestine had come out from the middle of the wound, and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I treated him and he is healed; and afterwards he lived for many years, and remained in his usual state of health.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle that he was, for I did not treat him with ointment, because it was in a place where ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I placed old linen on the wound twice a day and washed it frequently with honey water until it was healed.
But I will describe two kinds of sutures proper, according to the account of Galen (Paraula likely refers to Galen, a prominent ancient physician whose works were highly influential in medieval medicine).
And it is that you begin with the suture of the chain, and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And so when the needle penetrates the chain and into that same laceration according to its width and length of the whole body, leave the end of the thread in which you have inserted the needle; and make it penetrate at the other end, the needle from the inside to the outside at the other end of the half. (This is a description of a surgical suture technique; the language is quite technical and requires understanding of the procedure to translate accurately).
And when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again at that same end of the half from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is in this stitch, and make the needle penetrate at the other end from the inside to the outside. (Continuing the description of the surgical suture technique).
And make it penetrate with that penetration with which you make it penetrate into the thread at the end of the half which is on his side until all of that penetrates. (This sentence continues the technical description of the surgical stitching, and the meaning remains slightly unclear due to the archaic language and the medical procedure being described).
And then begin on that same side and sew that with the end that is of the thread in the other stitch, and extract the needle from the chain which it penetrated, and then return it into that chain and sew the end of the thread which is on the other side with this end of the half; and extract it from the chain which is on his side. (This continues the detailed, step-by-step description of the surgical suture technique.)
And do this once and again, and do this one time then another time until you sew everything that is outside according to that example. (This completes the instructions for the surgical stitching, indicating repetition until the wound is fully closed).
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a leather suture with which you sew skins, in such a way that you sew the thread once from the part of the end of the chain, and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.  (This passage summarizes the stitching technique, comparing it to stitching leather and emphasizing the method of passing the thread in and out.)
But the second type of suture, which is properly called, and this is from a speech by him and by him recounted, is that you sew it according to the similarity with which some physicians sew it, in such a way that they join each part to its corresponding part in a manner convenient to nature; and they apply the end of one to the other and the end of the half to the other end, and this is done as I have recounted to you. (This describes a second suture technique, referencing a physician's method focusing on joining corresponding parts of the wound naturally.)
So that you place the needle at the end of the half near you from the outside part, and make it penetrate to the inside part in that sole, and leave two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside through the two ends of the thread. (This gives detailed instructions for a step in the surgical procedure, describing needle placement and thread manipulation.)
And then turn each of them, and make each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part through two other ends of the half that are on the opposite side. (This continues the surgical instructions, describing the manipulation of the needle and thread to close the wound.)
And this manner of suture is better than the common, light suture, and it is the suture of which I previously made mention. (This compares the described suture technique to a simpler method, asserting its superiority.)
This is the speech of Galien.  (This identifies Galen as the source of the described surgical technique.)
And moreover, I would say to some, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is, that you insert into each of these a thread, and begin with the insertion of the needle from the side which is towards you, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew hides similarly. (This describes an alternative suture technique for closing an abdominal wound, using two needles and comparing it to the stitching of leather.)
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the belly, then the suture of it is more difficult than in other places of the belly. (This explains that suturing a wound in the center of the abdomen is more challenging than in other areas.)
But the judgment of the medication of a wound is the judgment of other wounds. (This states that the principles of treating one wound apply to others as well, indicating a general approach to wound care.)
The reason is that when you clean the fresh wound with its blood before the air alters it, and reduce the intestine and sew it and prepare it decently, then place on it incarnative powder. (This describes the treatment of a wound: cleanse it immediately, reduce any protruding intestine, stitch it closed, and then apply a powder to promote healing.)
And if the air has already altered the wound, then place on it certain ointments which are present with you until they form a hard scab and the threads fall out, and are consolidated with ciphac and myrrh; and then cure it as is the cure of other wounds until it is healed.  (This explains the treatment if the wound has already been exposed to air: apply ointments to promote scabbing, using ingredients like ciphac and myrrh, and then continue with standard wound care until healing is complete.)
But if you do not have the medicines present, then immediately place on it, as soon as it begins to discharge pus, old lint, and change that twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you do not need other medication in many situations when the wounds are simple. (This offers an alternative treatment if the necessary ointments are unavailable: use clean lint to dress the wound and change it twice daily until healed, stating that this is sufficient for many simple wounds.)
But if you fear that the wound will communicate infection to the principal members, then it is necessary that you submerge lamb's wool in good quality soaked oil, or in rose oil, and place that in the area around the places that are between the armpits and below the armpits. (This advises a preventative measure if there's a risk of the wound spreading: soak wool in oil and place it around the armpits to prevent the infection's spread to vital areas.)
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in his intestines, for many times this happens, then cauterize it with black, warm styptic wine, and especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and an exit wound has been made penetrating into its cavity. (This instructs to cauterize the area with styptic wine if there's pain or infection in the intestines, particularly if the infection has penetrated the intestinal wall.)
And know that that which is of the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is of difficult healing because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a wound which falls upon it at all; and this is because of the great multitude of veins which are in it and because of the magnitude of those which are in it, and because of the subtlety of the body of that, and because of the proximity of that to the nature of nerves. (This explains why wounds to the jejunum (a section of the small intestine) are difficult to heal: the abundance of veins, the delicate nature of the tissue, and its proximity to nerves hinder healing.)
But if that which proceeds from the wound is the web (likely referring to a membrane or tissue), and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your skill with which you reduce the intestines equally. (If a membrane is protruding from the wound, the instruction is to carefully and skillfully reduce it back to its proper place, similar to how the intestines would be repositioned.)
But if it has passed through the space and has already become green or black, then bind it with a bandage over the place that is blackened, so that a flow of blood comes, for in the web are veins and arteries. (If the tissue has become necrotic (green or black), the instructions are to apply a bandage to promote bleeding, as the affected area contains veins and arteries.)
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and place two ends of the bandage hanging down from the part below the wound outside it, so that the traction of it towards the falling of the web and emission of the arm of the wound may be relieved upon you. (Incise the tissue beyond the ligament and let two ends of the bandage hang down to relieve tension and prevent further protrusion of the affected tissue and the arm of the wound.)
