A reminder of wounds when they happen in the intestine. (This is a heading or title indicating the following text will discuss wound treatment pertaining to the intestines.)
When a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, because of this, for I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with a lance thrust, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and had become a fistula, and from it came pus and flatulence. (If intestinal rupture is small, it might be repairable, as exemplified by a case of an old, fistulized lance wound to the stomach that drained pus and gas.)
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his recovery, but I continued to treat him until he was healed and the place was consolidated. (Despite low expectations, the author treated the patient until the wound was fully healed and the area consolidated.)
And I, having seen the place now consolidated, feared for the patient that some accident of illness might befall him in his belly; and no such ill accident happened, and he returned to his usual disposition, eating and drinking, and then entered a bath and was exercised in his limbs.  (After the wound healed, the author worried about further abdominal complications. However, the patient recovered well, resuming normal diet, bathing, and exercise.)
And some experienced [people] have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine, and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner: and that is that you take ants with large heads; and then adjust the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants that has its mouth open over the two lips of the wound; then when it bites those and clenches its mouth, cut off its head, because it adheres and does not dissolve. (Experienced practitioners suggested using large-headed ants to close small intestinal wounds:  place an ant's open mouth across the wound edges, then decapitate it to keep it firmly in place.)
And then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease to do this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the entire wound. (Continue placing ants along the wound until it is completely closed.)
And then reduce [the intestines] and sew the wound; and let those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm will befall the patient. (After the ants close the wound, reduce the intestine and stitch the wound, leaving the ant heads in place until the intestine heals, causing no harm to the patient.)
And it is also possible that the intestine be tied with fine thread which is made from the intestine of an animal, since it is thinner in needle; and that is that you take the end of these intestinal threads, and it should be very thin, and then tie its end with its thread, wrapped, and then thread that thread into needles and sew the intestine with it, and reduce that [intestine] into the disruption [or rupture]. (Alternatively, the intestine can be sewn with fine thread made from animal gut.  Prepare the thread carefully, then sew the intestinal rupture using a needle, and finally, reduce the intestine back into place.)
And this method of suturing with ants is only a matter of trust and hope. (This ant-suturing method is merely a hopeful, uncertain approach.)
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, then this is only a palliation, and not a complete healing. (However, if the wound is large and particularly in the small intestine, this technique only provides temporary relief, not a complete cure.)
On the treatment of fistulas and fistulae. (Concerning the treatment of fistulas.)
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and forms an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus continually and is not excised, then, in whatever member it may be, it is universally called a fistula, and we call it a *zukem* (or *azukem*).
And a fistula, in truth, is a hard, white, knotted swelling, which is painless and has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and for this reason it is called a claw-like ulcer, that is, a claw.
And in some people it is moist, discharging pus; and very quickly the moisture is exuded in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes thin, and sometimes coarse, and sometimes fine.
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs, and their treatment with medicines, in the division (of the text).
And it is necessary that I make known to you the treatment of fistula, which is done by means of a manual operation.
And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body; for there are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the joints composed by composition, such as those of the hand and foot; and those that damage the proximity of the principal member and its likeness.
Therefore, that one of these fistulas which is according to this manner is one of the diseases of difficult healing, and does not receive treatment, and occupation with it is work and ignorance.
And if there is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its healing, and want to arrive at knowledge of it, then take a probe of brass or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, and search for it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a subtle lead probe, because lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily bent along the tortuosity.
And if the fistula has many horrific passages, it is not possible for you to determine its course with a probe.
Then, inject a clyster into the orifice, because the liquid by which you inject it will go through the other orifices and clean them.
And then inquire carefully, in what manner it is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves here, and if the depth of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a fistula which has many orifices, and be upon this entirely with the sum of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your compression with the hand over the place, and the resemblance of the signs and accidental causes to the abscess.
And when you have considered all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the cure with confidence: and it is that you consider then if it is a fistula apparently near or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you.
Then you dry the fistula as was said before in the description of it, and remove that which is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and that which has grown in the orifice of the flesh, or of the skin and the like, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it in the front as much as is possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; and then apply cauteries wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce them into the depth of the fistula, which you have grasped with an instrument.
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which engender healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you should investigate it and dry it out as we have recounted, if you do not prohibit any cause from a vein or nerve or principal member, as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that will cause it to grow new flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the total eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again and carefully perform the scraping and cleansing with your utmost strength, and then restore it.
And so if it is healed, good; but if not, then know that the corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which has not been reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to pull it out, then pull it out with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then carefully remove all of them, and with your strength leave none of them behind.
But if some of the moisture causes you difficulty, and there is no fear or error in their incision, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not ready to remove the bones at this time, then place on the wound something that will suppurate the wound, and leave it until this reaches the area around those bones of flesh and ligaments, and keep the wound from healing, and keep the incision that you made in your operation constricted.
Furthermore, if you fear, place on it a cotton ball submerged in crushed rosemary with oil, or a cotton ball submerged in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, because when those ligaments are suppurated, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction is easier.
But if it is a large bone, such as a leg bone or thigh bone or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp only on the surface until the blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and completely incise it to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use this as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, about thirty years of age, who suffered pain in his thigh, and from the inner parts, pus had spread through the body until it reached the thigh; it was a matter of an abscess, a large abscess, and there was no external cause for it.
And then time passed with this due to the error of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and much matter flowed from it, and its treatment was delayed until the leg was fistulated, and many orifices were formed in it, all of which discharged pus and the humors of the body.
And then many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no improvement in his condition, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter running from those orifices in great quantity; and the body of that man was all swollen, and his color was jaundiced.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and it reached the bone.
And then I examined all the orifices and found them to extend to all parts of the leg.
And then I considered and incised one of those orifices until I exposed a part of the bone, and I found it corrupted, and it was already earthy and corrupt and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted to the extent that it had penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sawed off what was exposed and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I thought that in the bone there was only that corruption which I cut away and sawed off, and I had already removed it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidative medicine for a long time, and it is not consolidated.
And then I iterated and uncovered the bone a second time over the first uncovering, and I found continuing corruption in the bone.
And I sawed off what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was thwarted in making its cure and restoration, and it is not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and did not cease to incise the bone little by little, piece by piece, and I was thwarted in its restoration, and it was not restored until I incised about a palm's breadth of it, and that I extracted with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicines, and it is slightly consolidated and healed.
And further iteration in its operation and section is not necessary except for the disposition of its fragility and for the paucity of its tolerance and its fear of death, for it suffered at all hours from syncope due to the superfluity of evacuation.
And therefore it is healed by a whole healing, and nothing is in place of the hard bone flesh; and its disposition in its body is made good and its strength returned; and it is preserved in its dispositions and suffered no impediment in walking nor harm in any respect.
But if it is a prominent bone in a place already excised, then you must excise it in this manner: that you take a ligature and bind it tightly to the extremity of that prominent bone, and command someone to extend it upwards; and place another woolen ligature thicker than the first; and then tie it over the flesh which is below the bone, and extend its extremity so that you pull the flesh to the lower parts, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place wood or a board decently under the bone, for when you do this, the saw is not prohibited from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted place little by little, for if corruption is in the concavity of the bone and does not appear in its outward aspect to the touch, then force a sawing of it another time.
And if the bone is corrupted and not prominent, with one part continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or part of it, then you uncover the flesh from all parts completely; and then place wood underneath, and then saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the location of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no reason for it but ingenuity (or skillful intervention).
And if the corruption is in the bone of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, you must inquire into the corruption in whatever manner appears to you, and you must open it, and clean it according to any disposition which is possible for you, and with every tool it should be rectified for you, when no vein or nerve opposes you.
And know that the incisors and saws for incising these bones are many according to the location of the bones, and the preparation of them, and the thickness of them, and the subtlety and size and smallness of them, and the hardness and their rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument convenient to that operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the type of instrument of which you should make use; for with you is great study and long and extensive knowledge of the methods of this art, and testimony of types of illnesses, because then you already have for yourself that which is convenient in the instruments for every illness.
And I am providing you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will keep as simple ones, with which you will proceed to your operation which you will practice on the others. Form of saw:

Concerning the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
The extremities may become diseased either by intrinsic or extrinsic causes; or if you have treated that corruption with those medicines and the treatment is ineffective, or you see the corruption spreading through the limb without being caused by anything, then you must incise that limb to the point where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may escape death or a greater impairment of the limb.
And the sign of this which happens is that the limb becomes black as if fire had already burned it, or the blackness spreads until that decay reaches the limb so that it affects and consumes the entire body; and then treat it with an incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the attack of some poisonous creature, such as a sea scorpion, a viper, or a spider, and the like.
And if the corruption or the attack is in the extremity of a finger, then you incise the finger and do not give space to the corruption so that it spreads to the hand.
And similarly, if it happens in the hand, then you incise it at the root of the palm, and do not give space to the corruption so that it spreads to the forearm.
And if it affects that [the forearm], then incise the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that case is the death of the patient; and to administer any other remedy beyond that is of little virtue.
And similarly, if corruption affects a toe, then you incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the heel, then you incise the whole foot. But if it ascends into the knee, then you incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy for him but dismissal, and to leave the patient to death.
And the manner of incising or sawing off a limb is that you tighten a ligature on the lower part which you wish to incise, and tighten another ligature above the location; and let an assistant stretch a ligature on the lower parts, and let another assistant stretch a ligature above; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures  so that all the flesh is exposed; and then let it be incised or sawn off.
And it is necessary that a linen cloth be placed on all parts so that it does not touch the healthy area, and that the patient receives a suitable lesion and a hot abscess.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while operating, then you carefully cauterize the affected area, or apply some powders that staunch the blood; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot with a burning that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe until it affected the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself because he saw the cure going to the limb with such vehemence that he found himself in pain and burning, and he incised that foot up to the joint, and it healed.
A long time later, the same corruption occurred in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I tried to repel that superfluity with what I applied to his hand in medicines, to purify his body; and the superfluity was not repelled and began to spread to another finger until it reached the hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, but I did not want to do that, hoping for the repulsion of that superfluity, and more so fearing death from the incision of his hand, because the patient's strength was failing.
And after he left me, he returned to his land, and it was reported to me that that man prepared and incised his whole hand, and he was healed.
And I have included this narrative so that it may be helpful in cases of this disease, and that it may serve as a sign by which it may be recognized and according to which it may be treated.
Concerning the treatment of sinuses and the quality of clyster administration with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy member and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken open and perforated, and the pus comes out of it, and the place remains empty, as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is over it is thin like a delicate cloth, and it has not reached further corruption, and does not impinge on the bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and not a fistula.
However, if the space is prolonged to the point that the corruption affects some member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the treatment of it is to incise that skin completely, especially when it is thinned and made like cloth, and to ensure that it does not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you suspect that the skin adheres and that the corruption has not spread further, and there is a swelling of the skin, then you treat that with a clyster.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the pus that comes out has a foul odor, then clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine, which is made of oil, sour wine, and resin, taking equal parts, and they are mixed in a vessel, and they are cooked over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
And then you take what is necessary of it and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with this, and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
And then extract that with a syringe; and do this for as many days until the sinus is cleansed and the stench is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with lye water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is that you take the ash of vines, and put water on it; and then strain it and clysterize with it until you are certain that the sinus is then clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the lye water, then clysterize it with water and honey and some crushed resin mixed in, and clysterize with wine and honey mixed, for it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes it adhere to the sinus, and especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are sure that the sinus is now clean, then clysterize it with something that causes the flesh to grow there.
And it is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or that you clysterize it with other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the orifice of the sinus is narrow, so that the clyster cannula cannot be inserted, then you should gradually widen it with a small iron instrument, and place a caulked plug wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment inside until it is widened.
And similarly, if the orifice is wide, then you should bring its lips together with sutures and leave an opening sufficient for the clyster to enter without addition [of force/widening].
And if the orifice from which the pus flows is located high up, then dry the lowest point of it so that the pus will flow downwards, for when the pus is contained within the depth of the sinus, flesh will not be generated there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus to the lowest point as you wish, then I advise you to prepare the limb with a preparation by which the pus flows lightly as it is prepared, and that no pus is retained.
But the cloth that is over the orifice of the sinus, this you should change every hour so that what is collected there from the pus can be removed. And so that you may know if the pus that flows is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this, you should examine the location of the sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment continues in this manner, and you see that the matter discharged onto the orifice of the sinus is already adhering, or is about to adhere, then, on the third day, loosen it; and when you loosen the dressing, change the cloth to another and renew the ointment.
But if near the sinus there is a subtle poison, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing approaches, for there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and there are others in whom the opposite occurs.
And if undigested pus has issued from the sinus for many days, then you should know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust to the drying of the medicines; and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their effect,  they are dried like diafinicon ointment when excess zegi is added to it.
And the best remedy with which it is cured is to take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and let old wine be added to it, equal in amount to its substance, and mix that with it, and use that.
And it resembles that action, when there is no pus, diafinicon ointment, honey cooked until thickened, and use it; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, grind it all, and powder it over the honey while it is on the fire, and then apply this to a cloth and bind it over the sinus; and apply this with honey which you have cooked until thickened, and powder it on a cloth and bind it.
And sometimes aristolochia longa and roots of lily of the valley (and it is lily of the valley) and barley flour and centaury are administered with it, and use these simples or compounded ones, grind them, sift them, and powder them over the honey which has been mentioned; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the depth of it, and it has already spread to the bones or other hard members or ligaments; then treat it with a fistular treatment, as has been said above.
Concerning the treatment of paronychia, and of the worn nail, and of the added incision of the finger, and of the section.
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of the finger or toe of the hand or foot, and quickly spreads to other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its treatment is neglected, or it is suppurated by a hot abscess, or it is corrupted, and pus is discharged until it corrodes the root of the nail, and quickly corrupts the whole of it, and quickly its corruption penetrates to the bone until there is a fetid odor to it, and the extremity of the finger becomes swollen, and its color becomes dark.
And therefore when you have treated it with what we have said in the division and you do not succeed in your treatment, then it is necessary that you incise with an iron all the superfluity which remains of the nail and then cauterize the wound there, for a hot cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail, being empty, now compress the flesh at the parts within, and begin to prick and injure it, then it is necessary that you place a thin blade under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it up and incise that flesh easily, and place on that which remains of the flesh corrosive medicines until all is removed; and then treat that with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already penetrated the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and extract it, for it will never heal if the bone is corrupted within.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption spreading in the finger, then you incise it at one of the joints according to what is prescribed in its treatment, and then cure it until it is healed.
But if the nail suffers percussion or abrasion, and there is vehement pain in it, then it is necessary that you first phlebotomize the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp instrument with a transverse section from above downwards, and be careful that the section does not reach the flesh which is beneath the nail, for you will cause the patient vehement pain, and it will be a cause of the generation of superfluous flesh in the place; and then treat that place with whatever relieves pain.
But a finger, which is born in the hands of some men, is very quickly fleshy, and very quickly bony, or very quickly develops a nail.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or their birth is at some joint of the fingers. But that which is born at some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born in the joints is very quickly painful.
And of those that are born fleshy, the section is easy, and it is that you incise that one below its root with a broad instrument.
But of that which is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore guard yourself from its excision.
But in that which is born on a finger between the joints, it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a round incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then cure it until it is healed.
But a consolidation which happens to the fingers between their joints, either by the birth of a man born thus, or by the consolidation of a wound or by burning with fire, or similarly, then it is necessary that you separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not lightly consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as is necessary.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you separate that consolidation according to what is prepared and suits the shape of the member.
Concerning the incision of vices, there are several.
Vices are varices, and they are twisted, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and most frequently in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and those who carry burdens.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with a strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
But the cure of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be elevated and completely extracted.
The incision is done in this manner: first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick, feculent blood of the leg is resolved. Then bind the patient's leg, from the upper part of the thigh to the part below the knee, with a bandage. Then incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision. Then draw the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards and downwards until the quantity you desire and which you deem sufficient is released, and which will remove the strength of the disease from the patient.
Then bind it up, and instruct him to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But their elevation is according to this manner: you who shave the patient's leg, if there is much hair on it, and then place them in a bath and bathe their leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are distended; or, if a bath is not available to you, that the limb be warmed by vigorous exercise, until the member is heated.
Then incise the vein on the straight part of the vein by a longitudinal incision, or behind it below the knee, or at its lowest point below the heel.
Then open the vein with an incision and scrape the vein completely until it bleeds and it is from this appearance that you see that redness changing to black.
And then, when you open the vein, you see that white which is like a cord.
Then you place a ribbon underneath so that it is raised and separated from the vein, and suspend it with a golden hook.
Then make another incision near that incision for three fingers' breadth, and scrape the vein from the part above the vein until it bleeds.
Then raise it and incise it at the back near the heel, and then pull it out and raise it until it comes out through the second incision, and then pull it out from the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until it is all removed; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you to be extracted and elevated, then insert a double-pointed strong needle and tie it and extract it, or insert a ribbon underneath and move your hand in every direction until it is released. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is made difficult for you to elevate it and it becomes a harm to the patient.
And when it is all removed, then place on the wound site wool soaked in rose-scented wine and olive oil and un-scented olive oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the intestine is constricted, having a tortuosity where it is coiled in the parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you make an incision upon it in every part of its tortuosity and in the places of its coiling, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it entirely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
The form of the incision with which the intestine is cut:
It is not sharpened like the hooks, nor is it of a sharp point so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of a thick, flexible lion [likely meaning "lion-like," referring to its strength or possibly a material], for if it were thin, it would cut the vein because of its thinness; therefore it has a superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of a vein from the leg.
This vein is engendered in the legs in hot lands, such as al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs, and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little moisture, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And it is quickly engendered in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, and scabies, and worms engendering between the skin and the flesh, come from within the body.
And a sign of the onset of this vein is that a vehement inflammation occurs in the leg; and the area is blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, which resembles the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachm to two drachms, and bind it and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as some cause from it appears outwardly, wrap it in lead and bind it. And if the growth is prolonged, then you incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise its root until it has emerged, for if you incise it, it is retracted and enters the flesh and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the area and a bad ulceration.
Therefore it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled from the bottom and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein has come forth in some men that is in its length of five palms and fingers, and it has already reached me that one of twenty palms issued.
And if it is incised at the time of its medication, then insert a probe into its opening and perforate it with a long opening into the body, until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the place for some days with medicines; and then cure it according to the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices come to it, and a branch comes out of each orifice.
And therefore cure it as we have said in the division and with what is said above.
Concerning the incision for worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands near us bovine illness because it often happens to cattle, and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and it goes throughout the body, ascending and descending, and it is felt by its movement from limb to limb until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is by the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms and serpents and earthworms and ascarids occur in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, for when it goes into the body and is elevated to the head, and has reached the eye, then very quickly it opens it and enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure this and extract it, then it is not done except by its movement and appearance felt, and then it is necessary that you tightly bind that which is above it and that which is below it with strong binding and constriction, and then cut over it and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you apply a cautery with fire to the place until it dies, and the greatest cause that is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see it has reached the head near the eye, then bind above the eyebrow with a strong constriction, and then cut above it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use purification of their body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and guard against foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision/operation for the illness nafir (a disease name, likely requiring further context for precise translation).
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in some limbs, and then is moved from limb to limb.
And I have already seen that, as I will recount to you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small swelling in the superficial vein of the arm, and after it was warm for an hour, I saw that swelling move like a worm climbing upwards, but more lightly than one would fear, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and remained there for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then I remembered that it was moving throughout her body, as I had seen; and I was very amazed by its change of location and its speed from limb to limb. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes as clearly as in the woman.
And I thought that this was so because the woman was old, with a dry body and exposed veins, and in this case, that wind-like sensation was easily perceived as it moved.
And it is necessary that it not appear, according to this treatment, in those who are robust and have moist bodies with well-hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, but it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on that place lightly and dryly until that constricted wind-like sensation is expelled, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you treat it with rubbing of the body and with things that expel winds, and drive them out with foul-smelling pills and pills of serapium and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they fall, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and some that have three angles, and some that have barbs; and some that have feathers.
And of the signs of the brain, if an arrow falls into it and pierces the bone and emerges from the cranium which is above the brain, for this causes a sudden and vehement headache, and scotoma (blurred vision), and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasms and confusion of the mind, and vomiting of bile, and quickly speech is lost and the voice is destroyed, and a white humidity resembling pus emerges from the wound, and pus from it similar to the watery substance of flesh.
And therefore, if these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and from the extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, which is near the left nipple, are that it feels as if it were stuck in something hard, and not in something empty; and quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope; and therefore know that death approaches without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are the emission of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck are swollen, and the patient's color is changed, and he breathes with great difficulty, and desires the inhalation of cold air.
And if the arrow falls into the diaphragm which is in the chest, the pain is then near the lower ribs, and the breath is labored with vehement pain and with sighing, and not all the muscles of the limbs are paralyzed.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes out of the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the abdomen, and is stuck in it, some broken tissue, or intestine, comes out of the wound, and there is no hope of cure without extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow falls into the bladder, and urine comes out, and some matter proceeds from it to the outer parts, and there is very quickly pain upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and similar members, are saved according to the better dispositions when the arrow does not find a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you, what I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may signify by this concerning your treatment.
Which is the reason why, in one man, an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye at the root of the nose, and I extracted it from the other side, below the lobe of his ear, and he was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell into him in the lobe of his eye, below the eyelid further below, and the arrow was already hidden and I only found a small extremity with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with an iron head on which there were no barbs.
And the Jew was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and this is the kind that has barbs; and then I incised over it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I was subtle with that, and I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and then, since thirty days had passed and nothing had changed about his condition, I incised over the arrow and I pulled it out, and he was healed, and no harm befell him.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound had healed over him, and since it had been about seven years, the arrow came out at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman into whom an arrow fell in her belly, and the wound is healed within her, and nothing has changed in her condition, and the woman found no hindrance in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his face, and the wound is healed and the arrow broken, finding no great pain, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the man, one of the king's princes, that had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already declined a little to the right side, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his cure about three days after the arrow's impact.
And then I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; and then I examined it with a thin probe, and did not feel it; but he felt a prickling under his right ear, and then I suspected that this prickling was due to the point of the arrow.
And I applied a plaster to the area which has attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the area would suppurate so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could operate on it.
And no significant accident occurred in the area that would indicate the arrow had reached that location; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no accident occurred; and the wound was consolidating meanwhile, and the patient refrained from its extraction for many days until one day he felt the arrow within his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with a probe I examined it, and found the thin point of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I carefully applied the sharp medicine to the opening of the wound until the point of the arrow appeared to me visibly, and I spent time doing this for a period of four months.
And then, once the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and remove it, and I did not succeed in this way, then I did not cease to refine and devise a method for this with ingenious means, until I succeeded one day in extracting it using specially made forceps, as the design of these will be shown at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored the wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, but I restored it and the wound was healed, and the patient was completely cured, and he suffered no ill effects.
And I recount to you the method of extraction of certain arrows so that you may make this a rule and explanation for what has not been said, for the parts of this art and its definitions are not comprehended in narration nor do books contain them.
And skilled artisans compare the small with the large; and the present with the absent; and find new operations and instruments because of the unexpected occurrences of strange causes in the art.
Therefore I say that arrows are not removed from the members in which they are fixed except in two ways: either by traction from the place where they enter; or by counter-traction from the opposite side.
And that which is removed from the place where it entered, is an arrow lodged in fleshy tissue, and it is pulled and removed.
And if it does not yield to such removal at the time it is caught, then it is necessary that you leave it for some days until the flesh around it softens, and its removal and egress are facilitated.
And similarly, if it is fixed in bone and does not yield to removal, then leave it for some days and attempt its removal with traction and movement each day, and it will eventually be removed.
And if it still does not yield after some days, then it is necessary that you perforate the bone around the arrow on all sides with a thin drill until the space around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden in the body and is beyond feeling, then you should probe for it with a probe, and if you feel it, then draw it out with instruments that are suitable for its extraction.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the projectile, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then you should incise the wound until it is widened and it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if it is held by barbs, then you should free the flesh in which it is embedded from all sides with all the skill you can; and work on this, if you cannot, by removing the flesh at the barbs' fracture points and twisting them until they release.
And when you perform the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use twisting with your hand and forceps on all sides until it yields; and proceed with great subtlety so that the arrow is not broken and that its extraction is not made difficult for you.
And if you cannot manage it at that time, then leave it for some days until the parts surrounding it soften, and then try again, for then it will be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then administer the treatment described in the appropriate chapter.
And be careful about your incision of veins or nerves or tendons, and use all possible skill to extract the arrow; and do this with ease, calmness, slowness and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And therefore, make the arrow straight so that the patient is in accordance with the position in which the arrow entered him, for this is more helpful. But if this is not possible for you, then administer what is possible according to the circumstances.
But if the arrows are protruding from the opposite side, or if any part of them projects outwards, or if the end of the arrow causes swelling and protrusion of the part above the bone, then you should cut above it; and let the incision be according to the size that the forceps can grasp; and then pull it out, for it will be easily removed in this way.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then you twist the blade against it until the arrow is released from the bone, and enlarge the opening, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is inside, then you push it through with him/her.
But if the shaft has fallen off and you want to administer an impulse, then you insert, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that the concavity of it fits onto the end of the arrow and then push it through.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then you push it through with a strong instrument entering it, because the arrowhead is relieved by it.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you remove all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you, and then treat it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow falls into the foot, or into the belly, or into the bladder, or into the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with your finger, and it is possible for you to cut around it, then cut around it and guard yourself against cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it, and then sew the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
The form of forceps with which arrows are pulled out:
Let the ends of their jaws be like a bird's beak, which are made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they do not release it.
And of these there are many types, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the size of the wound, and the tightness of the place.
like a ray, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased and it is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the customary number is thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; that is, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called alhasisain; two veins that are in the two apparent temples; and two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes that are called aspicientz; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein that is at the tip of the nose; and two guidegi that are at the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins that are called algelic —; and there are two veins that are under the tongue. But the veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of these, one is the cephalic, and it is near the wild olive; and the people call it "the vein of the head", and it is a hidden vein, and it is a mixed vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilic, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people call that one "vein of the whole body", and the basilic is the vein which is located in the domestic olive, and is called "citillar", and the common people call that one "vein of the belly"; and the funis of the arm, and it is located above the fold, and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the elbow by manifest appearance; and the salvatella, and it is the vein that is between the auricular finger and the little finger, and it has two branches in it.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of these is that which is below the bend of the knee of the wild olive; and the second is the saphena, and its location is below the leg of the domestic olive; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg of the wild olive; and in the leg there are other veins similar to these.
The benefit of the veins near the ears is for chronic catarrhs, migraine, headaches, and old, bad head ulcers.
And the manner of bleeding those veins is as I recount to you: that is, the patient's head should be shaved, and the patient's head should be rubbed in the occiput at the location of both veins with a wide cloth with good friction; then the patient should constrict their neck with a band until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed locations of the head; and seek them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of these with your finger, bleed them there carefully.
Then you take the lancet knife, and it is that which is incisive, and then you insert it under the vein in the elbow until the blood comes out. Then raise your hand with the vein and elbow to the upper parts and incise the vein with an incisive incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two fingers joined, or thereabouts; and let blood flow out as much as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And already and previously at the beginning of the book the incision of these and their cauterization [was described].
But of the two arterial veins that are at the temples, bloodletting is beneficial for chronic and severe migraine, and persistent ophthalmia, and a body of acute superfluities poured into the eyes.
And the way to perform phlebotomy on these is as I have recounted to you: constrict the patient's neck very tightly until the two veins appear to the touch by manifest appearance and their pulse is felt beneath your finger; and then you bleed with a lancet.
Then you raise the skin above the vein at the upper part with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy lancet underneath, and raise the vein upwards; and incise it just as you have done in the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to your need.

Concerning cupping and the qualities of its use.
Cupping glasses are made of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one is with scarification and blood extraction, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And the cupping glasses which are administered with scarification and bloodletting are applied to fourteen places on the body, of which one location where cupping glasses are placed is at the nape of the neck, and it is the back part of the head; and the middle of the neck on both sides together; and cupping glasses on the chin below the jaw, below the mouth; and cupping glasses on the shoulder blades; and cupping glasses on the armpit, and it is above the end of the rib; and cupping glasses on the flanks, and they are on the sides of the body; and cupping glasses on the hips; and cupping glasses on the thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not diminish the strength through them, as is the case with phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity dictates cupping for illness or for a continuous condition, administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at any time.
Therefore, when you see any of those conditions, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we order cupping to be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it is applied it relieves the heaviness in the head and that which is affecting the eyes; but this should be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not advisable to use it on someone of cold complexion, or in whom there is catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a person.
And therefore, it is not advisable to use this on the elderly, and on those whose heads suffer from cold illnesses.
But to one on whom this is continuously applied, forgetfulness is caused; and therefore you should instruct the cupper to place his hand lightly over the cupping area below, to prevent the generation of forgetfulness.
But cupping of the armpit is in place of phlebotomy of the dark vein (vena fusca) and the basilic vein: and therefore it is beneficial for asthma, and for the constriction of the throat, and for the rupture of the throat's instrument (presumably the windpipe or larynx), and for coughs, and for repletion (fullness).
And it is necessary that the armpit cupping be done slightly higher, for if it is placed lower down, it produces weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping of the temples benefits pains in the head, ophthalmia (eye inflammation), migraine, suffocation, and toothache; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press his hand on the scarification, lest he injure the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits canker sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases of the mouth, and it is in place of phlebotomy of the arteries that are in the two lips.
But cupping that is done on the two shoulder blades benefits catarrh, which is caused by an excess of heat (or "fulness of heat").
And cupping on the two inner parts of the thighs is helpful instead of phlebotomy of three veins, that is, the basilic, median cubital (fusca - this is a less certain translation, representing a dark vein; the median cubital is a likely candidate), and cephalic veins; because it draws blood from all those smaller veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins, until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed too deeply in scarification, because the place is devoid of much flesh, and there are nerves and arteries beneath.
And it is a cupping glass with which one cups above the buttocks and it benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that it should be a large cupping glass, and that it should be of bronze, because the location requires strong suction; and thin cupping glasses are quickly broken and should be scarified with scarification.
But cupping of the legs is less preferable for evident depletion, because they draw blood from the whole body, and they benefit old pains of the joints and of the womb, and of the bladder; and they provoke menstruation, and benefit a boil and swelling; and they stand in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and the femoral veins, and of the two iliac veins.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause fainting in many men.
And of the femoral vein cupping, the benefit is similar to the benefit of leg cupping.
And the quality of the placement of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw by moderate suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place those lightly, and remove those carefully, and that the humors may come to the place by equal admixture. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the redness of blood appear.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and then consider the disposition of the bodies.
Because that man who is of tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And command the cupper to amplify the scarification and probe a little, and temper the suction with facility.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary that you scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened to the blood and to its wateriness. But in the second [scarification], to the scrutiny acquired from the extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let a third scarification be made so that it achieves the latter [desired outcome].
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a little, one scarification suffices for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with a deep scarification.
And the term scarification in which there is the depth of the lancet only.
And if the one for whom the lancet is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are tight, it is necessary that the places of the cupping glasses be anointed with lenitive, aperient, and resolvent ointments; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, violet oil, sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or blood oil, and those similar.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then the oil should be amaranth oil, balm oil, aloe oil, or anise oil, and those similar.
But if the patient has large pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be prohibited, and for such patients, the areas for cupping should be washed with vinegar or cold water of solanum, or with poppy water, or with borrage water, and similar things.
And the one whose blood is of much humidity, wash their cupping areas with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar things.
And for the one whose superfluities are thick, wash their cupping areas with old wine, or with wormwood water (which is majoram), or with a decoction of anise or chamomile, and similar things.
And the cupping should be done before a bath; however, it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and the patient should sleep after the cupping.
What is it that the patient undergoing cupping or phlebotomy should observe before cupping?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: if the patient to be cupped or phlebotomized is choleric and has sharp blood and inflammation, then he should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, endive in sour wine and lettuce, and succory, and julep, and similar things.
And give him foods such as chicken, and the meat of domestic birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and the like.
And for one whose complexion is cold, he should drink syrup of almonds, or syrup of succory seeds, and sumac, and very fragrant wine, mid-way between old and new; and he should be advised to use moderation in drinking and eating, and give him foods such as chicken, hempseed, peas, small birds, and pigeons.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and phlebotomy his drink should be greater than his food.
And it is necessary that at certain times some men be given theriac alpharac, or medicines of musk, that is, nutmeg, or atherita, before cupping or before phlebotomy, or afterwards, so that the principal members may be comforted and the blood may be thinned; and they should not take those things when heated.
But the cuppings that are without scarification should be placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the places of the knees, and the groin, because these members do not tolerate scarification on them.
And it is not desired for these, but the drawing of blood from a member, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in the illness of nasal bleeding; and we use it so that cold, invisible flatulence in the member may be dissolved, just as we place it on the belly and the navel; for it rarefies the member and warms it and diminishes the pain because it resolves flatulence.
And it is placed on boils when they become obstructed or suppurated, for by the virtue of strong attraction it quickly opens the obstruction and eradicates the pus from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of enclosed flatulence in them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction alone; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of tepid water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger; that is, hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Method of cupping administered with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, according to how we have formed it; and let the length of the cup be half a palm; and let it be placed on the patient's side, in a place that is within the circumference, and half of it; the hole should be small enough that only a needle can enter it.
Let it be made of bronze, copper, or citrus wood, with smooth edges, polished, even, and smooth, so that it does not harm the limb upon its placement.
And let there be a transverse rod of bronze or iron in the middle of it, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and thin people are different from those for young people and those with large bodies.
And the way to place this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to light a small amount of wood fire decently before a small wax candle, and place this on the middle of the transverse rod in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the area underneath, so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then let it be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger on the hole we have mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glasses and do not need to remove the finger and the vapor escapes through that hole, then the cupping glass is immediately dissolved (i.e., the suction is released).
And then ignite the fire according to its manner, and repeat it if you need to.
But a cupping glass that is used in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse rod or hole, and is not used unless it is filled with water, and is placed on the limb alone.
And this is its form: And this cupping glass, the larger it is, the better it is, because it can receive a large quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where the application of cupping glasses is not possible because of their small size, such as the fingers and gums and similar parts; or on a limb lacking flesh, such as the fingers and nose and similar parts.
And the quality of using those leeches is that you should find leeches that are in clean, sweet waters of a spring.
Then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and so that no matter remains in their bellies; and then let the body be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it reddens; and then let them be placed upon it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if it is possible to draw blood from the area with a cupping glass, it is even more beneficial afterward.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with warm water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged after the application of leeches, and it oozes, then dip a cloth in cold water, and place that on top until the oozing stops.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle on it crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, until the bleeding stops; or place halved, cooked beans on the area, and leave them until the beans stick to the place, and the bleeding stops.
And it is necessary, if needed, to reapply leeches, when it is possible to change those.
And if leeches are prohibited due to a suspension [of bloodletting], then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood comes out; and then they may be applied, because when they sense any blood they immediately attach themselves.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle a little crushed aloes, or salt, or ash on them, and they will fall off immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
O, son, know that foolish physicians and the vulgar boast of this chapter, those who never looked at the books of the Ancients or read a single letter of them.
And for this reason, this method of the learned in our land is kept private; and I have never forgotten this at all; and I have not acquired this except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study of their meaning, until I extracted the science from them.
And then I applied myself to experimentation and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter all that my knowledge contains and upon which my experience has passed; since I have abbreviated it for you and freed it from the burden of length; and I have abbreviated it for the sake of brevity; and I have shown it for the sake of clarity; and I have shown in it many forms of instruments that are used in it, as is done by way of explanation, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter One.
Summary and collections concerning the treatment of bone fractures, those things which are necessary to be considered before we begin the discussion of broken limbs and their various types. Therefore, I will say in the prologue of this chapter the main points of the discourse; and the chapters in understanding which the practitioner is first obliged to stand upon the truth of those things; that is, you and those who are prompt to know or learn this noble art, different from you.
And therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall happens to someone, then you should first attend to it with phlebotomy or purging, or both together, if no cause prohibits it, such as weakness, or if the one to whom it happened is a child, or an old person in decrepitude, or it is a time of intense heat, or intense cold.
And furthermore, let him be content with a light diet of fresh herbs and the flesh of birds and rabbits; and we prohibit rich meats and filling foods, and all food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of avoiding a hot abscess, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his former regimen, to which he is accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed nutritious foods, very rich and heavy in nourishment, in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads [possibly referring to specific cuts of meat], and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and things similar to those, and with this regimen the consolidation of the fracture is made more easily and better.
And know that fractured bones, when they are in men who are in their first old age and in the decrepit, cannot be rejoined and consolidated according to their nature because of the desiccation of their limbs.
However, nature adjusts and brings to the broken bone from every part some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and binds it until they are together conglutinated and tied together, until they reach the end of strength and firmness, like fire at the beginning, until nothing prevents it from its work.
And for this reason it is necessary that you give the patient foods in which there is heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other limbs are different from each other.
And I come with a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinct from the others.
Many varieties of fracture in itself. And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there is an arched fracture, besides those which happen in the stars; and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the shaft and splinters and additions proceeding and not proceeding, just as is done in the quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to each type there is an appropriate device or treatment in its restoration, as will be recounted separately in their respective places.
Many signs of bone fracture. And thus a bone fracture is known: by its crookedness, and by that prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and by it when we compress it with the hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is this felt by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
Having said that, it is possible that there is a crookedness or small fissure; in that case, it is not fitting that you move it with your hand and with complete compression; rather, place upon it those medicines of which mention will be made nearby at the appropriate moment; and then let it be tightened with a subtle bandaging.
And know that if the place is curved and is not split in two or another thing that does not happen to it,  as if the whole part were loosened from its comparison, then you must begin at your hour with its rectification before an abscess happens to it through heat.
And if a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for some days until the abscess ceases; and then treat it according to whatever manner you can with lightness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration and healing of it is easier than of a bone in which a wound has occurred.
And then tighten it as the recollection/understanding of it comes.
But if there is a wound in the bone, then extension of that broken limb is necessary from two parts, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords or with one cord and your hand.
And be in a position with which you place the limb in such a way as to treat it according to its natural form, while the parts of the broken limb are extended, then it is necessary that you make those additions return with ingenuity and in any way you can to their places.
And take care, according to your strength, that no pain or injury happens to the patient because of your operation; and it is necessary according to your ability that you apply one bone to the other, its companion, according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that time you profitably touch that with your hand, and if you see something different here, rectify that and adjust it to the extent of your ability.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish doctors do, who many times cause a hot abscess by their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen this from their operations many times.
And the quality of the constriction of the quieted limb is that which I have recounted; so that you know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their greatness and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the penis and similar to those, then it is necessary that the wrapping be of very fine cloth.
But those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, it is necessary that in those there be a broad and firm wrapping, because the broad bandage connects the large member and tightens it on all sides with even constriction, so that no blemish enters it.
And then begin, properly, your equalization evacuation, placing a bandage suitable for this, on the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings, according to what the member requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then come with that to the most superior part of the place of the fracture, and tighten less than you did your constriction with which you tightened the place of the fracture. And then lengthen yourself with a wrapping of the place of the fracture a little, and soften the constriction little by little, until you take from the place a large part of it.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with a wrapping, and then come with the wrappings to the lower part of the fracture; and let your operation be in the tightening of the wrapping and in the softening of it according to what we have said in the first wrapping above.
And then place between the layers of the bandage linen or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is padded, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over it, and then wrap over these bandages well-made splints immediately, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the place, and then place over it that which dries the abscess and removes the inflammation; and leave that for a few days; and then you tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be of half a cane, smooth and well-formed skillfully; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over the fracture be a little thicker and smoother than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to how it fits the limb in size; and then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then bind over it with thread properly as we have said of tightening, and it is that your tightening should be greater according to the place of the fracture, and the more it is distant from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen by the operation of some fools placing their threads of twisted rope, and it is a great error, when the tightening lacks equality.
And likewise, very fine threads are not suitable, because you will not be able to achieve with them what you want by tightening, and it is not necessary that between splint and splint there should be less than a finger's width.
And if the patient is harmed by the ends of the splints near the bandages in those places, then place under those places tow, lion-wool, or carded wool until it does not cause him injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, and I will return with a discussion of that in the following chapter according to its particularities.
And know that not every broken limb is bound with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that, if the limb is large, it is not necessary to place splints on it until about five or seven days, or more, depending on your certainty of the development of a hot abscess.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients restored [limbs], which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
