A method of common plaster, a shortened restoration of fractures, suitable for many complexions and especially for women and children, because it is of that which neither heat nor cold overcomes:  it is that you take the powder of the mill, which is the fine flour dust that falls from the movement of the millstone on the walls of the mill.
And make it thus as it is, except that you sieve it with egg white, and make its consistency neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of plaster restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of ladanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and of wild pomegranate root, of each one dram (x); of myrrh and aloes, half a dram (v); of aloes wood, two drams (xx); all should be crushed and sifted and mixed with aloe water or with egg white if the patient's complexion is hot; and then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores lightly broken bones, and benefits many people.
Another method of plaster which benefits fractures and sprains: take of wild pomegranate root and of white marshmallow root, one dram (x); of myrrh and of aloes, half a dram (v); of acacia, six drams (vi); of Armenian bole, two drams (xx).
All should be crushed in a mortar with much grinding, and be sifted, and be mixed with water or with egg white and be applied.
Method for a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and if pain occurs in those and in fissures and fractures: let unwashed wool be taken and be submerged in sour wine and cooked oil and be placed on the area.
However, this plaster does not have restorative virtue, rather it draws out hot abscesses and expels pain effectively.
Another method of plaster that restores a broken bone: take leaves of wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; crush everything and make a plaster from them.
Method of a brief plaster administered for the restoration of bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remaining abscess: take roots of mallow, of chamomile, and violet flowers, of pea flour, of all an equal part; let all be crushed and be mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then mix that with moistened coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method of plaster stronger in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb: take roots of mallow and linseed, and pomegranate, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of all an equal part; let all be crushed and mixed with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the restoration of its color.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration except a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommended that it be of medium thickness.
But in Gaul it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the things which cause desiccation with little heat are such as myrrh, incense, and similar things to those we have mentioned.
Method for a plaster that relieves injury and pain: take millet,  wax, shaved human hair or bird down, and mallow, and salt, in equal parts; let them be crushed and sifted and mixed, and let a plaster be made.
But the amount of time the bandage should remain and when it should be loosened is that you consider whether pain or itching has occurred, or if the bone is broken or dislocated; if not, then you do not loosen it for many days. And if vehement pain or itching or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should carefully loosen or untie it immediately, and remove the plaster from it.
And take linen cloth and a moist sea sponge, and submerge it in lukewarm water, and with it wash the area until the itching ceases and the pain is soothed; then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place over it clean wool submerged in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it for a night until you are certain of a hot abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb subsides, and its pain is eased.
Then repeat that subtle binding and small plaster and do not tighten it as much as your first constriction, but refine it until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, the redness, the pain, the inflammation, and all the symptoms have subsided completely, and you wish to remove the plaster and binding, then you repeat this as you did at the beginning, similarly.
And if none of the conditions we have mentioned occurs in the limb, then do not untie it before three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days, depending on what appears to you from the unbinding of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh upon it.
And then adjust the binding more than in your first binding, and adjust it to the thickening of the patient's flesh, as his recovery progresses.
But if you see the fracture site drying and thinning more than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore, bathe it with lukewarm water every time you untie it; and let this be done every three days; and loosen the binding a little, and by this operation, nourishment will flow to the limb, and it will heal easily.
But what foolish restorers of a fractured limb do, when it is not set correctly at the beginning, and is set crookedly, is an error according to their practice, and a great danger; and if this were correct, the Ancients would have enlarged upon it in their books and would have worked with it.
And I have not seen any of them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable not to do it.
Chapter Two: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow from a sword, and the blow crushes all the bone into the marrow, which is beneath the bone, just as it does to wood, and for this reason this type is called an axial fracture; or the sword cuts some part of the bone, and cuts its flesh, but the cut does not go through to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called a *levatio absoluta* (complete elevation/separation).
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small; and of a fracture that is a contusion or attrition; and its cause is a blow with a stone or a fall onto a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the membrane that is beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone, as fine as a small hair, and for that reason this type is called a capillary fracture.
And there is a fracture that is caused either by a fall or by a blow with a stone and the like, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter the inner parts, and is made in a concave place, as happens to the sides of a ram's horn when a blow is struck and a part of it is driven into the inner parts.
And this happens especially in the bones of humid bodies, such as the heads of infants.
And in all these types of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will return all of this to its place.
And we recognize all these types of fracture by detection of them and by examination with probes, and by the presence of corrupted flesh that is over them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is uncovered on the bone, and is wiped clean, and then examined with a probe, for the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider first the patient's symptoms, and then if you see symptoms that clearly indicate danger, such as vomiting of bile, exhaustion, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, swelling of the eyes and redness of them, and similar symptoms in the worst cases, without doubt it is not healing.
And if you see no unusual symptoms and expect recovery, then you begin his treatment.
The reason is that if the injured person comes to you at the beginning as injured, and this is in the winter days, then you should study the state of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to all circumstances.
And if it is in the summer days, then you should try to set the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone is corrupted by the panniculus (flesh), and before the complications that we have mentioned occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and is with small, fragmented pieces and attrition, then you must incise the comminuted and atrophied part, as I shall explain to you.
And so you shave the head of the injured patient, and expose the bone according to custom and manner as best you can, according to the nature of the injury, and that it be light upon the patient.
And if you encounter, during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you must oppose it with what is necessary; and this is to fill the area with cloths soaked in rose wine and oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is settled and the bleeding is stopped; and then begin with the encirclement of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow scalpel, and this is its shape:  And then use another scalpel, a little wider than that one; and this is its shape:
And then use a wider scalpel than the second.
And it is necessary that you have several scalpels of different sizes, some wider than others; and some of them shorter than others, and sharpened at their ends, and made of Indian or tempered steel.
And use them carefully, striking the scalpel so that you do not strike the head and injure it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you must perforate its circumference before you use the scalpels with drills, which are called shallow drills.
And they are not called so except because these do not reach the depth of the skull bone which is near it, because the drill has a round end or edge below its sharp head, similar to an edge or a small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each suited to the thickness of each bone, so that you have at hand for all parts of the skull a drill whose point is sharp, the length and shortness being according to the thickness of that part of the skull.
And this is the manner of the three types of drills:  The method of perforating the circumference of the broken bone is to place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone is penetrated; then move the drill to another location, placing the distance between each hole to be the thickness of a finger or thereabouts.
And then incise with scalpels what is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as possible for ease of removal, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument that you have prepared for this, such as fine tongs and forceps.
And it is necessary that you take great care that the scalpel does not touch anything of the brain.
And so, when you have encircled the bone by perforation, and it is separated from the brain if it is continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you scrape and smooth the roughness of that which remains in the bone with another instrument similar to a scalpel, but it must be thinner and more delicate than the other scalpels.
And if any small and jagged pieces of bone remain, then remove them easily with whatever instrument you have; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments as we have described.
And the other method of operation is easier and less frightening, which Galen described and praised greatly; and this is his account: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin by uncovering the part of the bone at the location where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until, when you have uncovered that part and placed under it the extremity of that slow and gentle scalpel, of which this is the form:
And it is so that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is divided in two on the side facing forward in length, like a wedge; so that the lenticular part may be applied to the brain with its face, while the sharp part cuts into the bone.
And then strike the scalpel on one side with a small mallet until you incise all the bone easily as it is revealed, and you are sure that the periosteum will not suffer any accident that you fear in any way, even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, even if he were sleeping.
Then, if any substance attached to the bone remains in any place on the periosteum, incise it with the lenticular end of that scalpel, and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate the periosteum, and what is cut away or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughness and small splinters remain, then it is necessary to smooth that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, of which you have prepared several, of different sizes, so that it is possible for you to apply in each place the most suitable and the most appropriate of them, according to what your operation on that broken bone and its shape requires.
And you should begin your rasping of the bone with the coarsest of these rasps, and then gradually use the finer ones, and continue doing this until you end by using the finest and most subtle of them all.
But in all other small, hairline fractures and subtle fractures, care must be taken according to what is needed for their repair.
And it is a matter that is not overlooked by all those who have studied that art. And this is what we have shown above in the case of large fractures.
And if the panniculus (skin and subcutaneous tissue) is retracted when you reduce the bone, then you should take a cloth according to the size of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; and then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in wine and rose oil and place it over the first cloth, doing this as gently as you can so as not to compress the wound; and then apply a linen bandage, not tightening it except so much as to hold the wound in place only.
And leave it with this dressing for one or two days until you are sure there is no hot abscess; and then unbind it and apply medicines that counteract excessive dryness, such as linseed, barley flour, and powder of lentils and birthwort, and similar things.
And it is necessary that you make a powder of these medicines to be sprinkled on the wound, once they are dry; and generally, apply any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not bite (i.e., cause further damage).
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment remains clean; and that there is no filth of ointment or anything else; and do not let virulence increase there; because when it is applied to the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts and rots it, and this malignancy will then affect the patient.
And if it affects the cranial cavity, as the bone is cleaned, and especially when its treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if the patient suffers the consequences we have mentioned, know that it is undoubtedly pernicious.
And if the blackness is not caused by the medicine applied, but is due to the medicine's effect, then you should take one part honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these, moisten a cloth, and apply it to the cranial cavity; and then treat it with appropriate healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of a broken nose.
Know that a broken nose is not broken into two parts except the most prominent ones, or one of these sometimes, because it has two bones, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and only suffers abrasion, crookedness and inflammation.
But if one of its two parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the nose, and reduce the fracture inside with your index finger, and with the thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause the patient pain.
And if the fracture is in the most prominent part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it should be set with the end of a rod of small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same thing.
And strive to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the seventh or tenth day, you should treat the resulting abscess with cautery.
And then introduce into the end of the nose a pledget of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or two pledgets, if the fracture is in two parts, and let the pledget be of such a size as to fill the hole in the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the pledgets should be soaked in vinegar, and changed every day; but it is not so in my experience; rather it is necessary that the pledget be soaked in egg white mixed with powdered myrrh; and then leave the pledget there until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a pledget, a goose feather, wrapped in linen cloth, is introduced into the nose; and this is especially useful when the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, use pledgets.
And if a hot abscess should occur again, plaster the nose with cerate or with cotton soaked in sharp vinegar and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to plaster that area externally with sesame flour and powdered incense mixed with egg white; and then place linen cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then it is necessary to make an incision over them, and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then close the incision, and treat it with that which causes granulation and consolidation, and with plasters suitable for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then it is necessary to treat it with pledgets and administer lead canulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external, and is not broken in two and is bent inwards at the edges, then its diagnosis is easy.
And it is necessary therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and push with it the hump of the fracture inwards, easily to the outside parts, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; thus decently equalize that.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from two sides according to straightness until its equalization is possible.
And if there is concussion of teeth with disruption, then bind those which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or silk; and then place on the broken jaw a cerate, and then place on it a doubled cloth, and place on the cloth a well-made large splint, or a piece of wood equal to the length of the jaw; and then bind that on top as the binding appears suitable to you and as the application requires, until it is healed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him sorbitive drinks as food.
And if you estimate that the shape is already altered in some way, then you should examine and unbind it on the third day; and then rectify what is altered in it and plaster it with powdered barley, with egg white, or with bean flour, after you have removed the cerate from it, and place wool over the plaster.
And then, when this plaster is attached and adjusted, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it; do not unbind it until it is healed and the fracture is firm; for many times this fracture is healed in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and that abscess is softened.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of the bone is separated or splintered; then you should carefully remove those splinters with whatever is appropriate for removing them from the wound.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a fleam, according to your need.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you should sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters which is suitable for this and will cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken in the front part above the muscle, and this fracture is done in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that splinters occur in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the splint and aligns the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
Then bind the fracture with your fingers until its shape is made as it should be, and there is neither little prominence nor concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, and let its size be according to the amount of your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the pad with your hand until you bind the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle to the outer parts, because it is stuck to the sternum, then the patient should be supine on their neck, and place on the muscle a moderately sized cushion.
And the assistant presses the muscle downwards until the bone of the clavicle that is on the sternum above is elevated, and then you straighten the fracture and bind it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are harmful, then you should incise over it and extract those splinters easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you make an incision with one of the scalpels which you have prepared for this, after having placed under the clavicle the instrument that serves the bandage; and this is a wooden or iron instrument of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size or smallness of the bone.
But its length should be as long as is possible for you in the operation; let it have two ends, as you see, and let one end be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure of a hot abscess, then you join the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then dip a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and wine, and place it on that.
And above all, let the tablet not be moved from over the broken bone; and for that reason you must see the patient each day, because when the bandage is softened or you see the tablet is already moved, then you rectify that and tighten the bandage.
And then place the patient's pillow under their neck, and place a small cushion under their shin at night on top of the pillow; so that with this the limb is elevated from that side, and thus the fracture of the clavicle is elevated from the muscle; or let their arm be tied to their neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless a boil or abscess occurs at the site, before twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see the need, and adjust the reduction and restore it and leave it until the fracture of the clavicle is restored and coagulated.
And it is strengthened and healed in about twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken on its lateral side, and not broken through, but only at the edges.
And therefore, when the shoulder blade is broken — and it is broken in the middle of the shoulder blade — and is known only by touch, then, according to the nature of the fracture,  forcefully prepare it and reduce it to its natural shape in every way possible to you.
Then place on the area powdered  millet with egg white and lion's fluff; then place a dusting of doubled cloth; and then place over it a finely woven linen bandage, according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or a little more.
But if the bandage leaves a concavity in any part of the shoulder blade, then you should fill that concavity with lion's fluff, and then tighten the bandage evenly.
And then bind it above with a long bandage, with decent constriction and firmness of the bandage until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the bandage each day; and whenever it is loosened, bandage it again and tighten it, and reposition the bandage if it has shifted from its place.
And lay the patient on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And so when that number of days is complete, then you unbind the bandage, and you should know for certain that this is for bones whose beginning is not feared, nor their dissolution.
And if it proceeds from the location of the collarbone and extends under the armpit, then you incise over it, and remove it, and act according to what we have said in the fracture of the clavicle, with cauterization of the abscess if it occurs there.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this heals slowly; but its extremities heal much faster, even if they are crushed and comminuted.
And among its accidents, when the middle of the foot is broken, it inclines inwards, and causes great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, strong fever, and vomiting blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is evident and not hidden from the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient should be supine on his back, with a cushion placed between his shoulders; then press on the muscles of the foot, and align the bones with hands on both sides, being subtle in this operation and in the setting, in every way possible and appropriate, until the bone is restored to its proper shape.
Then apply a plaster and lint, and place over it a splint of thin willow or alder wood, or something similar in lightness, after wrapping it in cloth; and be careful in binding it over the broken bone so that it is not disturbed; and bind it circularly to the instep sometimes, and tighten it with a suitable bandage; and check the bandage frequently, and tighten it when it loosens.
And if necessity demands loosening of the bandage because of corrosion or itching, or pain, or an abscess that forms in the area, then carefully unbind it, remove the plaster, and correct what has occurred with appropriate treatment; and reapply the plaster, if it is appropriate; and adjust the bandage until it heals.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs only occurs in the thicker parts, which are two; and in their extremities, on the front part, no crushing occurs because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the feeling/sense through its examination with the fingers.
And the restoration should be thus:  equalize the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until it is aligned as needed, and then apply a plaster and tightly bind the broken bone with a bandage, if that is needed.
And if the rib fracture is inclined towards the inner parts, then the patient experiences vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that occurs in one who has pleurisy, and this is because the bone pierces the membrane; and it causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the Ancients were ingenious in that [treatment] with many inventions.
And of them it is said: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that create inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we would avoid this so that it does not easily cause a hot abscess if it does not occur; and if it does occur, then it helps with that.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be applied and that it should draw strongly; and this seems reasonable, but nevertheless it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the place because of weakness.
And some of them said: the place should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage is tight as it wraps that roundness; and then the patient should be treated with a treatment for pleurisy, with diet and medicine.
And if a vehement cause constricts the patient, which he cannot bear, and the bone pushes the covering with a painful puncture, threatening the patient, then it is necessary to cut open the place, uncover the broken rib, and then place on it the instrument that preserves the diaphragm (whose mention was given previously), and that we incise the bone easily, and extract it.
And then we adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitches, and we treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then you should take and immerse powders in rose oil and place it on the area, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him be supine on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and it is slow to heal, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider this: for if you see his hands already softened and numb and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend nor clench them, and when you squeeze them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he is not healed, and in most cases, it is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels constriction in them with the fingers and puncture, then know that the neck of the bone is safe and that the patient will heal with treatment.
And if the same thing happens to the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if he will heal or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, the same thing has happened to them as happened to the hands, and moreover when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and defecation without his will, and sometimes he lies on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not labor in his cure.
But if none of those things occur, the condition is better and milder.
And the cure for him is that you carefully pierce the abscess to settle it,  placing on the affected vertebra only pure rose oil, or with cooked egg yolks; apply this several times a day until the hot abscess is settled.
And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it tightly; and instruct the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where he feels pain until he is healed.
And if, due to the attrition of the bone, splinters or something that is already separated from it occur, then you must cut it at the bone and remove that bone; and then approximate the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment, until it is healed.
But if the posterior part of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the [thigh] bone, then you must insert the little finger of your left hand into the rectum, and set the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by counter-extension; and then apply a plaster and splint, if you have them, and then bind it tightly.
And if you feel broken splinters in it, then you should probe there, and remove them, and treat the wound with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the hip.
The hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are broken, then their fracture is only a separation at the ends of those bones and is incised in length and inclines towards the inner parts; and the patient experiences pain and throbbing in the area, and the leg is numb when fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is its shape.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you bind that according to what is prepared for you by equal binding until it is similar to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or inclines towards the lower parts, then you make the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared by you. And then when you have bound it, place a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or bone over that, and bind it tightly so that you do not fear puncture of the fracture or displacement of the splint.
And bind the concavity with something that fills it until it takes the binding equally, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is settled, and place on it that which settles it as is preceded; and then return to its restoration, and bind it as is necessary.
And if splinters occur in the bone or are comminuted, then you remove them.
Chapter 11.
Of the restoration of a fractured humerus (or upper arm bone).
The humerus is that which is between the side and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a wooden splint of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at two extremities, and then let it be suspended from a raised position.
And seat the patient on a stool; and then lay their broken arm on the splint until it reaches the elbow, in the middle of which the curve of the splint should be attached.
And then suspend some heavy weight from it, or let the attendant extend it downwards; and then let the physician join the fracture with his hands, until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other method is that the patient be laid supine on their back and suspend the arm from their neck with a bandage; and then order two assistants that one of them hold what is above the fracture with their hands; and the other hold the lower part; and each of them extend to their part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten above the fracture with a bandage and above that with another bandage, and each of the two assistants extend to their two parts.
And so if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the elbow and the other below the fracture near the joint.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that place and over that joint.
Equalize that with ease and without effort, and then when it is equalized as needed and is applied with a good application, then tighten it unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day, and place on it clean wool soaked in sharp wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten that.
And the way to tighten it is that you place a plaster on the fracture, and then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your open hand over its muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm on the support so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is so that you place on the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is so that your bandage over the fracture site should be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you do [proceed].
And if you fear a hot abscess, then you leave the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then consider the bandages in all parts for three days so that swelling or inflammation does not occur at the site; or it is forbidden for food to reach the limb due to the constriction of the bandage; and then rectify all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage until three days or more.
And let the patient's sleeping position be on their back, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place over the splint a poultice full of wool, evenly spread, and check every hour of the night and day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is softened;  then rectify this all with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until the binding begins; and then it is necessary to increase their food.
Because it is customary for the splint and their leg to be bound for forty days; and then it is necessary that they be unbound and a bath be administered, and it should be medicated with ointments that rectify this.
And if the fracture is badly crushed, then you should not unbind the bandage and splints from that for ten days or up to two months.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the little finger, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the little finger, on the lower side.
And very easily the larger focil is broken alone, or the smaller one, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small focil is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the focil is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the small focil above, then it is convenient for the physician, during its restoration, to apply slight extension easily until it is set.
But if the large focil is what is broken, then it is necessary to apply more forceful extension.
And if both focils are broken together, then you must apply the extension much more strongly.
And you must place your hand in position during its restoration and extension stretched out on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be at the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be lying on a square cushion.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer strain.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should carefully manipulate the bone until he reduces the fracture to the best possible alignment.
And if in the fracture of the bone there is a piece displaced, then you must forcefully reduce all parts to their place with your skill.
And if a separated fragment appears in him, that pushes the bone, and there is no hope of its restoration, then you operate on him and remove that piece according to the method which we have described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already dealt with that in a separate chapter, and therefore take care of it accordingly.
And if an abscess occurs near the beginning of your treatment, then you apply a cloth with a plaster made of rose oil and white wax, and let it be of moderate consistency, neither too thick nor too thin, and bind the cloth tightly over it until the abscess subsides; and then remove the plaster and apply a poultice prepared from crushed barley flour with egg white, and then apply a bandage; and the bandage which is applied over the fracture should be tighter and stronger.
And know that the number of bandages on the arm varies depending on the situation, whether the fracture is in one or two places simultaneously.
Then apply your bandage over the fracture site firmly and tightly, and where the bandage is closer to the parts above the fracture or to the lower parts of it, apply the bandage a little more loosely, as was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is bound should be of moderate thickness, neither too fine nor too coarse, as we have already recommended.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any problem arises that requires correction, such as itching that occurs in the limb, then you should bathe the limb with warm water until that itching ceases.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the constriction is already softened and the bone is already healed and similar to the others, then correct all this with your skill.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandage, for then you must loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient does not experience any of the problems we have mentioned, then the bandage should not be loosened for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then the patient's arm should be suspended by its own weight, allowing movement, and have him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or even quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the constitution and the strength of the individual.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures rarely occur in the wrist and finger joints; instead, they often suffer attrition.
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then the patient should lie down, and a cushion should be placed beneath him for support, and then place his hand on that cushion.
Then the assistant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should align them until they are joined with a good connection.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless a hot abscess occurs; and then apply a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and wrap it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to grasp it with his fractured hand; then it should be bound tightly with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather with cloth in which it is softened, so that the leather may be applied to the palm; and bind it as tightly as necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a bandage above and another bandage below on the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, just as the hand is bound to the splint between the fingers with bandages.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the finger joints, then if it is the thumb, it should be straightened as necessary and then bound to the palm, and if you wish, place a small, straight splint on it so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index finger, or in the ring finger, or in the little finger, then it should be straightened and bound to the finger next to it; or they should all be bound together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your treatment, and alongside it, the disposition of a hot abscess and oppose—with your operation—what is necessary when any of these things occur, according to the repeated account of them.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and this is revealed by the feeling, according to whether it is broken in the front part or the more prominent part.
And its treatment is done in this way: a bandage is tightened above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient is laid on their stomach and face.
And then the physician stretches the entire bandage to its proper length, according to the nature of the fracture, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the hip joint, then the bandage should be close to the knee on the hamstring side, so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then the bandage should be placed near the knee, and the extension should be towards the lower parts.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both hands until he reduces it to its natural shape and the bone is joined by a good conjunction; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs, then you leave it for a few days until the abscess has matured and subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made as follows: you must wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and double or triple it, removing any excess; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the hip joint.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the thigh and the leg near the knee on the lower parts, and let the ends of the cloth come from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the leg and thigh what is superfluous of the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is even and secure.
Then begin with a firm bandage in the middle, over the fracture site, with three or four turns; and the farther you get from the fracture site, the looser, lighter, and softer your bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the cloth to the lower parts until they reach the ankle, and tie both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not loosened from its place.
And then leave the bandage on it, such that it does not cause any corrosion, hot abscess, swelling, or similar problems to the limb; and if any of these problems occur, then loosen the bandage and correct whatever occurred, as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone that is sticking out of the bone, then you must set it as best you can; and if not, smooth it down and keep it in place, and treat it with what has been previously mentioned until it heals.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened in addition to the splints being applied, as we have described for the aid of the arm.
However, in the restoration done in this manner, lameness does not occur in the patient; and if it is restored alone, apart from the other leg being applied to it, then it is necessary that he will limp on the affected leg always.
And know that the thigh is bound for forty days, or a little more or a little less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions (of the patient).
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but often causes attrition (or wearing away).
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and on this everything depends, along with sensation (or feeling).
And its restoration is thus: that which is broken of its parts should be aligned with the said parts until it is joined and united as much as possible by equalization, ease, and fabrication (or setting).
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you have one; and bind it tightly above with a suitable bandage.
And then consider all the measures which we have recommended in other fractures, such as a warm poultice and similar treatments, so that all complications are countered with what is suitable for him until he is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is thick, and is named by the name of the tibia (shinbone); and the other is slender, and is named fibula.
And the same types of fractures occur to each of these bones of the leg as occur to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is the same.
And if the two bones are fractured together, the leg is deformed in all parts.
And if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is deformed in the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is fractured, you will find that below, and it is one of those things that are not easily amenable to treatment.
Then administer extension, reduction (setting), and ligation of the splints according to that same method.
However, if the leg fracture is extensive, having many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and alleviate it with your skill.
And in the leg, there is a certain operative procedure similar to that for the arm.  When you set the splints and complete your operation, you will then take pieces of pine wood which are placed beneath the bandages that are placed on the soles.  They are made expressly from palm branches; choose those of small thickness, not the thin ones, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the very bottom.
Then wrap around each (leg) a cloth in two layers according to its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side, and let them extend from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then tie the two splints in three places: at the two ends and in the middle.  For with this structure, the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains in a good position.
And now a small wooden splint, according to the length of the leg, has been prepared; and let the leg be placed on it so that it retains that (proper) position.
And especially it is necessary that you do this in a fracture, and when there is a wound properly so called.
And then examine the leg each day and study it carefully; and watch it for abscesses, inflammation, or other similar conditions; and if any of these conditions occur, then treat it as necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the talus and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the whole talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot do occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes rarely occur, and what happens to them is rather attrition (wear and tear) in the joints.
And if a fracture occurs to the bones of the foot and those bones are raised up among themselves, then the patient should place his foot on the ground prepared in the manner described before.
Then place one foot on that which is raised up from those bones, and then press down on them, and squeeze and manipulate them until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and tow over it, and place under the sole of the foot a small board to which two flat pieces are attached.
Then bind that firmly to the foot with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other necessary materials.
And when three or four days have passed for him, then unbind the bandage, for you will find the bones joined, whether they were broken or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if some say they are broken, then restore them and manipulate them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint on the broken finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be a little longer than the finger; and place under the foot that board which I have mentioned to you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in linen cloth, and bind the board at the lowest part of the foot, and let it rest on one of the ends of the board outside the instep so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you do not forget that you consider all the things which I have said to you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is suitable.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the hip of the woman is broken, then make her lie down in a lying-down position, and gently incline her to one side a little, and let her be supported from behind.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it should be made in her hip like a sphere; and then the woman should be held up, and her hip raised little by little, because everything is put back until the broken part of the hip is like a sphere and the broken bone returns to place.
And then apply powders to her hip, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and then she urinates, and reposition and return it according to the manner in which you initially placed it, and then return to the first powder application.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it will be restored.
And if you wish, take an ox bladder and attach a cane tube to its opening; and insert the whole bladder into her hip, and then inflate it through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, so that the fracture is set.
And then it should be filled with cotton and remain so for some days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the pubic bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and healing that which we have recommended for the hip bone.
And what is right is not hidden in these proximate fractures which rarely occur, especially for one who studies and uses and understands this my book well; for that fracture indicates to you in the greatest detail the correct way of restoration and setting it, and therefore understand well.
But in the case of a broken penis, take a hen's gizzard and place the penis in it; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and then let it remain for three days or so, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractured bones when they are accompanied by a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar, that you take care and bleed him immediately if the conditions for phlebotomy indicate it as we have said above.
And if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you attend to stopping it, so that you sprinkle crushed egg whites on it, if no other cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this, lest an abscess form.
But if a hot abscess forms, then you should leave the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, uncovered, then it is necessary that you carefully reduce it and align it with your hands easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the setting of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands with this instrument; and this instrument, which is made of iron,  has a length of seven or eight fingers and a width according to the size of the wound; and for this it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these, according to the quantity needed for this in the treatment of every type of fracture.
And let it be round, and of little thickness, so that it is not bent by the compression upon it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp end which has a margin at its ends; and let the upper part of it be of increasing thickness, and from the middle of it to the parts.  (This is a difficult sentence due to ambiguous phrasing.  The meaning hinges on "partidas" which could refer to various things in this context—likely parts of the body being manipulated).
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Therefore it is necessary that its sharp end be raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And push that in at once until the bone returns, and is equalized with some equality/balance. (The meaning of "engualat am alcuna equacio" is somewhat uncertain;  it suggests a restoration of proper alignment or positioning.)
And then strive to equalize the ends of the fracture between them, and if the broken end of the bone is thin, and does not accept the instrument properly, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have recounted, then incise it with what is suitable from the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whichever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds vehement and hurting pain, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do what you can to confer great benefit upon the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is completed, then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine, and especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound cerate or anything else containing oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you incise the opening with scissors and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and caused death or a corrosive or suppurating illness, that is, madness, upon their patients. And if your constriction is light, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have recounted, and find pain in the place, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a bandage of hard cloths instead of stitches and bind it tightly with those.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to suppurate, then remove the cloth which you had placed upon it with wine, and then apply poultices, ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to unbind the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed, and it is necessary that the limb be positioned in such a way that the pus flows down from it to the lower parts easily.
If many days pass and the wound is not healed, nor is the pus discharged from it, then know that here there are hidden impediments, for which you must probe the wound with a probe. And of those impediments, those which are separated, remove and extract them; those which are not separated and which constrict the limb and cause pain, you are compelled to incise them, as far as is possible for you.
And if a swelling or ulcer occurs in the wound—that is, stye or other kind of corruption and putrefaction—then it is necessary that you treat each of these with what is appropriate, the treatment of which is described in the preceding chapter.
And here is what my discourse should address, and you should keep in mind: when a large bone is fractured,  apply a dressing to the limb, such as for a fracture of the thigh bone or the humerus, and similar for other large bones, and do not attempt traction or extraction, because death often results; leave it until it suppurates, for it will easily fall away by itself after approximately twenty or thirty days; and then cure the wound if you see fit, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20: On the treatment of a swelling that occurs at the site of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of a healing fracture, especially near the joints, and distorts the shape of the limb; and it quickly prevents the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have a styptic effect, such as aloe, incense, myrrh, cerussa (white lead), and similar substances;  take some or all of these, and mix them with styptic wine, or with egg white, or with sour wine, and apply this to the swelling on a compress, and bind it tightly.
And leave the bandage undisturbed for many days; then unbind it and apply another, until the swelling is reduced; or apply a lead plate directly, because lead has the property of reducing anything swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already become hardened and constricted and necessitates its careful removal, then incise it in the most suitable place, and cut away the excess and prominent parts, or scrape it with a sharp instrument until it is reduced; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Chapter 21. Of the restoration of a fracture when, after it has been set, the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is set, and afterwards the limb remains thin and weak, this is caused by many things: one of which is the excessive dissolution of the ligaments and their binding not being done as it should be; or by excessive tightness of the bandages so that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb; or by too much superfluous embrocation; or by excessive movement not at the proper time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and make their heart cheerful so that their blood is increased; and administer baths; and provide joy and merriment for them.
And then apply a plaster to the limb, so that the plaster draws much nourishment to it, or continuous embrocation with warm water, until nourishment flows and the limb returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Chapter 22.  Of the treatment of broken bones when they are set crookedly and the proper operation is prevented.
When, after a limb has been set, there is a return of crookedness and swelling at the broken bone and joint, and because of this the shape of the limb is impaired, but the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not believe the person who says that the limb is newly broken.
But there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land; and this practice is highly blameworthy, leading to great fear, the sign of which is death.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed in warm water in which softening herbs are boiled, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar things; and a softening poultice should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be mixed with white clay or myrtle oil and applied as a poultice; or ripe figs should be crushed with pigeon droppings, and similar things from among those medicines called consolidating unguents.
And sometimes it is resolved with gentle, frequent rubbing with moistened hands, and movement of the limb in all its parts should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become severe and hardened, and it becomes necessary to treat it with iron, then the outermost layer should be scraped away and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which superfluously swells around the bone should be incised with fine scalpels, and care and diligence should be applied to this.
Chapter 23.
Concerning discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints from its place, wherefore movement is prevented, and the form of the limb is distorted, and it brings great pain and injury upon the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to strive immediately that it be reduced, and not delay that reduction, for if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and very quickly thereby the reduction of the dislocation is made difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, because many times it causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, then you should attend to the bloodletting of the patient, and then let it rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with that which will bring back the memory (or recall the function) to its place.
And I have now ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what precedes in fracture; from the uppermost part of the body down to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, it will not delay; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are removed from their places by slight movement, and are softened a little; or they are dislocated by complete and full dislocation, until they are softened at the joint, so that saliva flows from the patient and they cannot hold it in nor close their mouth, and their tongue is entangled in their speech.
But when their dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, then a reduction must be administered lightly, and not delayed at all.
And let the assistant hold the patient's head, and let the physician insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the jaw dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or let him insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands be outside with which he will pull; and he commands the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to move from each side; and the physician pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer a vaporization with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on it powders of cloth with cerot (a type of plaster), and let it be made of wax and rose oil; and then let it be bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be positioned between two pillows, so that it does not lean to the right or left; and do not force them to chew anything; before their meal let their food be finely chopped, until when the pains are reduced and the jaws are healed, then let them eat what they want.
And this should be administered easily, and do not work on opening the mouth during eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is healed and mended.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and persistent thirst occur, and very quickly the patient's belly is distended, and very quickly they vomit pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and many times one who this happens to dies within a few days (x days).
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inwards from its connection with the breastbone; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. Then constrict the area with your palm with firm compression, for it will return.
Then apply a plaster and powders to that area and bind it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is the elbow and is continuous with it, is not dislocated on any day; then it should be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as is appropriate to you.
Then apply a plaster, powders, and a bandage, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated in only three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated in the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and protrudes outwards to the lower parts below the upper part, and especially in those whose flesh is scant, it is easily dislocated; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it protrudes with difficulty.
And it quickly happens to some men through a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed by a hot abscess, and it is thought to be dislocated because of this.
Then you must examine this carefully before you treat him, and then proceed with his cure.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the nipple; so that you may distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, for you will find a manifest difference between them; and you will find in the head of the muscle a concavity below the nipple, towards the touching of the head of the muscle like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
If it is dislocated towards the feet or upwards, and you then find a clear swelling, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the assistant raises his hand to the upper parts; and then place your hands below his nipple, and raise the joint firmly to its place, and the assistant raises his hand, and extends it upwards; and then place it below because it returns easily.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and from the beginning of the dislocation many days have passed, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and that a softening and relaxing embrocation be administered, such as boiled roots of mallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be applied.
And then the patient should be supine on his back and a wool ball placed under his nipple, of a consistency between soft and firm; and then the physician places his heel on the ball, and raises the head of the muscle forcefully, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not moved downwards, and another assistant holds the patient's head so that it is not moved downwards, because then it immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner:  have a man present who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on that side, and place his shoulder under the patient's nipple and lift the nipple upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's hand from the lower part of his belly. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with him to weigh him down, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced easily in another way: a long, round-topped post is fixed in the ground, like a pestle, neither thick nor thin. Then it is placed under the patient; after placing soft cloths on top of the post, the patient stands along the length of the post, and then his hand is extended downwards on the opposite side; and his body is forcefully extended on the other side, because the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if reduction is difficult for all the reasons we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a stick two cubits long and four fingers wide, and its thickness should be relieved at the nipple's point in this way:
And then soft cloths should be tied over the rounded head so that the stick does not injure the patient.
Then it is placed under the patient's nipple, and the hand or arm is extended over the stick down to the lower parts, and the stick is tied to the shoulder, arm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a rung of a ladder according to its width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is suspended on the other side, for the joint enters immediately.
And when its entry is complete in whatever way possible for you, then you should place under the nipple a ball of wool of equal size; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered myrrh and egg white over the whole muscle, so that it is rolled over, and then tighten under the nipple with a suitable bandage; and the bandage should be rolled over the plaster, and his hand should be attached to his neck, and it should be left, and his hand should not be moved for seven days.
And the patient's diet should be light until the limb is strengthened, for this makes his healing easier.
Then the diet should be increased after seven or five days, and movement should be exercised; and if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity that affects it or another cause, then cautery with three holes should be administered in that place, as previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not fixed, and the plaster and the bandage are reapplied several times, and it is not fixed and sags and he cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that his nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are incised or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
For the dislocation that occurs between the foot and the ankle or the back parts, the reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And another bandage should be applied to it, and care for it until it is healed.
And if during healing heaviness occurs in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths many times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its former natural state.
Chapter 27.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, but mostly forward rather than backward.  And the dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is evident to sight or touch depending on how it is dislocated. And when you align the dislocated elbow with its socket, this is clearly evident to you, for the joint is hollowed out and cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its muscle with it.
And it is necessary that you act quickly and reduce the dislocation before an abscess forms, for if an abscess forms, reduction is difficult, and it is not easily completely healed; and especially when it is in the back part, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and more painful, and sometimes death results from it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's hand should be extended with two hands, and the arm expanded, and the physician's hands should be on and under the elbow, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front, then reduce it by bending the hand with your hand until you strike the elbow with the base of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous and very strong extension.
And it is so that two assistants extend the arm, and two assistants hold that extension so that it is not disturbed during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, once it is wrapped with a long doubled cloth over the patient's hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this aids in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with vigorous pressure until it returns to place.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that he be placed on a plaster which has styptic and drying properties with egg white, and be bound tightly with a descending bandage, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and left like that for some days.
And afterwards it should be unbound; and then if the joint is formed in its place, then you untie his bandage and leave it like that.
And if you see the joint not well-knit, then you repeat the plaster and the bandage, and leave it like that for some days until it is tightened; and then unbind it.
But if there is great difficulty in the reduction of the joint, and the patient's disposition is restless, then administer moist heat in a bath and gentle friction, and compression, until it is eased; or place on the joint  alliative agents, that is, something very oily; and then bind it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And then remove that, and put it in a bath; and when it is softened, then you rub the joint with a tempered friction, and then repeat the application of the alliative agent, that is, very oily ointment, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the aid of a bath, until it is calmed.
And if you wish, place on it a warm, moist cow's bladder,  bound tightly over it; and do this several times, because it is moist and restores it to its proper state.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated wrist, that is, of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated wrist is sometimes easily reduced, unlike other joints; however, speed in reduction is necessary at the time of dislocation, before the place becomes inflamed or a hot abscess occurs there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table and the assistant extends the hand, and the physician places his hand or palm on the prominence of the joint, and pulls it until it returns to its place.
