However, it must be considered whether the palm is already turned towards the belly of the hand; then the patient places the back of his hand on a table during extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is on the back of the hand, then let the position of the hand be inverted on the table, so that the physician's hand falls upon that prominence of the joint.
And thus, if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then constrict it with a poultice, allowing the inflammation to subside, and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will not be able to endure or succeed in its reduction after days have passed.
However, if the joint remains twisted, and no harm befalls the patient except that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything, then know that the nerves are pinched or crushed, and then there is no remedy except to constrict it with a cautery near the injury, and quickly apply some dressing.
Therefore, when the limb is reduced, then apply the poultice we have mentioned, and let it be left and constricted for five days; and then let it be loosened and left, and let the patient's hand be exercised.
But if its movement is impeded and some gravity occurs, then soothe it with warm water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes fingers are dislocated in every part.
And when a finger is dislocated towards the palm or its belly, then stretch the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumbs, until it returns to its place.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it away from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is straightened on that same day.
And when it is night, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is tight.
And do likewise if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the spinal vertebrae.
When a complete dislocation happens to one of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are displaced from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death awaits the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excretion is expelled involuntarily, and they cannot retain it; and many times some of their limbs are paralyzed, or two of their legs, or two of their arms, or one of those; or one vertebra is displaced from its place, for many times it is displaced and many times the displacement is to four parts.
Therefore, it is already displaced to the back parts and is called yellowness (jaundice, or possibly referring to a yellowish discoloration of the skin in this context).
But the cure for him is that then you consider if the yellowness (jaundice) comes from purulence (pus), for then in that case there is no cure, nor is there any healing at all.
But in that which comes from a fall or from percussion or similar to those, the Ancients already described the methods of cure with lengthy discourse, of which the most returns with profit; but I have abbreviated it from that of which the little excuses the excess, due to my proximity to my intended explanation, with which I have expounded and formed this from various instruments with which I will show this and explain that.
And of those, only that which occurs in the two (presumably referring to specific locations or types of jaundice) properly, with that which I have recounted, is cured.
And it is that the patient be extended on his face on a cushion near a wall, and a soft instrument be placed under him so that his feet are not hurt.
And then a straight stick should be placed, fixed in the earth at the end of the cushion near his head, and another stick near his feet at the other end of the cushion.
And the assistant should hold the stick, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another assistant should hold the other one in the same manner.
Then wrap a bandage around the patient's head and under the armpits firmly; and extend the end of the bandage to the stick which is near his head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around his hips, thighs, and legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other stick which is near his feet.
And then each assistant should extend the bandage to the part of the stick, and the two sticks and the bandages should not be removed from their places where they are fixed, but nevertheless, they are not fixed in the way we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with strength until it returns, or place a board on it, and then adjust his feet on the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three braccia, and make a hole in the wall which we have said is near the patient, in which the end of the board will fit. And then place half of the board on the spine, and the physician should place his feet on the other end, and press firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, use a lathe which is rotated by hand; and you should place in the ground below the patient's head, at the back of the neck, two sticks, the length of each being one braccio, and below the head at the back of the neck, two more sticks; and the distance between each stick should be one palm, and a hole should be made in each stick through which the lathe passes, and each should be firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move.
And let there be a round stick on which the lathe is mounted, and let it be rotated in the two holes of the two sticks.
And at its end is a hole into which the stick is fitted, whose length is one palm including what is rotated; and the other two sticks are similar to that one.
Then tighten the bandages that you have tied around the patient's feet to the stick which is below their head, and the bandages that you have tied around their thighs to the lathe which is below their feet.
And then beside each lathe let a minister stand who turns the lathe with his hand, and the physician manipulates the vertebra as we have said.
Then, once the vertebra is returned and the location is manipulated, you should apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a piece of linen cloth whose width is about three fingers, and whose length is sufficient to cover the affected area, and the part of the healthy vertebra.
Then it should be bound with the bandage we have mentioned, and nourish the patient with light food until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of healing, then medicines should be administered that soften and smooth, with the continued application of the linen cloth we have recommended; and a lead plate is applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs behind the vertebrae of the back, and is caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already swells, so that no attempt should be made to treat it with this medicine, for it will very quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint suffer only dislocation, and not what happens to other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its inward dislocation is that, when you compare the patient's affected leg to the healthy one, it is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without pain, and it is the place where the pain is a hot abscess, because the head of the femur has already arrived there.
And the sign of that to which dislocation occurs outwards is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And a sign of that to which dislocation occurs forwards is that he extends his leg completely, yet he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk he cannot do so forwards; and his urine is retained and his groin is inflamed, and he walks on his heel.
And a sign of that to which dislocation occurs backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the hip, and his leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is behind that place.
But the reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, that is, it has been there a long time, and you administer its reduction and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not completely cured; and then one should not make a prognosis in that case.
But in the case of one whose dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types of dislocation, then take care that you return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it quickly returns and does not require further treatment.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part, either with your hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant extend the hand of that one from above so that he places his hands below the patient's testicles; and then tighten with a linen bandage around the neck of the femur, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be from the inner part to the groin to the part of the clavicle, or from the back part to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all these be at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type what we shall say about its own cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inner parts, the patient should be placed supine on his sound side; and then place the ligatures on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the groin; and then extend the ligatures upwards from the groin to the highest part of the clavicle.
And then another strong attendant should take his arms, and grasp the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outwards with a strong extension, for then it will return to its place.
And this type of dislocation is of easier cure, with which this member is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is dislocated upwards and does not yield to treatment with this method of cure, then it is necessary to bind the patient's two feet together with a strong, light ligature, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each [ligature] be the quantity of four digits, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two digits.
And then let the patient be suspended by the head from a beam, and let him be indoors; and let his height from the ground be the quantity of two arms' lengths.
And then command a strong young man to pull down the head of the thigh, and let another young man be suspended with the patient, and the other young man, pulling the thigh with strength, will push, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the patient lie on his back as we have recounted in the case of a frozen limb; and let the ligature be tightened on the affected leg and on his feet.
And then let two beams be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head; and then let adjusted beams be placed in the middle of the abdomen, upon which wet cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the beam is between his thighs, so that during the extension of the lower parts it is not pulled.
And then each assistant extends to his part, and the physician with his hand performs the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narrative about the abdomen; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the patient's hip joint, and then grasps that with the other hand, and with that grasping pulls that extended to the lower parts towards the knee joint.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower parts, and be elevated above the ground; rather it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the elevation if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said of his lying on two hips; and it is on his face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, it doesn't matter much.
And it is necessary that compression be applied with a tablet to the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should be — and the sign of his reduction is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those equal and the patient straightens his leg and extends it without difficulty, then you know that the limb is returned as it should be — and then you adjust the two hips and apply a plaster and bind it so that the hip is not moved in any direction; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
Then untie the bandages and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is now healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of loosening, then you return and re-plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
Then untie it, and have him delay walking for a few days until he is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee is dislocated in three ways: outwards, inwards, and downwards (more towards the back); it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to bring his leg towards his thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration for all its types is that you make the patient lie down straight, so that he extends his leg as much as possible on that; then adhere your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; and then adhere his knee to your palms, and hold those between your fingers on his knee firmly; and another assistant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And a sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to the thigh easily and without difficulty.
And then plaster that and bind the leg to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if its reduction is hindered for you, then administer strong extension with the bandages which were previously described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the talus. (Talus refers to the ankle bone)
The talus is sometimes displaced by a slight movement, and sometimes dislocated completely, and its dislocation is made either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the talus swells, protruding towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its displacement is a gentle reduction, and it is that it be extended easily with the hands and be manipulated until it returns (to its place).
But in its treatment when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you make the patient lie down straight, and that a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind at his back in the middle of his body.
And then you take with your right hand the foot from the upper side, and with your left hand the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with the left hand twice, and then extend with the left; and push the bones of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice as we have recounted.
And then push the bones of the foot to the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
Then it returns in one or two times according to this manner, and if you see the foot set correctly, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, because it will return.
But if the reduction is forbidden to you with what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and tightly fasten a strong rope to the ground, so that it falls between their thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around them so that the patient is not harmed.
And then let his assistant hold his thighs, and then another assistant extend the foot with his hands or with a bandage tied around the ankle; and then let each assistant extend against the extension of his pair, and let the rope be extended between the patient's thighs, restraining them, so that the body is not pulled downwards against their extension.
And then the physician sets the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant holds the healthy thigh downwards, because the dislocation easily returns.
And so when the dislocation is reduced and the success of its reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And it is necessary that you fear or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not too tight and strongly harms them.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
And then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and corrupted again, and then it will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess happens to him, then it is necessary that you administer to its healing that which precedes our narrative, in another place of treatment and embrocation, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocations of the toes of the feet.
It is necessary that the one who is dislocated be healed with little extension without work; and this which is not difficult, is rather light (easy).
But if the dislocation is in one of the bones of the toe, then it is necessary that you make the patient place his foot on the ground or on a table, and be standing as if he were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from the joints, and then press that with your foot with force until it returns, and you see that it is healed and no prominence appears in the place.
And then place under the sole of his foot a board that covers the whole foot, to which are attached two caps (possibly meaning splints or supports), and then bind it with a decent and firm bandage for three days; and then unbind it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its reduction is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are made with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both together.
When any of those causes occur, and you exert yourself in its cure and in his restoration, many times death occurs.
And therefore it is not fitting that one should presume upon the cure of him, except he who is wise in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash or presumptuous; and that he use in the beginning medicines that only reduce hot abscesses, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except in that case in which you hope for salvation from destruction, with alleviation of the sickness, and in which some hope appears. And then strive immediately to effect his reduction at the beginning of the cause, before a hot abscess develops.
And then, if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen which reduces hot abscesses and treat the wound with what is suitable for it of drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and occurs in the jagged separated bones, then you force their alignment, and do in this what we have said in the simple illnesses of what precedes in those places.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, that entry into the paths of fear as my preaching has preceded you; for this is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more salutary to your true body in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
